THE RED PAGE .
Serenades.
Beating Heart! we come again
Where my Love reposes;
This is Mabel’s window-pane,
These are Mabel's roses.
Is she nested ? Does she kneel
In the twilight stilly,
Lily-clad from throat to heel,
She, my Virgin Lily ?
Soon the wan, the wistful stars,
Fading, will forsake her ;
Elves of light, on beamy bars,
Whisper then, and wake her I
Let this friendly pebble plead
At the flowery grating ;
If she hear me, will she heed?
Mabel, J am wailing.
Mabel will be decked anon,
Zoned in bride’s apparel ;
Happy zone 1 . . . Oh, hark to yon
Passion-shaken carol!
Sing thy song, thou tranced thrush 1
Pipe thy best, thy clearest 1
Hush, her lattice moves, O, hush—
Dearest Mabel!—dearest!
Frederick Locker-Lampson.
+ -
I arise from dreams of thee
In the first sweet sleep of night,
When the winds are breathing low,
And the stars are shining bright;
I arise from dreams of thee,
And a spirit in my feet
Hath led me —who knows how?—
To thy chamber window, sweet 1
The wandering airs, they faint
On the dark, the silent stream ;
The champak odors fail
Like sweet thoughts in a dream}
The nightingale’s complaint.
It dies upon her heart,
As I must die on thine,
O beloved as thou art!
O, lift me from the grass I
I die! I faint! I fail!
Let thy love in kisses rain
On my lips and eyelids pale.
My cheek is cold and white, alas!
My heart beats loud and fast;
Oh, press it close to thine again,
Where it will break at last 1
Percy Bysshe Shelley.
- , + —:—— ■
Look out upon the stars, nr love,
And shame them with thine eyes,
On which, than on the stars above,
There hang more destinies.
Night’s beauty is the harmony
Of blending shades and light;
Then, lady, up 1 look out, and be
A sister to the night.
Sleep not! thine image wakes for aye
Within my watching breast;
Sleep not! from her soft sleep should fly
Who robs all hearts of rest
Nay, lady 1 from thy slumbers break,
And make this darkness gay
With looks whose brightness well might make
Of darker nights a day.
Edward Pinckney.

Hangs charm'd, and pauseth in its flight;
Is its own sweetness sleeps the flow r er,
And doves lie hushed in deep delight 1
Awake 1 awake!
Look forth, my love, for Love’s sw't-et sake 1
Awake 1 Soft dews will soon arise.
From daisied mead and thorny brake ;
Then, sweet, uncloud those eastern eyes.
And like the tender morning break 1
Awake 1 awake 1
Dawn forth, tny love, for Love's sweet sake 1
Awake 1 Within the musk-rose bower
I watch, pale flower of love, for thee ;
Ah, come, and show the starry hour
What wealth of love t.hou hid’st from me J
Awake 1 awake 1
Show all thy love, for Love’s sweet sake I
Awake I Ne’er heed, though listening Night
Steal music from thy silver voice ;
Uncloud thy beauty, rare and bright,
And bid the wot Id and me rejoice!
Awake 1 awake 1
She comes,—at last, for Love’s sweet sake 1
Barry Cornwall.
Notes and Queries.
[lt i» proposed to take advantage of Tus Bulletin’s
unique Australasian circulation to collect, information
from all manner of readers and observers on all manner
of curious and interesting subjects. Preference will be
given to notes and queries which are especially Austra-
lasian, or kin with Australasian, in their origin and
reference —particularly to those dealing with aboriginal
life and language, natural history, whifce-Australasiao
language, customs, exploits, and typical developments,
characteristic olu-time anecdotes, and so on. As space is
limited, brevity of statement is essential. Writers must
observe the ordinary correspondence conditions. The
help of The Bulletin readers everywhere is asked in
recording some of the many valuable facts, impressions,
or experiences which will otherwise be lost to our
country. Any relevant information will be welcome,
even if not precisely' answering the queries. Contribu-
tions must be endorsed ‘ Notes and Queries,’ and the
number of the question and date of issue must be
added. All references to books should state title,
author, publisher, date of publication, place where pub-
lished, and pages quoted from ; and all records of personal
experience should give date, place (province, district,
miles from nearest post-town), and other helpful details.
The first batch of queries is culled from some which have
recently reached Tiir Bulletin.]
NOTE.—I. Red Hair.
The red-headed person seems to have pecu-
liarly intense vitality, together with a low
power of resistance to strain, involving a
high death-rate as if the unusual exaltation
brought a corresponding depression during which
disease-germs readily found lodgment. A red-
headed person marks the extreme of the light-
haired classes (passing the albino as comparatively
exceptional), so the qualities with which I credit
him agree with the observations collated by
Havelock Ellis (‘ Man and Woman,’ p. 225 et sq.)
tending to show that there is a progressive diminu-
tion of fair people in Europe, and that women are
darker-haired than men —their pigmentation being
probably connected with their less intense but
tougher vitality. Ellis quotes Dr. Alice Ross
(‘Med. and Surg. Reporter,’ Oct., ’92): ‘ln red-
haired women and those brunettes who have red
lips, red cheeks, and are inclined to freckle rather
than to tan, lacerated perineum and cracked
nipples occur most frequently.’
Dr. F. N. Manning, N.S. YV. Inspector-General
of the Insane, tells me there are not many red-
haired persons in the asylums under his charge.
He estimates the proportion of red-heads to the
general N.S.W. population at about 4 per cent. —
which is the projiortion found, according to Dr.
Beddoes, ‘the chief authority on this subject,’
among the lower classes in London (Ellis, ‘ The
Criminal,’ p. 76). But the proportion of red-
heads in N.S.W. insane asylums is only about
2 per cent., and there are more dark-haired than
light-haired among the inmates.
Dr. Manning noticed when a surgeon in the
marines that red-heads make good soldiers, which
substantiates the ‘ ginger for pluck’ idea. Pluck,
ex hy<iothesi, would naturally follow on a red-
head’s abounding vitality. But this very vitality,
it seems, makes Dr. Manning dislike red-headed
nurses. They have ‘ too much zeal,’ are too
boisterous, exercise a disturbing influence in a
sick-room.
Ellis (‘The Criminal,’p. 77) says that of 1220
insane persons in the New Brunswick asylum the
hair of 1050 was described as ‘dark,’ ‘dark brown,’
‘brown,’ while 170, or a little less than 1£ per
cent., had ‘light,’ ‘auburn,’or ‘red’ hair. And
he proceeds, summing up this and previous evi-
dence (which, however, is not full enough to be
conclusive) —
One person in seven among the insane has fair hair, one
in five among criminals; one person in fifty among the
insane has red hair, one in 129 among the criminals;
one in forty among the insane has auburn hair,
cne in 129 among the crimiuials. So that while
the proportion of fair - haired is distinctly smaller
among the insane, the proportion of red-haired and
auburn-haired is very decidedly larger than among the
criminals. So far as exact evidence on the color of the
hair goes, it points chi fly to a relative deficiency of red-
haired persons among criminals. This may, perhaps, be
accounted for. There seems to be a lessened power of
resistance to disease among persons of brilliant pigmenta-
tion. The extensive anthropological statistics of the
American War showed a very marked inferiority on the
part of fair persons. . . . Our evidence as to the propor-
tion of bright-haiied people in lunatic asylums seems to
point in this direction. These r-d-haired people, wilh
their ‘sanguine’ temperament of body, are peculiarly
susceptible to zymotic disease ; they take scarlet fever,
for instance, very easily, and suff. r fr«-m it severely*
Among the manifold risks of a criminal life the brightly
pigmented person, with his sensitive vascular system,
seems to be soon eliminated.
The high vitality of red-heads is naturally fol-
lowed by noticeable sexual activity, I have never
known a red-headed old bachelor or old maid.
Why is the red-head a mark for the opprobrium
of children? At school he is invariably known as
‘ Ginger,’ or ‘ Carrots,’ or (more reeonditely)
‘Coppertop.’ Is it merely that he is rare and
remarkable, or is there in him some characteristi-
cally red-headed element which wins hostility?—
SCHREIBZKUG.
[The red-headed male and girl have evidently left
large footprints on the sands of time and literature,
and further red-headed facts, observations, or **,*»
pressione will be welcome.]
QUERIES.
2. ‘The Never-Never,’ or ‘Never Never Coun-
try.’ To what part of Australia precisely is this
phrase applied? and why? Who originated it?
and when was it first printed ? —T.S.S.
3. In ‘Queenslander’ of 20/3/97 A. Meston
writes—‘No kind of spear ever hisses or whistles,
or makes any noise whatever, except in the writ-
ing of people profoundly ignorant of the whole
subject. The fast-driven woomera spear passes
in perfect silence through the atmosphere, and
would pass unheard within an inch of the most
sensitive ear. Arrows are equally silent. There-
fore the “ hiss ” and “ whistle ” of spears and
arrows exist only in the imagination of those who
have only seen them in a museum, people who are
also ignorant of ordinary laws governing the flight
of projectiles.’ Are these statements and their
implications invariably correct ?—D.R.
4. ‘ls the joey born on the teat?’—Back-
blocksman. [Many statements on this still-dis-
puted question have already been printed in The
Bulletin, but they are scattered over a con-
siderable time and space. It is desired now to
concentrate available information on the subject;
and quotations from the book-authorities, as well
as observations from eye-witnesses, are sought.]
5. Who named Australia ?—J. T. Warris.
6. Where, when, and by whom was the first
Australian theatre (so called) erected ? What
piece was p'ayed on the opening night, and what
was the cast?—Stockwhip.
T. B. Saunders issues the half-dozenth volume
of his translations from Schopenhauer— ‘ On
Human Nature’ (posted, Ss.). If not quite so
bright as one or two of those preceding, it is still
eminently readable. Schopenhauer was an acute,
cotnmon-sensy philosopher who did not argue to
pet theories, but took things as they came, and
made the best or the worst of them just as the
case appeared to demand. If he brings Ahriman
on top nine times out of ten, it is only because
Ahriman is on top nine times out of ten. And
Schopenhauer was a very human animal—ex-
tenuating nothing, and setting down a great
deal in malice. The present volume, besides the
considerations classed under the tide, includes
reflections on ‘ Government,’ on ‘ Free Will and
Fatalism,’ on ‘Character,’and on ‘Moral Instinct’
with a lot of little reflections on various subjects.
* « * *
Authors’ histories of ‘My First Book,’ re-
printed from ‘The Idler,’ make really a very
entertaining vohnno in the new illustrated
edition (posted, 4s. 3d.) Kipling and Stevenson
are represented, Bret Harte and Zangwill,
Robert Buchanan, Hall Caine, and Conan Doyle
—and a dozen others, who all find something
interesting to say. The illustrations are particu-
larly good. Stevenson recommends everybody to
write with a map and an almanack—having him-
self come to grief with his moon in ‘ Prince Otto.’
* With a map before him, a man will scarce allow
the sun to set in the east, as it does in
“The Antiquary.” With the almanack at hand,
he will scarce allow two horsemen, journeying on
the most urgent affair, to employ six days, from
three of the Monday morning till late in the
Saturday right, upori a journey of, say, ninety or
a hundred miles, and before the week is out, and
still on the same nags, to cover fifty miles in a
day, as may be read at length in the inimitable
novel of “Rob Rov.” ’
• * * *
Nansen’s ‘Farthest North’ (Macmillan & Co.)
looks formidable in its two volumes and 800
pages, but it is not really one of those snares in
which publishers trap unwary readers with the bait
of a boom. Nansen is a fine, manly fellow who wins
your heart at once, and even in the multiplicity
of petty details which crowd his volumes there is
warm personal charm. And his business as ex-
plorer lie seetns to know better than any who have
preceded him. His success is rather a pill for the
expert English prophets of failure, but they have
gulped it admirably. Posted from BULLETIN
office, paper 6s. 3d. ; cloth Bs. fid.
* * * 1
The first issue of ‘The Literary Year-Book
promises more than it perforins. The criticisms
might be made of permanent value, and the
Burvey of publications should be much wider and
more thorough. Still, even what is given was
badly wanted ; and the Year-Book evidently has
a future. The. portraits and notices of authors
lighten it agreeably. There is a specially curious
picture of W. B, Yeats, a gaunt, glassed, hollow-
cheeked, forehead-wrinkled, night-haired, open-
mouthe I poet who looks as if he was posing as the
embodiment of the Celtic spirit.
Small Change.
Justin M'Carthy is bringing ‘ A History of uur Own
Times ’ almost up to date-to publish in a month or two.
George Grey seems to have won the good opinion of
honest people wherever he went. Olive Schreiner dedi-
cates to him ‘Trooper Peter HaDet,’ as to ‘ a Great Good
Man who, during his rule in South Africa, bound to him-
self the Dutchmen, Englishmen, and natives he governed
by an incorruptible justice and a broad humanity, and
who is remembered among us to-day as representing the
noblest attributes of an Imperial rule.’
Australian Dr. George Morrison’s pluck and pen have
won him the post of Loud. Times correspondent in China.
Paperknife: ‘Aldrich’s true poem, “ My Goddess," sug-
gesting De Musset’s “Muse,” has the base of Emerson on
compensation, showing that every gain involves its loss,
and otherwise. So Goethe writes “ Take what you want,
and pay the price,” and I lately found the Aldrich idea
in a fine anonymous poem called “The Tormentor.”
Octave Feuillet was almost worried to death over his
rivalry of himself. Suppose Dickens had continued
poor 1 ’
Re. : 'Re Kipling's “speokshioner,” the equivalent
German nautical expression is “speckschneider," literally,
“fat-cutter.” Talking of Kipling, what would happen
to a Bulletin poet who did this ?
“ His tire was leaky of tongue and pen,
His dam was a clucking Khussuck hen.
And the colt bred close to the vice of each,” etc.'
[Kipling is by no means flawless, though straws of cavil
are swept away in his vigorous flood. A writer in To-
Dav instances ‘ Follow Me 'Ome,' in the last book of
verses. ‘ Lines three and four read
*• But I strook ’im cruel ’ard, an’ I wish I 'adn't now,
Which is just what a man oaa't do.”
“Can’t do” what? Can’t “wish ’e’adn’t”? Again,
lines seven and eight read :
“ But I’d give my pay and stripe for to get the beggar
back.
Which it's just too late to do.”
Too late to “give ’is stripe and pay”? How had that
ever been possible, and, if it had never been possible, how
cou'd it be “ too late ” ? If the lines “ just what a man
can’t do," and “just too late to do” were transposed,
the meaning would seem to me much clearer; and I had
expected to find it so when the poem appeared in a book,
fancying that possibly the reading given was a printer's
error when it first appeared a couple of years ago. Ap-
parently, however, the author’s intention was to write it
so. Possibly he is intentionally suggesting a certain in-
consequence of thought, due to the turmoil in the
mourner’s mind.’}
ILL.: ‘Are a majority of magazine and newspaper
illustrators left-handed? If not, why do they so often
put the lady rider on the wrong side of her escort, her
legs on the wrong side of the horse, the driver on the
wrong side of the cart, and the whole business, wherever
possible, on the wrong side of the street? A recent Bul-
letin was left-handed all through—with the exception of
‘ Hop,’ who is generally right-handed, if he does occasion-
ally give a swagman a stick. There was a boy holding up
left hand in school, a man pointing over left shoulder at
left-handed poster, an Irishman digging potatoes with
left hand and foot on the spade, and so on. Even in as
illustrated advertisement of the pantomime nine-tenths
of the ballet held their wands and lances in the left
hand. Why?’
A.M.: ‘lf the indeterminate-gender pronoun difficulty
cannot be overcome by simply presupposing the mascu-
line, and the se-sim-sU pronoun is really required, it—or
its equivalent—will sooner or later come to stay. Utility
is, in the long run, invariably too strong for sentimental
or aesthetic objections. Even Swift's ridicule couldn’t
kill “ ambassador ” and “ amusement,” and for those who
declaim against any neonism as impossible there should
be a lesson in Addison’s hesitating supposition that the
“ mob ’’-abbreviation of “ mobile ” might not impossibly
some day take its place “ even in legitimate language." ‘
What an appetite some Americans have for tedious
little details about Washington, Lincoln, and Co. ! There
are hundreds of lives of G.W. ; the little known of him
has been said in every possible shape; yet the most
modern magazines never tire of saying it all over again.
‘M‘Cliire’s’ is now printing the most insignificant re-
miniscences regarding Gru.t—a second-rate man in point
of mental capacity, hut brought to the front by his physi-
cal grit, endurance, obstinacy. There must be a public
for such matter, or it would not get printed. Probably
the public exists chiefly among the brooding agricultural
classes, cherishing heroic kinship.

THE SHARK.
They tell us, when a vessel is at sea,
That ever in its wake a single shark,
Patient and tireless, follows silently
Watching for prey. So, out of each man's past
Some sin forgotten follows his life's bark,
And if he slip, devours him, at the last.
‘ John-a-Dreamt.

and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-

succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my ease every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I nm now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, H. WALKER,
Balmain, Sydney.’’

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
THE CARRION CROW.
Old Sandy M‘Million is mighty of paunch,
His talk is of shoulder and sirloin and haunch,
His sheep in their myriads are dotting the plain,
And to number his herds were a labor in vain ;
But, spite of his airs and his bounce and his brag
As he drives in his buggy past you and your swag,
He’s only a varlet and vassal—for, lo !
True King of the West is the Carrion Crow.
The stoutest of heart and the staunchest of limb
And the daintiest of flesh are a banquet for him ;
There’ll be bleating of lambkins and picking of
eyes
When he drops with a “ Caw ! ” down the creek
on his prize,
And the cattle that fail in the swamp-land shall
feei
Fierce clutching of claws and a beak as of steel,
And the Downs as they droop where the desert
winds blow
Must hail him as Master —the Carrion Crow !
He hunts the grey hawk if he wanders anigh ;
He’s always at hand when the wanderers die ;
A-perch on a fence or a rock or a limb,
He’ll laugh, as you droop, with a humor so grim ;
And never a swagman a mourner shall lack
So long as our bird can appear in his black,
With his dirge for the dying so gentle—“Ho!
Ho !
Here’s luck to the House of the Carrion Crow! ”
And I ween that the Lord of a weariless wing
Who preys on the choicest our pastures can bring,
Who drops by the dying to jibe and to jest,
Who feeds on the hearts of the bravest and best,
I ween he is regal—if regal there be ;
If “ manners make monarchs ” a monarch is he,
And as Monarch I hail him in song—that ye
know
How the West hath a king in the Carrion Crow.
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG.
THE OUTCASTS.
It’s a worn and weary legion that is battling in
the West %
For the truth of some old motto woven round some
family crest,
Skirmishing in scattered order, charging forward
side by side
Underneath the riven banner of the shreds of
family pride;
Our hands are to the axes and our shoulders to the
wheel,
And the daylight never whispers what the silent
nights reveal,
And we drive our dusty treadmill, and we play our
mocking parts,
And there’s no one knows the sorrow that is ach-
ing at our hearts,
If across the sand and sun-glare and the burning
blaze of blue
There’s a home beyond the ocean, and a hope that
holds us true
To the lustre of fine linen and the pride of armor
plates,
And the honor of old elm-trees and the pomp of
lioned gates.
So the lost life lies behind us for the curbless years
of youth
That laughed at shadowed sorrow and made mock
of whispered ruth,
And we lost the sweets of living when our life
was scarce begun,
And we gathered Pleasure’s rosebuds ere they
opened to the sun,
In the Wine or Play that drove us, or the Love that
learned too well
The word that locks us Heaven and lays bare the
path to Hell.
We the stars that guide us and the
jewelled hands that lead,
The whispers in the gloaming and the wildflowers
in the mead,
And down Life’s rocky pathway, while our hearts
are near to break,
We drink the Cup of Bitterness we chose for some-
one’s sake.
Outside dim moonlit gardens we have watched
the light forms tread,
And learned that Love was over and Hope for us
lay dead,
And the great world thunders past us—we hear
the hoofs go by—
While Poverty forbids us live and Pride forbids
us die !
And lighter loves have mocked us, and Passion’s
sheeted ghost
Has glimmered through the mulgas with the pulse
of Passion lost,
And browner hands have clasped us who have laid
those hands aside
For the memory of old meetings and the old un-
bidden pride,
And if there be amongst us some few that pride
hath flown,
God pardon us the broken hearts we’ve laid beside
our own!
Yet through the golden ranges we have tasted
now and then
The glory of our freedom and the strength that
makes us men;
In the scent of eucalyptus that we gather as we
pass,
In the glamor of the Bushland when the dawn-dew
dips the grass;
And a moment we are tempted o’er the lifting seas
between
To fling a gauntlet backward to the men we might
have been.
But through the sunset splendor creep the shadow
and the mist,
The white arms madly fondled and the red lips
madly kissed,
The bitterness of parting and the pain of one
“ good-bye ”
That rings across the starlight with a wail will
never die;
And Death comes very near us, and the hand of
Dark Despair
Is heavy on our heart-beat and her slow breath
chills the air,
And none but God has knowledge how men may
long to feel
The clinging arms of Poison and the kiss of Naked
Steel;
But the pride that wrecked our boyhood and the
pride we learned too late
Will hold us clean and guiltless till the opening of
the Gate.
GLEN ROWAN.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 17, 1897.
Some Doubtful Points.
The Federal Constitution has, at time of
writing, got through its first stage, and has
left the hands of the drafting committees.
It has next to be considered and argued over
and possibly amended by the Convention as
a whole, and then to go through the same
process at the hands of the various Parlia-
ments, and after that it has to be debated
and re-considered and put into final shape
at a second meeting of the Convention, and
then if it passes the ordeal of a popular vote,
and gets safely through some other stages, it
will become law. So far as it has gone there
are fewer conspicuous foolishnesses in it
than in most constitutions, whether written
or unwritten ; nevertheless, at least half-a-
dozen weak points stick out so conspicuously
that they are visible to almost any eye on a
very casual inspection.
* * * *
One glaringly weak point is the proposal
that the Federal military force cannot legally
enter any state for the purpose of putting
down riot or civil war, except at the request
of the provincial authorities. The object of
this proviso, of course, is to prevent the
Federal Government sending troops into a
state under pretence that civil commotion is
imminent, and keeping them there to over-
awe the State Legislature with some sinister
end in view. The precaution, however, is
not worth the paper it is written on ;
a Federal Government, unscrupulous enough
to break the law by using the military force
which is legally in any state to overawe the
civil authority, will be also quite unscrupu-
lous enough to break the law by sending a
military force illegally into the state. A
somewhat similar proviso in the United
States constitution makes it impossible for
the Federal authority to put down lyncliings
and other disorders, to interfere in the racial
fights and butcheries which arise periodically
in some of the Southern states, and made it
even impossible to legally interfere when an
army of rioters held Chicago and threatened
to reduce that stupendous city to ashes. Tf
the constitution starts considering and
making provision for possibilities of civil
war, it should take into account all the possi-
bilities. It is proposed that the State Minis-
try shall be the only power which can law-
fully call in Federal assistance, therefore it
is assumed—which is utterly absurd—that
the Ministry will necessarily be on the side
of order. Yet it might be that a Ministry
badly defeated at the polls would start the
civil war, and let an armed force loose on its
opponents to prevent them assuming office.
Then it naturally would not call in the
Federal forces, and, according to law, the
Commonwealth Government must stand by
and watch the commotion and murder which
ensued, without any attempt at interference.
* * * *
Again, if any Australian state went in for
bloodshed and revolution, it is quite possible
that the party of disorder might hold the
capital, keep the Ministry under surveillance,
and inform its members that any attempt to
call in Federal aid would mean summary
execution. In that case, also, the requisi-
tion for Federal troops would not be sent,
and the Commonwealth Government could
not legally interfere. Also, a State Ministry
might commit acts which were calculated to
bring about war with a foreign power, and
seeing that the Commonwealth Government
would have to do the fighting when the war
arrived, it should have powers of prompt
interference to enable it to avert the catas-
trophe —which would be distinctly its catas-
trophe. (In the U.S., for example, the state
provokes the quarrel, and the Federal Go-
vernment, which can’t interfere to preserve
order, does the grovel and apology and
pays the indemnity). These possibilities
certainly look very remote, but in pro-
posing any clause dealing with such a sub-
ject the Convention is dealing only with
remote possibilities, and if they are dealt
with at all it should be in a sane and reason-
able fashion. Either the clause should read
that the Federal Government shall have
power to send troops to any part of its terri-
tory entirely at its own discretion, or else it
should be omitted altogether, and the Federal
Government should be left to take whatever
powers it considers necessary. In any case
the suspicion which the proviso suggests
seems utterly foolish and uncalled for. If sub-
ordinate Governments, such as the municipal
councils of Bathurst and Geelong, can live
in safety without any special treaty to pre-
vent them being overawed and coerced by
troops sent from Sydney or Melbourne, then
there is a fair probability that subor-
dinate Governments at Sydney and Mel-
bourne can do the same. If the Australian
can’t trust himself in his Federal capacity
not to levy military execution on himself in
his provincial capacity without a special law
to protect him from himself, then he can’t
trust himself to keep the law that he has
made for his own protection.
* » * *
It is provided in the draft Constitution
that each province, regardless of size and
population, shall have six members in the
Senate. In the Lower House there is to be
one member for each 50,000 people, but no
state is to have less than five members.
Without this last proviso the two least popu-
lous provinces—Westralia and Tasmania—
would start with only three members each,
so in both Houses the smaller states receive
considerable concessions. It would be in-
teresting to know distinctly how these pro-
visions are to affect new states. If Queens-
land, for example, came into the Common-
wealth, and shortly afterwards the long-
expected division into three provinces came
off, would it thereupon send 18 members to
the Upper Federal House and 15 to the
Lower House, and thus have a much larger
vote than New South Wales ? Westralia,
from its mere unwieldiness, must some day
sub-divide into various provinces ; could it
divide itself by its own mutual consent into
six, and thereupon have 36 votes in the
Senate and 30 in the Lower House, and its
great empty wastes thus outvote both New
South Wales and Victoria ? The concession
to the small provinces which exist now may
be necessary to induce them to come into the
Commonwealth, but that is quite a different
thing from granting similar concessions to all
the small provinces which may exist in the
future. The sub-division of the three cum-
bersome and top-heavy states of Queensland,
South Australia, and Westralia is inevitable
by-and-bye, but some definite arrangement
seems necessary to prevent the sub-division
shifting the balance of power right out into
the desert.
* * * •
It is further suggested, in a vague sort of
fashion, that the Federal Senate shall have
a limited power over money bills—that it
shall have authority to amend them so as to
reduce taxation, but not so as to increase it.
Judging by many years experience of the
financial ways of our Parliaments, this idea
should be absolutely reversed ; the Senate
should have power to increase taxation, but
not to reduce it. There is not, and has not
been for many years, one Australasian
Government that dared to levy enough taxa-
tion to meet its requirements. They all
dabble in deficits, and charge current ex-
penses to loans, calling these everyday ex’s
‘ ‘ permanent and reproductive works, ” and
adopt strange shady expedients to avoid un-
pleasant responsibility. To create two
Houses with power to reduce taxation and
only one with power to increase it, in a
country which is run on these lines, is a
huge anomaly. And to give any House
power to hunt, for transient popularity by
reducing a tax, without imposing on it also
the responsibility of creating a new one to
fill the gap thus made in the Treasury, is a
one-sided way of doing things The Upper
House, by this device, can create deficits
while the Lower House, which is to have
the management of the finances, will be
responsible for them and have to bear the
obloquy of them ; and no scheme built on
such lines as these is likely to work without
tremendous friction.
* * * *
Probably, however, the most doubtful
point, and the largest mistake, and the
worst anomaly up to date, is the comprehen-
sive proviso that all powers not expressly
reserved to the Commonwealth Government
shall belong to the provincial authorities.
How much this means no one can tell
exactly until the Federal machine gets into
motion. Then it will probably be found
that the number of matters which no one
has remembered to make special provision
for is legion, and that the powers of the
Federal Government are so much more
limited than was generally supposed, that it
is an affair with very little authority of any
kind. Even the powers it does specifically
possess may prove useless in many cases for
want of control over some small detail or
insignificant-looking side issue needful for
the exercise of them. If the clause read in
the exactly opposite sense, and all powers
not specifically reserved to the Provincial
Governments belonged to the central autho-
rity, it would at least be much more work-
able. Then, if it was found that the Federal
Government had reserved to itself certain
powers which could be better exercised by
the local legislatures, a single enactment of
the Commonwealth Parliament could trans-
fer them ; if the same trouble arises under
the proposed order of things it will probably
be necessary to cajole six Legislatures, all of
which can never be found in the same mood
at the same time, to make the surrender.
Further, civilisation is always changing, and
new conditions are arising which have to be
dealt with, and new powers are required to
deal with them, and for the Central Govern-
ment to leave all the unknowable possibilities
of the future to a mere provincial authority
is doing a thing the meaning, or size, or
hazard of which no one can estimate.
Further, by always giving the benefit of the
doubt to the local Governments, it is assumed
that the prominent statesmen who find their
way into the Legislature of all Australia will
be necessarily less intelligent, and honest,
and patriotic than the small men who are left
behind in provincial politics ; and that the
parochial politician will necessarily take a
wider and clearer view than the national
one ; and that the country is more likely to
find about 800 upright men to form 12
Houses of Provincial Legislature than, say,
150 to form two Federal Houses. And all
these are very reckless assumptions indeed
The Policy of Shirk.
The Federal Convention discussion on the rail-
way gauge problem is, so far as it lias gone, a
fine example of the policy of hard, unflinching
shirk. There are three different gauges in this
country, and the change from 3ft. 6in. in Queens-
land to 4ft. in New South Wales, and
then to sft. 3in. in Victoria, and then to 3ft.
6in. again in South Australia, means much use-
less loading and unloading and re-loading of
passengers and cargo, much needless delay,
damage in handling goods, cost of handling
goods, many extra cars, trucks, and loco-
motives, and other varieties of waste and exas-
peration. In addition to all this, the break of
gauge would almost paralyse the military
department in case of invasion by making
impossible the rapid conveyance of troops and
material from one province to another, and it
would make it impracticable to shift rolling-
stock out of any particular province to prevent
it being seized and used or destroyed by an
enemy. The trouble is already very bad,
despite the fact that the New South Wales
and Queensland provincial systems only meet
at one point; likewise the New South Wales-
Victorian systems and those of Victoria and
South Australia have only one point of contact
apiece, while the system of the latter province
doesn’t, so far, meet that of Westralia at all,
nor is there any direct connection as yet between
South Australia and New South Wales or
Queensland. When the Victorian and New
South Wales lines meet in five or six or ten or
more places, and the Cobar and Bourke lines
connect with South Australia, and there are
a number of points of contact between New
South Wales and Queensland instead of only
one, the evil will be several dozen times worse
than it is, and the waste and circumlocution
several dozen times greater, and the exaspera-
tion will grow into an unknown quantity.
• * * *
All the provinces agree that there should be
only one gauge ; that there must, sooner or
later, be only one ; and that if the change isn’t
made at great expense now, it will have to be
made at much greater expense later on when
the systems have expanded. Also, it is unani-
mously agreed that the needless loss caused by
the present system is already very great when
the break of gauge makes trouble at three
places only, and that it will be immensely
greater when there are one or two or three
dozen separate breaks. And each province is
unanimously of opinion that the one way out of
the difficulty is for all the other provinces to
altet their lines to its gauge at their own ex-
pense. The unanimity on this point is simpiy
wonderful. 1 "
* * * *
The shortest and most obvious settlement of
the problem is to consolidate the railways and
the provincial debts under one management
Then there would be a central authority which
could decide on and establish a uniform gauge
and debit the cost of the alteration to Australia
in general, consequently the impossible asonv
of settling how much of the expense should
fairly be paid by each province would h F
averted. There would be no question of apnor
turning the cost among the provincial railway
departments or the provincial treasuries at all-
it would simply be charged to the Federation
at large. So far, however, the financial com-
mittee of the Convention has shirked the ques .
tion of Federal railway control because there
are “serious difficulties in the way,” and pro-
poses, as the next best resource, that a confer-
ence should be held to attempt some amicable
arrangement. Also, it is proposed by several
members that the matter should be hung up for
a while because the establishment of a uniform
gauge now would cost an appalling sum—
variously estimated at from £5,000,000 to
.£10,000,000, which probably the quaintest
piece of logic in this connection up to date.
Assuming the estimate to be correct, it natur-
ally follows that, if the matter is hung up long
enough, so that there will be more railway in
existence and more diverse gauge to alter,' the
cost of the work may easily be £15,000,000 to
£30,000,000 instead of the figure now quoted.
Also, it seems reasonably obvious that, if a
thing is very bad now' and is getting worse
every day, hanging it up will only make the
difficulty larger instead of smaller. But, so far
as financial problems are concerned, the Federal
committee is consistent to its policy of shirk
and postponement, and to very little else.
• * • *
As an amendment on the scheme for merely
talking over the matter, and the other scheme for
hanging it up, Premier Reid has suggested still
anothermethodof shirking the difficulty. Reid’s
idea is that the great main line which now runs
from Brisbane to Adelaide, and which will by-
and-bye go on to Perth, should be put on a
uniform gauge all through, and that branch
lines should remain as they are, and be allow’ed
to grow according to the present uncertain
method. If this idea is adopted some beauti-
fully interesting results will follow. The New
South Wales line to Cooma is a mere branch at
present; so is the Victorian line to Bairnsdale.
When they meet, however, they will be as dis-
tinctly a main line from Sydney to Melbourne
and a part of the great Brisbane-Adelaide sys-
tem as the track via Albury, and that new line
will have then to be put on the uniform gauge.
The only result of waiting until these two
branches become a main line will be that there
will then be 200 miles more track to re-lay,
and a corresponding increase of expense. When
the small gap from Deniliquin to Jerilderie is
covered, two more branch lines will become
a main railroad connecting Queensland and
South Australia and the intermediate provinces.
When the very small gap between Cobram and
Jerilderie is filled in, the Victorian branch via
Shepparton will likewise be a main interpro-
vincial line. The New South Wales line to
Cobar is only a branch now ; when it reaches
Broken Hill it w T ill probably be the main line
from Brisbane and Sydney to Adelaide and
Perth and the western provinces generally.
Also the idea of treating the South Australian
transcontinental line as a branch is absurd ; it
may be one at present, but when it is finished,
which must happen sooner or later, it will be
the main line, par excellence, of Australia. The
proposal to establish a uniform gauge for main
lines only looks good enough at the first glance;
the trouble is that there are about 3000 miles of
branch lines which will become main lines by-
and-bye, and will have to be treated as such.
And Premier Reid’s proposal simply means
postponing the necessary alteration of these
till they have expanded to 8000 or 10,000, and
the problem has become proportionately larger.
Also the Reid idea isn’t new ; in fact, it is the
same old shirk that has guided the policy of
this country from the beginning. The Victorian
railway-builders going north, and the New
South Wales engineers building towards the
south and the north, and the Queensland men
building southward, all decided for many years
that their varied gauges didn’t matter because
their isolated branches didn’t meet, and might
not meet for many years, and they all agreed
that when they did meet it would be time
enough to consider the difficulty. Now that
they do meet Premier Reid proposes spending
£2,000,000 or £3,000,000 in rectifying these
two particular blunders represented by the
breaks on the Victorian and Queensland borders.
Also, learning nothing by experience, he pro-
poses to go right on with eight or ten more
blunders of exactly the same character, with
exactly the same result ahead in each case.
* » * *
How very small the prospect is of settling
the difficulty by conference or treaty among the
various independent managements of the main-
land provinces can be measured by anyone who
considers the questions at issue. (Tasmania,
with an entirely isolated system, and Queens-
land, as a province standing, at present, out-
side the Federal movement, need not be con-
sidered just at present). Assuming that the
4ft. gauge of New South Wales—gener-
ally regarded as the most serviceable —is
adopted as the standard, Victoria must reduce
its lines from the present sft. 3in. measure-
ment ; South Australia will have to reduce the
width of some of its lines from sft. 3in. while
most of its railway system must be broadened
from the present 3ft. 6in. gauge; while the whole
Westralian system must be broadened from
3ft. 6in. to the regulation 4ft. BJin. Reducing
the width of railway lines costs very little; in-
creasing the gauge costs a great deal, as it in-
volves widening bridges, tunnels, viaducts,
embankments and cuttings, shifting back
fences, platforms and buildings, &c. The ex-
pense will have to be divided among the four
provinces equitably, as each is acting for the
common benefit, and the query arises—what is
an equitable division ? If the cost is allotted
according to population, New South Wales and
Victoria will pay about twice as much, in pro-
portion to the amount of railway they possess,
as the two other provinces. If the bill is
allotted according to the amount of line in
each province—if each province is called upon
to pay so much per mile of its own railway
system—South Australia will pay nearly three
times as much per inhabitant as New South
Wales and twice as much per inhabitant as
Victoria, and will probably be quite unable to
meet the bill. The liability can’t be allotted


according to area, for then South Australia and
Westralia, the two poorest and least populous
provinces, will pay practically all of it. And
each province can’t be called upon to pay its
own separate invoice, for then the one whose
gauge is adopted will contribute nothing at all.
• • * •
Further, if the 4ft. B|in. gauge is adopted,
Victoria makes a great saving on lines it may
construct in the future ; South Australia and
Westralia, on the other hand, make their future
lines much more costly, and will probably want
some consideration for the indefinite liability to
which they thus commit themselves. In the
provinces where the gauge is altered all the
rolling stock must also be altered or replaced.
This will likewise have to be allowed for, and
the amount of the allowance will depend to
some extent on the value—to be estimated as
impartially as possible—and age and dilapida-
tion of the stock sacrificed. The provinces
where a new gauge has to be introduced gain
some indirect benefit through the expenditure
of much money, partly contributed by their
neighbors, within their borders, and the conse-
quent furnishing of much employment to their
population, and a demand may possibly be
made that this should also be taken into
account. If Premier Reid’s proposal, to make
only the present interprovincial main line uni-
form, should be accepted, and the 4ft. B|in.
gauge is the one adopted, then in addition to
all the other troubles Victoria will doubtless
want some compensation for the extra expense
of working two gauges instead of one. And
when the bill, whatever it may be, is made up,
the insuperable problem is : How much of it
should be paid by a province with 2530 miles of
line and 1,277,000 people; and what is a fair
share for a province with 3103 miles of railway
and only 1,180,000 people ; and where does a
province with 360,000 people and 1722 miles of
line come in, and so on ?
* * • *
If the railways were consolidated into one
system under Federal management the whole
difficulty would disappear. It would then be
simply a case of deciding on a uniform Federal
gauge, making the necessary alterations, and
charging the cost to the system as a whole—
the impossible problems about how to divide
the bill fairly, and how to make sure of collect-
ing it when it is fairly divided, and about
which Treasurer will put up the money in the
first place and take his chance of never getting
the cheque from the Treasurer next door, would
all be eliminated. But the financial committee
of the Convention admits that it doesn’t feel
equal to the great problem of railway consoli-
dation ; and though it highly approves of a
uniform gauge, it doesn’t feel personally equal
to dealing with that either. In fact, it seems
not quite equal to anything save the great art
of Shirk.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Compromise.
The brightest thing that the Convention’s
financial committee has done so far, is the com-
promise it has effected on the surplus question.
It is, on the whole, probably as good a com-
promise as circumstances—a very bad set of
circumstances—would allow. For the first
year or two years, until the Federal tariff is
agreed upon, the present provincial tariffs are
to remain in force. The Federal Government
will collect the money, debit each province
with its share of the Federal expenditure ac-
cording to population, and return the balance
to the provincial Treasurers. When the
Federal tariff gets into operation the same
book-keeping system is to be followed for five
years ; each province is to be credited with the
revenue which it yields to the Central Govern-
ment, and charged with its share of the Federal
expenditure, and is to get back the balance,
but it is stipulated that the said balance
must be at least as large as it was during the
last year before the uniform tariff came in force.
After five years, when the provinces have got
their second wind, and the Federal and Pro-
vincial Governments have begun to understand
where they are, and the worst of the confusion
and shindy is over, the elaborate book-keeping
system is to be abandoned, and the surplus dis-
tributed according to the population of each
province. This arrangement, if it isn’t overset
later on, is at least a decent way of striking an
average among a lot of difficulties. The first and
best idea was that the provinces should handover
their customs and excise revenues, and that the
Federal Government should pay the interest on
the public debts, and then things would be
about even, and there would be no surplus to
worry about. This fell through, no one knows
exactly how. Then the Freetrade party at-
tempted a weird scheme whereby the provinces
gave up their customs and excise revenues
(about <£9,000,000) ; the Federal Government
paid its own expenses (about £2,000,000) out of
them, and then abolished the rest as a matter
of necessity because it wouldn’t be allowed
to hand the difference back, or pay the inter-
est or the principal of the public debt with it,
or build railways with it; and thus a beautiful
era of almost absolute Freetrade would come
about by compulsion. Meanwhile the pro-
vinces, having still the interest on their debts
to pay, and having surrendered the right to
levy Customs and excise and thus lost the re-
venue out of which the interest has hitherto been
paid, would mostly have to go broke for the
sake of Foreign-trade, the gap in the finances
being too large to be filled by direct taxation.
The compromise proposed means that the policy
of going broke in the name of Foreign-trade is
postponed for five years. During that period
it is estimated that the Federal Government
will have to raise enough revenue to pay
its own expenses and also to divide ,£6,000,000
a year among the provinces. It can raise
the required amount by a protective tariff,
or a revenue tariff, or partly by a tariff
and partly by direct taxation, or wholly by
direct taxation, as it pleases, so every party
and policy and fad gets an equal show. At the
end of the five years its obligation to subsidise
the States apparently ceases, and by that time
it will probably, if it is a reasonably sane
Government, have come to some definite deei-
sion about what to do next. Unless the Con-
vention upsets the whole arrangement the
great financial difficulty seems to be shunted
well forward into next century, and a lot of
people will be dead or disappeared or in gaol
before then, and a lot of new people will have
to tackle the problem.
The Sacredness of Property.
Michael O’Connor, a recently-deceased Vic-
torian who is alleged to have been eccentric in
his lifetime and a martyr to indigestion, fur-
nishes a convenient peg whereon to hang a wail
about the stupidity of Australian law. Michael
died leaving over .£5OO worth of property
behind him, all of which he carefully be-
queathed to two neighbors, leaving Mrs.
Bridget O’Connor penniless. No reason, ex-
cept misanthropy arising from aggravated in-
digestion, was shown for this action, but as it
was impossible to prove absolute insanity on
the part of the deceased, or undue influence on
the part of the legatees, the Supreme Court
decided that the will must hold good. As
Judge a’Beckett put the matter :
A man has a right to dispose of his property in what-
ever manner he pleases. Although he has a faithful wife
and dutiful children he has a right, if he pleases, to leave
his property to others.
The Bulletin urges that the above right
should be promptly thunder-blasted by Act of
Parliament, for various reasons. One is that in
most cases a man’s property is not his own by
moral right, whatever it may be legally.
Michael O’Connor, corporation laborer by
profession, couldn’t accumulate .£5OO at his
precarious occupation save by denying himself
and Bridget, his wife, many luxuries and en-
joyments, therefore, in a moral sense, it was as
much her money, accumulated by her self-
denial, as his. And this rule holds good
in probably the majority of cases. But,
even assuming that the family pile is
solely a husband’s own accumulation, and that
his wife is not only useless in a financial sense,
but is also a thoroughly objectionable character
who deserves to be disinherited, the law which
permits such a thing is questionable policy. If
the deceased husband doesn’t support her, then
the community in many cases must, and the
deceased has at least as good a right to support
his own objectionable Bridget as a perfect
stranger or a number of perfect strangers have.
The system by which, when a woman is too
bad to have a claim on her deceased husband,
she thereby establishes a claim on other people,
is a pleasant consideration—for the other people.
Moreover, the law is a self-contradictory ass.
It doesn’t allow a husband, under any circum-
stances, to leave his wife without support while
he is alive, however bad the lady may be —the
only way he can get out of the liability is
by divorce, whereupon she ceases to be
his wife; and it seems ludicrous to allow
a corpse, which doesn’t amount to much
at the best, privileges which are refused
to the living individual. The mere beastly
sacredness of property doesn’t account for the
law’s present attitude ; if property is so dis-
gustingly holy that the statutes can’t prevent a
man of means leaving his wife penniless after
his death, then they have no right to so far
dictate to him what he shall do with his
own worldly goods as to prevent him leav-
ing her penniless while he is alive. Either
it is absolutely wrong for the law to interfere
in the latter case, or it is absolutely wrong for
it not to interfere in the former. The only
moral injury that would be done to the de-
ceased by a reversal of the present law, would
be that the old man would be deprived of the
pleasure of thinking, in his last moments, how
surprised the old woman would be when she
had to take in Washing for a livelihood later on,
and there is no absolute reason why the law
should encourage this frame of mind in any
dying citizen. ' On the whole, The Bulletin
holds a brief for widow Bridget all the time.
Some Advice to Young Men.
“ Melbourneite ” to The Bulletin :
Mr. Justice Hood (Yic.) the other day publicly gave what
has been described as some “ advice to young men.” The
occasion was an application by the trustees of a fund of
£5OO to be allowed to apply £2OO of the amount to the
medical education of the beneficiary. The alleged advice
consisted in making some references to the folly of young
men without considerable means going in for the learned
professions, and, in refusing the application with the
luminous remark that if it were granted “ the applicant
would then have i 300 while he starved at a profession,
instead of becoming an honest tradesman.” The practi-
cal value of his Honor’s advice would have been greatly
increased had he indicated the particular trade which he
thought it advisable for that young man to follow. Doubt-
less the number of young fellows going in for law and
medicine is calculated to make one wonder how they
are all going to make a living; but the mistake the Judge
makes is in concentrating the judicial spectacles upon
one small aspect of a very large question. His Honor, no
doubt, observes that, mainly owing to the intrusion of
“ young men without considerable means,” law and medi-
cine are not the pleasant monopolies they used to be, and
therefore he insists that this particular youth shall turn
his hand to something else. But what his Honor does
not appear to have taken judicial cognisance of is the
fact that the honest tradesman runs some risk of starving
too, as well as the honest (or otherwise) lawyer or doctor,
and that on the whole it is, perhaps, less unpleasant to
be a starving medico than, say, a starving bricklayer or
blacksmith. His Honor’s attention might have been
drawn to the fact that in this young and progressive
country most other trades seem to be suffering from the
same painful superfluity of practitioners. Forinstance, the
possibilities of the small-grocery business seem to have been
pretty fully explored, and there does not appear to be any
great dearth of bakers, butchers, shoemakers, or milk-
men. Even in the undertaking business, although the
total turnover does not show any marked diminution,
competition has so cut into the profits that some honest
tradesmen have had to combine the legitimate trade with
the allied occupation of reading burial services. Also,
Horace Greeley’s advice, “Go West, young man,”
appears to have found sufficient followers for the present,
and the number of people who are “ settled on the land ”
—and can’t see any chance to get off it—appears to be as
great as the exigencies of the case require. And, after all,
there are a few prizes in the so-called learned professions
which don’t come in the way of the honest tradesman.
For example, it is hard at the moment to recall any
instance in which a tradesman, however honest, was able
(like his Honor) before the age of 40 to collar a £3OOO a
year billet with a huge pension attached. On the other
hand, it is easy to remember many instances of men who
probably did not have a clear £3OO to start with who can
earn more money in 12 months than any honest tradesman
would earn in 12 years. Perhaps, on further considera-
tion, his Honor will see his way to let this youth take his
chance and hustle with the rest, and will leave him and
his mother to settle the question of a profession between
them.
Yet another miscarriage of justice through a
jury fearing to leave the sentencing of an
accused to the mercies of the presiding Judge.
In a conspiracy case at At bury, N.S. W.,in which
a man and woman were arraigned, the jury-
men, having been locked up all night, announced
that they were unable to agree unless his Honor
could assure them that if a verdict of guilty
were brought in the female accused would be
dealt with under the First Offenders Act. His
Honor declined to entertain the proposition,
and the twelve then announced that they could
arrive at no verdict, and were discharged.
Juries, if competent to find a man guilty,
should be equally competent to say how guilty
they think him by fixing the punishment with-
in statutory limits. The Executive could
always revise the sentence of a jury just as it
now does that of a Judge. When is this much-
needed reform coming along ?

Premier Forrest, of Boomland, has organised
in the Convention a temporary league of the
three un-populous provinces—Westralia, S.A.,
and Tasmania—against Victoria and N.S.
Wales, and is t unning a hurried, kaleidoscopic
little shindy of his own re the powers of the
Federal Upper House in money matters. As a
large, robust cormorant, there are some pro-
minent points about Forrest. It has been
agreed that in the popular chamber each pro-
vince shall have one member for each 50,000
inhabitants, but not less than five members
in all—this latter being a special conces-
sion to the smaller states; while in the
Upper House each state shall have six
members regardless of population. There-
fore, N.S.W. and Victoria, with 2,480,000
people, will have 12 senators and 49 representa-
tives between them ; while Westralia, S.A. and
Tasmania, with about 640,000 inhabitants, will
have 18 senators and 17 representatives. This
is tolerably liberal, but in addition Forrest
demands that the Senate shall have exactly the
same power in money matters as the Lower
House, and as the small provinces will dominate
the Senate and the large ones the other Cham-
ber this means that the power shall be equally
divided between them—that the 640,000 people
who pay one-fifth of the taxes shall have
as much control over them as the 2,480,000
who pay the other four - fifths. The re-
sult, in all probability, wouldn’t pan out in
anything like the way Forrest anticipates, but
that doesn’t make his demand any the less
ridiculous. For the moment, the three less
populous provinces are acting together, and
Forrest assumes that they will keep on acting
together always, which is wholly improbable.
Tasmania, to all intents and purposes, is a
satellite of Victoria, and it has very little in
common with Westralia, except the desire for
more than its fair share of power, and, if the
Federal Parliament is formed as Forrest
desires, Tasmania, N.S.W., and Victoria will
probably run together in most matters, and
then Forrest will wish he had never got all
these extra votes for the little isle. Also, there
are many other possibilities to be considered.
In their own stupid, short-sighted fashion,
however, Forrest and his followers are fine
samples of the cormorant tribe, and the warn-
ing issued by some of the N.S.W. and Vic.
delegates, that they would rather throw Wes-
tralia out of the Commonwealth bodily than
give way any further, is fully justified.

“ Swamper” to The Bulletin :
Part of the theory of English law is that the Sovereign
can do no wrong, and, therefore, cannot be sued by a
subject. But the Government, which is the Sovereign,
often does do wrong, and, to get over the difficulty, the
subject presents a “ petition of right,” which is endorsed
by the Sovereign “ Let right be done in this matter ” in
such-and-such a court. Royalty having thus graciously
condescended, the matter becomes determinable in court.
In theory, everyone has the liberty to present a petition
of right and to know the formal decision of the Sovereign
in the matter. But in the absolute monarchy of Wes-
tralia King Forrest has gone one better than Queen Vic-
toria. When a petition of right presented by a Wes-
tralian citizen is returned to the Governor of that pro-
vince endorsed “ Let right be done in this matter
in the Supreme Court of Westralia” to be handed to
the petitioner, it is first enquired into by the
Forrest Ministry, and if thought fit the Governor
is constitutionally advised not to hand it to the peti-
tioner or allow it to he filed for decision in the
Supreme Court. The Governor is bound to accept this
advice, and the result is that the particular case never
gets to the Supreme Court. In the first case of this kind
a petition of right presented by the Westralian Land
Co. was endorsed, and the representative of the co. in
London informed of the endorsement, but the Westralian
Govt, would not give up the petition nevertheless. The
cause of action was that the Govt., to prevent the co. from
selecting under its land-grant concession the unsold lots in
the towns along the Great Southern railway, declared (ille-
gally, the co. contends) such lots Govt, reserves as “ resting-
places for travellers and stock.” The local representative
of the company, who is also an Opposition politician, has
been unavailingly endeavoring to induce the Govt, to
publish the endorsement, as a matter of right. A second
case is that in which a Westralian resident desires to sue
the Govt, for damages in a claim based on “ perversely
stupid and long-continued maladministration of the
Lands Department.” He was informed by the Governor
that his petition of right had been returned from Eng-
land endorsed with the usual formula, but the Govt,
blocked the “ official return ” of the petition to the
Supreme Court, and that tribunal cannot therefore try
the matter. The Govt, states that it is unable to
forward the petition as “ communications are still
pending between the Govt, and the Secretary of
State for the Colonies in regard to the question.”
The petitioner replies that no communication should
be requisite in respect of a petition of right.
Here is an argument, if any were needed, for Australian
legal procedure to be freed from the fooleries consequent
on the idea that it is infra dig. for the “ Crown,” which
embraces every wretched Govt, department, to be sued
unless the suitor first prays for a petition and the petition
is graciously granted. Govt, departments should be
amenable for their omissions and commissions in the same
way as any other public institution, without the necessity
of an appeal to England. It aggravates the abuse that after
the roundabout process of petition to the English Govt,
permission to sue should be suppressed by an autocratic
provincial Govt., against which there is no possibility
of appeal—in the case of the Westralian Govt, not even to
public opinion. Premier Forrrst hawked the question
to the last gathering of the Federal Council with the
object of soliciting the support of the other Australian
Govts, for his attitude towards petitions of right. The
fact has not been officially disclosed, neither has been the
result of Premier Forrest’s representations to his fellow
Premiers, which are said not to have been satisfactory
from his point of view.
Australian officialdom might bake a hint from
the Prince of Wales’s recent epistle to the
Londoners re commemorating his mother’s good
luck in reigning so long. Barring an allusion
to “ the sentiment of gratitude for the blessings
which the country has enjoyed during the last
sixty years,” Wales (or his private secretary)
doesn’t utter a line of nonsense, and even that
can’t be called “ loyal ” nonsense. The words
“throne,” “gracious,” “Her Majesty” and
“blessing of Providence” are conspicuously
absent from the Heir-Apparent’s yarn, which
stamps that plump and elderly personage as a
much cuter man than his grovelsome adorers
suppose him to be. Force of circumstances has
made Albert Edward the most genuinely loyal
subject in Great Britain, but in setting up the
“ Diamond Jubilee ” as a particular excuse for
raising a big charity fund, he doesn’t place his
mother in front of the more deserving object,
or beside it, or anywhere in the object’s vici-
nity. Also, he cheerfully admits that the earth
belongs to One Above, by mentioning ground-
landlords as the first people who should express
their sentiment of gratitude in hard cash. The
“ Wales” epistle may be read by any respect-
able democrat without a blush. Can one say
as much for the jubilee manifestoes of Her
Gracious Majesty’s leading loyal subjects at
this end of the Hempire ?

J.D. : —The greasy contributors to the Fat Man
papers excel in saying small and spiteful things
in the most bilious way. For instance, Sed-
don’s proposed inscription of “he loved the
people ” on the Ballance monument is des-
cribed by an Australasian scribe as “hate-
ful ” ; and Mrs. Seddon’s action in planting a
wreath on the grave of Hori Grey’s infant,
buried fifty years ago in South Australia, is
written up as being in the worst possible taste.
The crowd that goes madder than usual and
weeps maudlin tears of joy while howling the
National Anathema, is fearfully jealous when
“ common people ” tap the artesian stream of
sentimentality. How they squealed when Sed-
don took off his coat to helu at the Brunner
mine disaster ! Yet how noble it was, accord-
ing to those same papers, when that trans-
parent self-advertiser, Baron Carington, sent
round his vice-regal carriage to cart a bashed
footballer off the field of gore !
Apropos Mr. John Morley’s recent remarks in
the House of Commons : —The native problem
in the Nor’west (W.A.) should soon disappear,
owing to the nig. supply running short. A few
months ago two natives, after the attempted
murder of a white man, joined their own tribe.
Result: Police declared war against the tribe,
killed six at the first encounter, and, following
them up, three more three days afterwards.
The two men who were wanted, as usual,
escaped, but the police found that those killed
were all “ noted murderers and cattle-stealers,”
besides having “associated” with the two
delinquents. Another murder occurred on the
Fitzroy River recently ; result, two nigs, killed
and several (number unknown) wounded—no
policemen hurt. Black troopers “ obeyed in-
structions admirably.” Again the murderers
escaped. It is unhealthy to be a nig. in the
north if there has been any spearing done
recently. Pathetic telegrams from the scene of
the outbreak appear in the Perth papers,
expressing the hope that the police authorities
in Perth would not interfere before the nigger-
drive was brought off. The authorities did not
interfere, and the next telegram was to the
effect that “arrangements had been made for
finally dispersing the gang if the Perth authori-
ties concur.” The Perth authorities concurred.
The Cold-Tea brigade already raises its voice
to demand that the power shall be reserved to
the separate States of the Federation that
hasn’t yet arrived, to abolish Der-rink, each
within its own borders. Apparently under the
draft constitution they have got the power ; it
is provided that all powers not expressly
reserved to the Federal Government shall
remain with the States, and there is no men-
tion of whisky in the Constitution. This item
should lead to some first-class riot by-and-bye.
The present idea is that, after a certain period,
the Federal Government shall simply lumD
together the customs and excise revenue in one
fund, and divide the surplus, if any, among the
States according to population. Whatever else
happens, it may be safely assumed that the
liquor duties will still remain, and pro-
bably at about the same rate as at pre-
sent. Therefore, if, say, N.S.W. shut down
on alcohol in all forms, and prohibited its
manufacture, sale or importation, it would
deprive the Federal Government of about
£900,000 a year revenue, and thus make the
Federal Treasurer sit up quite suddenly. Then
the other provinces, which were still given to
drink, would have to pay a great part of
N.S.W.’s share of the Federal expenses as
well as their own ; and if the surplus was
divided on a population basis, N.S.W. might
actually get more money handed back to it
than it contributed. It looks as if it would be
necessary to make some special provision to
prevent any long-haired fanatics fooling with
the Federal revenue in the name of the Lord.


A forceful letter from Mr. James Ashton,,
M. L. A., “ Map Di vi sions versus Men, ” dealing with
the subject of representation in the Federal
Senate, will appear in next issue of The Bulletin.
Dan. O’Connor’s recent after-dinner dia-
tribe against the N.S.W. Public Service Commis-
sion much amuses those who happen to know
that one of the O’Connor clan has just been made
to sit up by the Board for neglecting his official
duties, and that this is not the first occasion on
which the hated triumvirate has had to haul one
of the said clan over the coals.
There are shows and shows. The Agricul-
tural Society’s aims to be good ; sundry shows are
occasionally better; but A. Saunders’s show of
watches, jewelry, and silver-plate at George-street,
Sydney, near the railway station, is always em-
phatically the best. A. Saunders stocks every-
thing that is good and fashionable ; and his prices
are the lowest in Sydney for the quality.*
The Corbett - Fitzsimmons battle will
shortly be reproduced with faithful details at
the Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-st., Sydney.
It was cabled that the great fight was recorded
all through by the Kinetoscope, and the colonial
branch of the Edison Laboratory before the fight
took place made its arrangements for securing the
record. The Edison Vitascope, Kinetoscope, and
Kinetophone, and the wonderful X-rays are still
on show in the Parlor, and are attracting great
public interest.*
