SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Criterion “ Charley’s Aunt.”
Tivoli Rickards’ Variety Co.
Her Majesty's “ThePrisonerofZenda.”
Lyceum “ Sq uattocracy. ”
Palace Ovide Musin Concert Co
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “Romeo and Juliet.”
Royal “ in Sight of St. Paul’s.”
Bijou “ The Beggar Student.”
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
The great desolation which surrounds the
Good Friday season and heralds the hot-cross
bun is upon the Sydney theatres. The Royal re-
mains closed; Her Majesty’s and the Palace are
shut up till Saturday ; the Lyceum is still de-
serted ; the Opera House is deserted practically
all the time, and has no occupant except the ghost
of many previous ghosts that failed to walk ; the
Gaiety and Royal Standard are abandoned, as
usual ; and except for the Tivoli and Criterion,
which still keep to the fore, the cold blast of
shutting-up has swept over every house in Sydney.
Altogether it is about the roost beautifully unani-
mous closing on record.
** * *
Her Majesty’s re-opens on Saturday, with
the “Prisoner of Zenda,’’ when Williamson’s
recently-imported co. will lay before a presumably
large and fas ionable audience the troubles of the
red-headed King of Ruritania. The closing nights
of “ Matsa ” were ornamented by a prosperous
farewell matinee to Miss Florence Young and by
an indignant speech from Mr. Knowles, who took
a pathetic leave of the audience and made sundry
unfeeling observations about this paper. As far
as could be gathered, Mr. Knowles is not going to
become a regular subscriber to The Bulletin for
the rest of his life, and its red cover won’t be found
blowing about his backyard if he can help it, and
the Knowles infant, if there is one, will not be
allowed to amuse itself with “ Hop’s ” cartoons
unless it subscribes surreptitiously on its own
account when its father is away at the theatre in a
hurry as usual. It was the first time a Sydney
audience saw the recent funny man in an in-
tentionally serious mood, and his attitude was
impressive, not to say homicidal.
** * *
That heavenly fiddler, Ovide Musin, backed
up by Madame Musin, Misses Eva Mylott, and
Constance Hatherley, Mr. Burns-Walker—
whose singing “ mak.es the girls cry ” and
others, gives a Good Friday night concert at Syd-
ney Town Hall. On Saturday the same seraphic
combination starts a concert season at the Palace.
** * #
The same night the adventurous William
Cosgrove the man who waged the historic
war with Westmacott for possession of Her
Majesty’s and carried out the opposition firm and
laid it gently on the pavement—re-opens his tem-
pestuous career at the Lyceum with a comedy-
drama called “ Squattocracy.” His advt. an-
nounces the “ special engagement of Miss Myra
Kemble after her wonderful recovery from an
almost fatal illness.” Sydney had practically lost
sight of this favorite of brighter and more pro-
sperous days—the days when the Harbor City was
a gay and frivolous place with vine-leaves in its
hair, and when sand-shifting wasn’t the prevailing
industry—but it should roll up in a stupendous
mass to congratulate her on her recovery, and give
her its unanimous assurance that she still lives in
the great horsey soul of the community,
** * *
That pretty comedy - drama, “Sweet
Lavender,” will fill in this week at Sydney Cri-
terion. A revival of “Charley’s Aunt” follows
on Saturday, when the respectable elderly relative
from Brazil, where the hard and exasperating
vegetable comes from, should have an excellent
reception. The Tivoli has added the silvery-
voiced Phil Newbery to an already strong com-
pany, and all lovers of excellent music are hereby
cordially invited to go and listen to that stout
phenomenon. And there is practically nothing
else to report, save that Jimmy M‘Mahon’s cine-
matographe goeth on for ever; and the vitascope,
where the Corbett-fight machine is, hangs out at
the old address. An indefinable air of expect-
ancy, born of waiting till the dyspeptic bun has
blown over, pervades even the smaller shows this
week, and the same’* feeling enshrouds these few
paragraphs. The prevailing sentiment is that
nothing in particular is worth doing till the soul-
ful Friday victual rolls by. Next week’s issue
will duly chronicle the outburst of glory which
follows when the publio has got that provender off
its mind.
»* * *
Some rather complimentary remarks upon
“ A Woman’s Revenge,” at Melb. Royal, should
have appeared in last issue of this family journal,
but didn’t, owing, perhaps, to the Melbourne
Postal Department’s slow recovery from a recent
annual picnic. The remarks in question are there-
fore lost to literature. “A Woman’s Revenge”
will be numbered among the Bland Holtian by-
gones after to-night (Thursday), and the theatre
will close for the next night only, by Act of Par-
liament, leaving the management at liberty to
engage in a solemn Easter bun-struggle. The
new show for Saturday is “In Sight of St.
Paul’s,” a drama that includes the most glaring
fire-scene on record, and is in other respects calcu-
lated to make an audience turn pale.
«* * *
Talking of pallid audiences—at the Dra-
matic Fund Benefit, a fortnight ago, there was a
scare. Not a serious scare, fortunately, but just
sufficient of an alarm to excite the self-preservation
instinct in several loving husbands, who were
about to leave Anna Maria to her doom, when the
voices of a large majority forced them to sit down
again. Under such conditions, the resumption of
one’s place beside the woman one intended to flee
from is a very painful ordeal.
** * *
Mrs. Potter and Bellew are returning to
the Princess’s on Saturday for a three-weeks’
season. The first revival of those picturesque
artists will happen in “Romeo and Juliet,” a
substantial co. having been engaged to assist their
interpretation of the Immortal Bard. Kyrle
Bellew, it seems, has “thoroughly thought out ”
the question as to whether Juliet’s marriage, was
a failure, and the Firm expects gratifying results
from his cogitation.
#* * *
The Bijou, closed since the Broughs tore
themselves away, re-opens on Saturday with
Henry Bracy in the chair. In Melbourne the
time is nearly always ripe for comic opera, and
just at present “ The Beggar Student ” sounds
promising. Also, the prices are popular, and the
leading ladies have the charm of newness, conse-
quently Bracy is entitled to expect a fair run for
his money.
** * *
At Rickards’s Opera House a neatly-
balanced programme is filling the premises, as
usual, and if the management ventures to attempt
special attractions for Easter The Bulletin will
not be answerable for the decease of anyone who
gets fatally squeezed in the doorway. The Phil-
harmonic Society breaks out again at Melbourne
Town Hall, on Good Friday, with “ Elijah,” sup-
ported by the presence of Brassey, K.C.B. At
the Exhibition Building an excited state of affairs
will prevail for some days to come. On Friday a
concert, on Saturday the great Eight Hours
corroboree, and on Monday and during the rest of
next week, a Druids’ Gala on a stupendous scale.
Art-Union prizes, phenomenal wheel-races, un-
rivalled baby-contests, and other festivities
arranged in honor of the Eight Hours movement
and the Ancient Druids are too numerous to men-
tion. The price of a ticket for either show is a
nimble shilling to whieh remote possibilities are
attached.
** * *
Two nights last week a ghastly caricature
of “ Rob Roy ” was perpetrated at Melbourne
Alexandra by the local Caledonian Society. In
some way or other the local Thistle Club was con-
cerned in this hideous event, but whether the
Thistles acted for the benefit of the Caledonians,
or the Caledonians made objects of themselves
whilst the Thistles cheered them on, the present
writer is unable to say. In either case both par-
ties deserve unqualified condemnation, inas-
much as the doers of the deed were
no worse than the loyal Scots who en-
couraged them to do it again. Democrats at a
distance may argue that applause bestowed on
Jock M‘lntyre, M.L.A., would be excusable if it
induced him to abandon politics for the stage.
The writer held the same opinion until he actually
saw M‘lntyre as Nicol Jarvie, since when he feels
certain that the veteran legislator had better be
left in charge of the Conservative party.
• • • •
“ Rob Roy ” was a public entertainment,
open to Irishmen, Englishmen, and every
other kind of foreigner who could be
bounced into paying for admission. A
Scotch in a kilt displayed himself out-
side the theatre as a pictorial advt., with the
result that a few members of the general
public dropped their money down the sink of his-
trionio iniquity. Concerning Miss Eloise Juno
and a couple of nice-looking lady amateurs, the
Voice of Truth will remain silent, but, as regards
MTntyre and his male colleagues, the Voice takes
this chance to let off a derisive yell.
At Poverty Point.
Kate Vaughan, who lately visited Australia, and was
understood to be dying of Bright’s disease, has been
divorced by her husband, Col. Wellesley, for adultery
with an actor. Miss Vaughan, it will 'be remembered,
was married by Col. Wellesley, erstwhile a member of
the Prince of YVales’ special set, after he had been divorced
by his wife, a daughter of N.S.W. former-Governor Lord
Augustus Loftus.
From S.M. Herald, Monday, April 12, ’97 :—“ Miss
Wray, who has a great deal of comic talent, has done
some brilliant work during her short career, which in
Sydney dates only from her appearance as Clairette, in
‘ Madame Angot,’ on 25th Sept., 1895. Her retirement is
due to her approaching marriage with a leading medical
practitioner at Auckland.” It may interest the public
to know that the lady was married to Dr. Sharman by
the N.S.W. Registrar-General last month. The regis-
ter sets forth that the bride was Juliette Gertrude
Flegeltaub, of Ballarat, aged 21, and that the other party
to the proceedings was Edward William Sharman,
Juliette’s senior by ten years. The deed was perpetrated
on March 15, so that the honeymoon is over, and Juliette
is a well-established married woman.
The Bulletin’s announcement of Olga Nethersole’s
marriage is probably premature. The event was arranged
to happen last month, but letters recently to hand from
’Murka state that Miss N. and her husband-elect have put
off the evil day until September.
At a musical matinee given in honor of Massenet at the
Ecole Marchesi, the most prominently-placed artiste was
Cicely Staunton (Goldenstedt), of Sydney, who sang an
Ave Maria and other items. Miss Staunton got some of
her Australasian musical training in her native Maori-
land, and subsequently gained much benefit from the
tuition of Annis Montague, of whose company she was
for some time a member.
The rivalry between Melba and Nordica daily grows
more pronounced, especially over the rendering of Wag-
nerian roles. Melba, writing to a N.Y. journal, states
that she does not intend representing Brunhilde for
years to come for fear of injuring her voice. Nordica,
who subsequently appeared as Brunhilde, wrote to the
same journal as a parting dig: “ Wagner’s music never
injured anyone who could sing it, and who knew how.”
Artist Harry Furniss comes to Australia, as Mark Twain
came, to build up broken fortunes. He has never pro-
spered since he left London Punch, and his paper, Lika
Joko, merged in the New Budget, died without many
public tears. Furniss, as a black-and-white artist, does
not seem to have done much that is notable outside his
field as a Parliamentary cartoonist, and ranks to-day
rather among the bygones.
During the Potter-Bellew Melb. season a little play,
written by a smart Australian youth, is to have a hearing.
“ A Studio Episode,” by Arnold Denham, is to be given
as a curtain-raiser to “ Forget-me-not.”
Melb. : “ Sims makes a bad shot, in his last ‘ Mustard
and Cress,’ of London Referee. He ingeniously pieces it
up that Gabriel, the nigger turned white, shown lately in
Mein., is one born white, who appeared in London.
Gabriel's change is certified to by the Adelaide Hospital
authorities.”
The Bulletin hears, on good authority, that Miss Bessie
Rignold’s much-criticised red wig in “ Lavender ” was not
her own idea, b'ut was forced on her by a color-
blind management with a yearning for weird, tomatO-
hued effects. Considering that the character was very
good on the whole, and that the w r ig was very awful, this
paper is of opinion that the management should allow
Miss Bessie to select her own hair in future. If she
selects it as well as she does the rest of her adorable self
she will make a much better success than the person who
chose the present lurid top-hamper did. Besides, if a
lady can’t select her own hair, where has the liberty of
the subject gone to ?
Marion Terry’s claim for damages against the newspaper
which “ engaged” her to a brother-in-law- was presumably
based on the fact that she is already married to another
fellow.
Melb. demise : Allan, of the Collins-street music ware*
house. A singularly amiable, graceful man.
Melb. : “ The proverbial coolness of our audiences was
not evinced in the fire scare at the Princess matinee.
This house is on a corner, as every theatre should be, with
no vestibule, bars, or other impedimenta, between the
audience and the streets. The Bijou, all upstairs, is com-
paratively a trap, the Opera House a tinder-box.
Melb. : “ ‘ Romeo and Juliet’ has not often been acted
in this city. Brook only took Mercutio. So did Barry
Sullivan. Curious that no adequate Romeo was ever seen
here till Bellew s first visit. Mrs. Scott-Siddons has been
our best Juliet, with Dampier snorting Romeo. The
Mighty Atom fetched to Australia the scenery of Irving’s
London production, with Miss Reid as Juliet! while Cates
got about his last chance as Romeo.”
Rossi was a splendid Romeo up to sixty, but Bellew
doesn’t age—any more.
The Bach Passion Music, Melb. Anglican Cathedral,
half-a-crown a noli, was announced as a “ service.” Con-
sequently, Toorakia attended in evening-dress, as a com-
pliment to the Almighty.
Musgrove has secured the rights of the enormously-suc-
cessful comic-opera “ La Poupie,” by Audran.
Apropos Ada Ward. A clergyman brought under
Irving’s notice the fact that Sheridan Knowles abandoned
the stage for the pulpit. Irving : “ But he went on tak-
ing the royalties for his plays.”
Sam Moyle, whose rugged baritone did useful service,
of yore, at Holy Trinity Church, Melbourne, is now choir-
master at the Church of Reconciliation, New York.
Gladstone Wright, back in Melbourne, resumes his old
place among the £lOO a-year members of St. Paul’s Cathed-
ral choir, and will stay for twelve months, anyway. Al-
ways an artistic singer, Wright has benefited, vocally, by
his trip to England, where he will in due course settle
down for good if he can’t see a certainty of doing better
on this side. At present Australia is about the coldest
country on earth for concert-singers.
Miss Eileen O’Moore, known to Australians as Bessie
Doyle, is professionally prospering in London, and lives
in great style—lovely gowns, plenty of diamonds, and a
well-appointed carriage.
For “ La Poup6e,” which is doing wonderful biz. at the
Prince of Wales’s, writes a Bulletin correspondent from
London, Billy Walshe shaved his beautiful moustache,
and in the new guise is singularly like Greenaway.
Walshe manages to run in a few odd engagements
between the waits in “ La Poup6e.’.
Melba, at latest, was ill in Paris, and makes a very bad
patient. Her enforced retirement from her American
season is very galling, for she is quite the idol of the
States. Besides, there is a guarantee of £40,000 hanging
on to her Brazilian tour.
When Will R. Barnes, of New York, gets there, he is
bound to run up against his one-time deadly rival for the
smile of a certain actress—the bewtchus “Bob” Burt,
one time Age dramatic critic, and now trying his luck in
America. Barnes and Burt both used to turn up nightly
at the Princess with floral sprays for the willowy one
and, after delivering them solemnly at the dressing-room
of the diva, would scowl at one another “ in front ” while
they waited to see whose’favor would be worn. Madame
was wily, and regularly wore the offering of each lovelorn
loon in alternate acts.
They are telling- a story of the scare at the Melbourne
Princess’s down Yarrawards. Immediately after the out-
break of the alarm, a portly citizen, crimson, breathless
and tattered as if he had just been tossed by ten cows’
landed with a bound in front of the stalls pay-box, howl!
ing : “ Theatre’s a-fire ! gimme me money back !’* ’
Mrs. Molyneux, the handsome and clever actress, well-
remembered in Sydney, is now in the English provinces"
playing the leading character in “ Under the Red Robe '
English press notices to hand are mostly jam and
enthusiasm.
Colborne Baber—under the name of “ Lilia Harrison "
—made her London ddbut at the Queen's Hall sacred
concert on Asn Wednesday, and created quite a favorable
impression. “ Among the more attractive features of the
concert,” said London D.T., “was the earnest and un-
affected rendering of the solo in ‘Hear My Prayer’ bv
Madame Lilia Harrison, an Australian soprano, w ho made
a promising dbbut.”
Cooper, J. (Brisbane'), commenting on bankruptcy
counsel’s charge that a bankrupt pro. had borrowed £4O
from Tom Finney under false pretences: “He seems to
be able to strike some very good-natured people.
This transaction will probably save Mr. Finney a good
many times £4O in the future.” His Honor declined to
“ commit.” In short, the Court seemed to hold that that
m immer had done Finney a good turn—given him sav
£2OO worth of experience for £4O (!).
Young Stanley Moss, late of Windsor, N.S.W., has
made such rapid strides as a violinist at Brussels Con-
servatoire that the famous Ysaye, writing to the lad’s
father, informs him that the director has chosen Moss out
of 60, who passed the Conservatoire examination, to fill
the vacant place of first violin in his orchestra.
The troubles of the cheap bush pro. are many and
various. One co. not long since played “East Lynne”
in the backblocks with a cast of four. One man did
Francis Levison, Richard Hare, old Justice Hare, the
man-servant, and Lady Isabel’s guardian—five characters
in all. A lady did Barbara Hare and Little Willie, and
two more people did the other characters. The co. did
its own scene-shifting, and the remaining member was
bill-sticker, business manager, and money-taker. One
especially disastrous night of “East Lynne ” left the co.
with 17s. all told, and it became necessary to walk to the
next town, 15 miles away, and hump the props. The co.
slept in a vineyard en route, Lady Isabel and Barbara
Hare being discreetly planted by themselves, while
Levison went round with a stick to keep off snakes.
S.G. : “ After pondering over many late shows. Wanted
a ‘don’t-care-a-damn-age ’ actor—(look at the list—
Davenapt, Betterton, Shakespeare, Macready, Kean,
Foot, Montgomery, Brooke, and plenty more). " The pre-
sent day mummer is mostly a mere creature of the stage-
manager, who loses his intellectual rigidity in a profound
desire not to spoil his pretty clothes or fall over the hired
furnishings. There is an opening as wide and broad and
deep as the Bottomless Pit for an acter with brains, and
blood in them.”
“ Fanny Brough is the moving spirit in a Diamond
Jubilee scheme to form an English Acting Academy on
the lines of the French Conservatoire,” writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent. “ Privately she is
vivacious and sparkling with naught of brother Bob’s
lugubriousness, and on the stage the most popular drama
or comedy actress in London. She resides in plutocratio

The WILD CAL
COLUMN
The Bulletin has persistently maintained that
the only way to get a decent railway system without
differential rates, cut-throat border tariffs, diverse gauges
and the many other extravagances that now prevail, is to
make the lines Federal property with one central manage-
ment for all Australia. The Convention favors instead
the establishment of a Federal Commission which will fix
uniform rates for uniform distances, so that commerce
can always go to the nearest port (if there is any avail-
able road). Otherwise things are to be left to provincial
management as before. But Commissioner Eddy’s evi-
dence as to how he proposes to work the uniform-rate
business effectually blasts the prospects of this device.
On the surface, “ uniform rates ” simply mean that rates
will be uniform ; for instance, it is 288 miles from Junee
to Sydnev, and the same distance from Junee to Mel-
bourne, therefore dull, common people take a uniform-
rate to mean that the freight will be the same from
Junee to either city. Commissioner Eddy figures it out
quite differently. Freights diminish (in one sense) as
distances increase ; say the rate for a certain class of
goods is fixed at ss. per ton for 50 miles, it may then be
9s. 6d. for 100 miles, 16s. 6d. for 200 miles, 21s. 6d. for 300
miles, 255. per ton for 400 miles, and all distances beyond
that. Therefore : Junee to Sydney, 288 miles, £1 Is. 6d.
per ton. If the same goods were sent Melbourne-wards,
however, the distance would be counted as follows : Junee
to Albury (where the N.S.W. system ends), 100 miles, 9s.
6d. ; Albury to Melbourne (Victorian system), 190 miles,
16s. 6d. ; consequently, for that 288 miles the
freight would be £1 ‘6s. Commissioner Eddy says
this is what he calls equal freights. Commissioner
Mathieson, of Victoria, says that what he calls equal
freights are that the rate should be the same from the
half-way town to either Melbourne or Sydney, and that
anything else is only a juggle and a swindle, or something
like that. As Eddy’s idea of equal rates for equal
distances would enable N.S.W., when the Barrier railway
is finished, to make the freights pretty nearly as high
from Broken Hill to Adelaide (about 300 miles) as from
the same town to Sydney (900 miles), it is likely the S.A.
people will make remarks very like Mathieson s. When
the rival managements can’t even agree on the meaning
of “ uniform rates,” the prospects of arriving at any good
results under the present divided control look about as
small as a baby microbe.
* » * •
Sydney Teleoraph seems to have curiously
misread the financial proposals of the Convention. These
were as follows : “ That during the first five years after
the uniform tariff has come into operation, the Federal
Government shall continue to keep such statistics as may
be necessary to secure the distribution of the surplus to
each State, on the s une bar is as that adopted during pre-
vious years, but so that the amount to be returned
during any year shall not be less than the amount re-
turned during the year previous to the uniform tariff
having come into operation.” This means that at first,
until the Federal Government shall devise a uniform
system, each province shall retain its own tariff, the
revenue received from each province shall be kept
separate, it shall be debited with its share of the expendi-
ture, and get back the surplus. When the Federal
tariff comes into force, each province shall for five
years still have its revenue and expenditure kept
separate, and shall get back the surplus “on the
same basis in other words, it shall get back
its own surplus, and the Federal Government
promises to raise so much revenue that the surplus re-
turned to each province shall be not less —though it may
be a lot more—than it was before the uniform tariff was
created. The Teleoraph, by some quaint inversion of
things, reads that each province shall get back not more
than the “ unalterable minimum ” it received under the
separate tariffs, and it publishes huge masses of figures
to demonstrate that the other provinces will, therefore,
get N.S.W.’s surplus, and that N.S.W. will be robbed of a
million or so per annum by its grasping neighbors, and
sools on the province to resist this wholesale fraud to the
death. _ ,
• * * *
Maoriland finishes the financial year with a sur-
plus of £340,000, including a small balance brought for-
ward from the previous year, and it is proposed to put
£200,000 of this into the Public Works Fund. But the
fund can’t live on the casual and unreliable surplus alone,
so the Seddon Government is floating its £1,000,000 loan
ta keep things going. There will never be an end to bor-
rowing for public works until the Govt, sets aside taxes
(which will yield at least £500,000 a year) for this especial
purpose, and causes the proceeds of them to be paid into
a fund altogether apart from the regular revenue. Maori-
land has had a remarkably prosperous career—and its
public debt has increased during the 12 months by
£1,270,682. The “ self-reliant policy” again !
* * * *
The Victorian State Land Bank proposes to get
to work in real earnest by raising, every fortnight,
a loan of about £25,000, at 3 per oent., as heretofore.
It is calculated that this amount will just about keep pace
with the advances made on mortgage, and there will thus
be no loss of interest through money lying idle. If the
department can run the business on this basis, Victoria
will have the best and most economically-managed Land
Bank in Australasia, and will be in a position to advance
nearly £700,000 a year at not much over one-half what
the sweated borrower has hitherto been paying. The
relief whieh this will afford to the harassed agricultural
industry in the course of a few years is almost beyond
•calculation.
* * * *
By the way, how long is it since anything was
heard of the great scheme to amalgamate the two N.S.W.
savings banks, and to establish a State loan department,
and a bank of issue, and other beautiful things on the
foundation thus created V

The National Bank announces that, on 22nd
July, it will pay off another 15 per cent, of its recon-
structed deposits, making 67 per cent, paid off in all.
This clears off all liabilities up to the end of 1899 The
Bank*of Victoria pays another instalment on 12th July,
which will make about £4,000,000 paid out of the original
liability of £6,000,000. Both institutions reckon on get-
ting rid of the last of their reconstructed deposits early
next year. t »
After a long silence, the Royal Bank (Mel-
bourne') Days a 3 percent, dividend. The profits for the
half-year 3 are £3398, as against £2044 last half, £2099 at
March ’96, and £2038 and £2136 for the two halves of 90.
The figures, therefore, show a handsome improvement.
The Roval lately wrote off the accumulated balance at
credit of profit and loss (£21,521), and £150,000 (one-half)
of its capital, and as these amounts amount to a good
deal more than half the shareholders’ funds, they ought
to fully cover all losses and possible losses, and make the
institution absolutely solid. The dividend absorbs
£2250, so the Royal starts its new career with
a balance of £ll4B. And it should take this
opportunity to decide that it will put away 25 per cent, of
its pr lilts, in good years and bad, as a reserve fund, until
the said reserve fund reaches at least £150,000. A bank
with 150,000 capital, no reserve, and only tIUS of
accumulations is likely to be a very small earthen pot to
the end of its days unless it takes some such resolution as
the above. The Royal has apparently a good little busi-
ness ■ it has a very solid cash reserve equal to 27 per cent.
on liabilities, and it ought to do a lot better in the future
than it has been doing of late years.

Fanner and Co., of Sydney, has been floated
into a hig absentee co. (capital, £350,000) in London.
This huge drapery was always more or less of a British
institution, and now it will, presumably, be British alto-
gether. If anyone could produce an accurate list of the
British and other foreign concerns in Sydney, and show
just how little the cornstalk owns of his own city, it
should be interesting reading.
» * * »
The Industrial and Provident Permanent Bene-
fit Building Society (Sydney), as the result of a year’s
work, pays 7 per cent, to shareholders, writes off £2095
for depreciation, and has £3OO left to add to previous
balancej making £4765. These results show that, even in
times like the present, there is money in the building
society business, if the society sticks to its own business,
and keeps clear of the delusive suburban estate that is
cut up into time-payment allotments.
• « * •
The saddest piece of financial news just now is
to the effect that the Tenterfield (N.S.W.) beet-sugar
scheme has fallen through for the present, because the
British capitalist won’t put up the needful £20,000. The
self-reliant province of N.S.W.—which claims to be the
richest country on this earth, and has over £7,000,000 of
gold in its banks, and which is always talking about its
amazing progress and enterprise—wouldn’t risk this
microscopic sum to start a new enterprise out of which
commonplace Germans and Frenchmen and Russians and
other trash make millions annually. But it followed its
usual instinct and howled on the door-mat of Father Bull
for assistance, and as he wasn’t on the show apparently
collapses like a house of cards.
* * * *
Auckland city (M.L.) is enjoying an experience
of the ways of private enterprise. A long time ago it was
agreed to give to a syndicate the sole right of construct-
ing, owning, and working electric tramways in the city
for 21 years. The dunderheaded local Council, however,
quite forgot to make it obligatory on the syndicate to
construct and work the trams, so that it could, if it
pleased, simply do nothing and prevent anybody else
doing anything for the best part of a generation. The
syndicate didn t build any trams, but by-and-bye it de-
manded an extension of the monopoly to 30 years. This
time the Council had sense enough to require a deposit
of £lOOO as a guarantee that the syndicate would really
do something, and, the terms being agreed to, it gave the
sole right for 30 years as demanded. In its usual
premature way, however, it gave the concession be-
fore the £lOOO deposit was forthcoming, and neither
it nor the electric tramway has, so far, appeared.
Next the syndicate demands an extension of its
monopoly to 50 years, and practically admits that
it really isn’t going to build any trams at all ;
its purpose is to sell the concession in London at a big
figure, and let the company that will be floated there do
the building, or not do it, as it pleases. The syndicate
frankly admits that it wants the 50-year monopoly to
make the concession more saleable —so that it may get a
larger sum for doing nothing at all. This latest demand
will probably be a failure, but even as things are the syn-
dicate, if it likes to deposit £lOOO, which is a very
moderate amount after all, can block all improvement of
Auckland's primitive tram way-service for 30 years, and if
it can’t sell its concession to advantage in London, it will
probably lie low and keep on blocking the wav' until the
city gives it £20,000 or £50,000 or so, to surrender its
“ rights ” and get out of the road. Meanwhile the years
roll on, and the primitive horse-tram toils about the
streets as usual, and as the Council has given to the
syndicate, practically gratis, the right to forbid any im-
provement until 1926 unless it is bought off, the old,
square vehicle and the old, angular oats’-meat fainting in
the shafts are likely to be permanent institutions.
Victorian brandy is now supplied in the refresh-
ment-room of the British House of Commons, it is used
to some extent by the British army, and the General
Asylum Tender Board admits it as being of equal quality
with the famous brands of Ilennessy and Martell. Yet
the local drinker doesn’t drink it as a rule ; he briefly
describes it as “ colonial rubbish” ; and Victoria imports
about 75,000 gallons of brandy and alleged brandy
annually from Europe in order that it may have the joy
of swallowing real foreign rotgut. The cheerful, off-hand
assumption that everything local, except pugilists, foot-
ballers and wool, is “colonial rubbish” is one of the chief
curses of this country.

“ The Q.N. Bank owes its existence to publio

* * * *
One of the subjects to which the interprovincial
railway-commissioners gave attention at their conference
in Adelaide was the sale of tickets by unauthorised
ao-ents. This industry alone provides a tip-top living for
three persons in Sydney, five in Melbourne, and three in
Adelaide and Brisbane, to whom may be added can-
vassers, outside runners, and clerks. It is estimated that
the combined loss to the railways from this source amounts
to between £3OOO and £4OOO annually. The reason no
prosecutions have been instituted so far is that tickets
are only subject to the by-laws of the province of issue,
and, as the return halves are mostly sold in the other
provinces, the difficulties and expense of prosecution
would not be recouped by the fine imposed. Under Fed-
eration, of course, this would be done away with, but
meanwhile, it is understood, the commissioners have ar-
ranged a scheme which, it is hoped, will materially check
this leakage in the revenue.

The secession of MTlwraith, M‘Eacharn and
Co from the Westralian freights-and-fares schedule of the
Associated Steamship Association means a big fight, al-
most to a finish. No one need be surprised if a man will be
able soon to get from Sydney to Perth for “ a note.” But
the future will be as the past. The co’s that don t knock
under wall pool and make up for temporary losses by
awful extortions. Besides, the “lowness of freights,”
the “absence of profits,” etc., will give shipowners an
apparent justification for “reducing expenses” (wages)
when the chance comes.
* • * *
As illustrating the never-ending audacity of
“bust” banks, a case before Chief Justice Madden, in
Melbourne, the other day, is interesting. One Ferguson
sued the liquidator of the City of Melb. Bank, to recover
certain moneys paid into that financial sink in error,
Before the City went under, Ferguson drew a cheque on
his account to pay two creditors in England, and the usual
drafts were sent along. This overdrew the customer’s
account £ll9. Believing the drafts would be 0.K., Fer-
guson subsequently paid this £ll9 into the bank. But
the Citv of M. broke, and refused to meet the drafts, and,
furthermore, the wild-time bank blandly declined to
ante up the £ll9 The customer sued to recover, and it
is comforting to know that the financial institution was
compelled to disgorge, the verdict being an assurance
that all the money a broken bank gets hold of under any
circumstances is not its own to have and to hold, till the
crack of doom or thereabouts. #
A profitable department of business of some
Sydney insurance co’s is the insurance of gold coin ex-
ported by Chinese merchants. The usual premium is
7s. 6d. per £lOO for boxes of 1000 sovs. and over. Each
£lOOO is covered for £l2oo—the extra £2OO being the
exporter’s cost and profit. Chinese gardeners remit their
tens and twenties to their relatives in China through the
big merchants, who charge Is. in the £ and take all risks.

Cobar: “T-he secretary of the Westralia pro-
specting syndicate which owns the Queen of the Earth
mine at Yerilla, W.A., has received information that

* • * *
The jumpers of the Homeward Bound mine,
Yalwal (N.S.W.), having abandoned the intention to
appeal to the Privy Council, that co. can now resume
work on the mine. Small satisfaction this, after having
had the mine shut up for three years. The fear of
jumping is the greatest curse Australasian mining has
to put up with. For the hundredth time, why should a
jumper, who may be, and often is, the meanest creature
on earth, get the mine he jumps? Why shouldn’t he be
paid a fee, like the hangman, while the State more or less
heavily fines the defaulting lessees, or, in extreme cases,
auctions the mine, its own property, for the benefit of the
consolidated revenue ?
* • * *
“Conley and Barrass’s battery at the Peak mine,
Cobar, has been running continuously since Ist April.
Tables are coming on nicely, but plates inside the boxes
are doing most of the work, prominent ribs of hard amal-
gam showing under the feed-hole.” Why not wait for the
clean-up? When talk like this occurs in any sensible
mining-camp the result of the crushing has to be carried
away in a dray.

Golconda, Parkes, N.S.W. : “Reef more
pinched, but widened and looks better ; hauled about
three tons of stone, have another ton ready for hauling.”
How, in Heaven’s name, is all this hauling done? By a
windlass ? Then why does it not get the credit of it ? Is
the windlass working well? If so, why not at once say
so in the S.M.H., D.T., and other well-known and re-
spectable newspapers ?
Says the London correspondent of the S.M.
Herald :—“ The difficult task of selecting an inspector
of mines for N.S.W. out of the numerous applicants has
at last been accomplished.” Only from this source it is
learnt that 82 applicants were reduced to 6 by the Agent-
General, and one has been recommended for the post.
That England is not the best place in the world to go for
mining-inspectors, nor for mining men of any kind needed
in the working of Australian mines, there is surely ample
evidence. Men trained in the big mines of Australia are
quitegood enough to inspect mines in N. S.W. Australians
are considered good enough for such work in the other
provinces. » * *

Westralian mining news from a reliable
source “ Our mines (Hannan’s) are doing wonderfully
well. The Great Boulder has a good lode of from three
to five ounce ore on the 300, and all the other parts of the
mine are looking well. The Lake View mine is getting
into shape, and it will do all that people have claimed for
it. The Ivanhoe at the 300 was a disappointment to some,
but where the crosscuts strike the vein it has always been
of lower grade in the upper levels. The Horseshoe mine
is also looking very |well. The Australia is developing
large bodies of telluride ore. The reef at one point must
be 20ft. wide, and it can be shipped as it comes from the
mine for 6oz. to the ton average. By putting up Ropp
furnaces and cyaniding after roasting, they will be able to
extract over 90 per cent, at a cost of less than 40s. Quite
a number of tests have already been made by roasting
and cyaniding, and the extraction runs up to 96 per cent.
The next mine to open up on the 300 will be the Lake
View ; this will be done during the next six weeks.”
* * * *
A Westralian correspondent writes to Adelaide
Qnz: “For goodness sake try to prevent people from
breaking up their homes and coming over here at present.
There is no work, and 1500 men are idle at Kalgoorlie.
Paddington and Broad Arrow are like deserted villages.
Men are working for 255. a week and find themselves, and
they pay ss. a week for a bed in a room. Food is high,
and rent ditto.”
* * * *
We have to go to London for news of the
General Gordon, W.A. “For some time it has been
known that the 43ft.-wide reef has not gone down as was
anticipated, and more recently it has le .ked out that the
rich stringers of opaline quartz, which furnished such
splendid assay returns, have disappeared. As nearly as
is known, the Gordon has about 1000 tons of valuable ore
at grass, 400 tons of which is being crushed. What will
happen after this no man knoweth. Not even the expert
manager, Prof. Krause, can say whether the main reef
will be picked up again lower down. For the sake of W.A.
interests in the London market it is satisfactory to learn
that only a very small number of General Gordon shares
are held on this side,” says a London paper. No suoh
news has ever been known here. The writer is once more
really sorry for the tipsters who have gone on tipping Gor-
dons up to't.he last. The strong doubts expressed months
ago in this column about the Gordon were founded on
examinations by three mining men, not one of whom had
the slightest interest in the mine. In 99 cases in 100
opinions of any other sort are not worth having. The present
writer is sorry to say that there are not “1000 tons of valu-
able ore at grass as the battery will soon prove. The
former manager—and there are few better in W.A. —made
no secret of his opinion of the mine ; and now we have
the ominous silence of Professor Krause and the unmis-
takeable verdict of the share-market.
* * * *
Allen H. P. Stoneham, at the meeting, held in
London early in March, to protest against the labor con-
ditions and titles of mining leases in W.A., presided over
by Remember-Me-No-More Robinson: “He was very
much interested in W.A. The country was at present
under a cloud, and he could not but think that a great
deal of the depression waa owin'; to the adverse conditions
which governed the mining industry.” A much greater
deal of it is due to several other things. The hundreds of
thousands paid for mining-swindles, the hundreds of
thousands wasted on machinery for which there never
was any use, and the hundreds of thousands taken to
work off wild-cats by false reports, bribing newspapers, and
general boodle, have had a heap to do with the depres-
sion. To protest against) the labor-conditions, and to
demand better titles, are" only miserable excuses for rob-
bery and failure —and well these gentry know it.

owned by a Geelong crowd and managed by Jack Roberts,
now mining-editor of Sydney Daily Telegraph. Was
sold to Dr. Simon’s exploration company for £40,000 cash ;
a splendid deal —for the sellers. To give that money for
a mine, and to have one crushing in two years, means
bad business for somebody.
* * * •
A short-hand report of the evidence at the in-
quiry into the alleged conduct of two of the late directors
and a clerk in the office of the Queen Margaret No. 1
South, W.A., has been issued. When the shares were at
a premium there were 7200 shares unapplied for. These
were issued to the clerk at 2s. each ; and the investigating
Board finds that two of the late directors “ did expect to
be allowed to participate therein or in the profits to be
derived therefrom.” A late official also “ admits that he
accepted from the clerk one-sixth of the 7200 shares at
cost with a view of making a profit thereon and not for
the purpose of protecting the company.” This week the
shareholders meet to decide upon a course of action.
* » » *
A large crowd of men still working alluvial at
Lake Lefroy, Widgiemoola (W.A.). Reports of richness
vary greatly, but in W.A. it is safer to believe the worst.
’* » * •
The Hainault, an English mine at Hannan’s, on
which £30,000 has been spent, is again in trouble—this
time three miner’s-right claims have been pegged. Last
time it was forfeited for accidental non-payment of rent.
The odds are a million to one on the 4 co. winning. Look
at the state of the London market. P.S. —Late;: The oo.
won.
• * * *
Ope of the unfortunate dupes who came out to
the golden West to do something in connection with’the
imaginary properties of W.A. Gold District Trading Cor-
poration writes : —“ Most mining wild-cats do possess a
lease of some kind or other, but the co. now in liquidation
actually advertised a ‘ 300oz.-to-the-ton-rock-absolutely-
co vie red-with-gold ’ mine belonging to another co. alto-
gether as their own, presumably on the ground that the
manager had brought an action against them for non-
delivery of machinery under contract. The founders’
shares in this concern dropped in three months from £105
to 9s., and the philanthropist who engineered it has
dropped right out.” Truly an awful sample of Westralian
“ enterprise ” in London.
* * * •
Warnings still come from every part of Wes-
tralia urging eastern miners not to make for that pro-
vince, and hundreds still going weekly in the face of
them. The worse phase of the dead-boom has not yet
arrived. 1 ,
f * * «
Five reefs, discovered in Eastern Gippslani,
are reported to be traceable a distance of six miles, to be
in places 4ft. wide, and to have yielded ldwt. gold to lib.
stone where tested. The finrt»may be greatly exaggerated,
still it sets one thinking what a boom anything like that
would have made in Westralia three years ago, and what
a riot it would create to-day if it were only located in
some fever-haunted New Guinea swamp, with the skull of
the last prospector just showing above the green slime,
and some homicidal niggers in the distance, and 250 miles
of mountain and bush to be struggled through to reach it.

Long Tunnel, Vic., month: 1843tons, 1811oz.
The longest div.-payer in the province, with years of
work in sight. Some day, when the mine becomes poorer,
the management will have time to think over the amount
of gold that has gone away for ever in the tailings.

hold up bravely. Day Dawn Block and Wyndham, 3260
tons, 3203oz. —another 6d. div., equalling £1500 (£2500 in
three months). The Phoebe’s last crushing returned
lOOOoz. from 326 tons. A second distinct reef has been
found in this mine.

* * * *
The new Dead Horse Lead at Clermont pro-
mises to be the most permanent thing in the district.
Between six and eight hundred men are working there
just now. The lead has been traced 1J miles into deep
ground and is superior to anything about Clermont, the
wash-dirt being what is commonly called “ drive-high ”
and very rich. Last washing of Christensen and party
gave two loads for lib. fdwt. of clean gold. Many others
are doing remarkably well.
* * * *
Western silver-mine, Tas.—39th div. ; total to
date, 27s. a share or £81,000. Claimed for this mine that
per square foot worked it is the boss silver-mine of Aus-
tralasia. Cheeky, when it is remembered that one shil-
ling Broken Hill div. absorbs more money than its 39 I
“ A rich reef ” reported as discovered in New
Guinea, inland from Port Moresby. Wise men will await
much confirmation - both of the news and the reef—
before venturing their lives in that land of malaria and
massacre.
* « * *
According to Mulhall, all the gold yet raised in
Australia would fill a room 30ft. sq. by 12ft. high. The
quartz from which it was obtained would pile up a mass
30 times the size of the pyramid of Cheops. According
to the same authority, it has taken on an average 60,000
tons of stone to produce a ton of gold in Australia, against
70,000 tons in America.

SOCIETY
Now is the time for some well-informed
Australian scribe to furnish Pearson’s Weekly
and other English penny papers with details of
the London-bound Premiers. It might be stated
that Reid is a young and stately gentleman with
long, flowing, auburn hair, is of athletic build, and
much respected as the man who discovered coal at
Melbourne, N.Q. It might also be alleged, in an
off-hand way, that Kingston is an aboriginal, en-
tirely self-educated, and son of a harum-scarum
English Viscount who went to Australia in 1843,
married a black-gin, became Speaker of the Jam-
beroo Assembly, and made a fortune as a squatter
at Manly (S.A.). Some pathetic remarks about
the half-caste son returning to England to intro-
duce himself to his haughty relatives at the an-
cestral halls would come in well just here. A few
more items like these will be accepted readily by
the London papers, and will hit pretty hard the
well-informed people who are doing the work at
present.
THE BOGGED COW: A TALE OF A TALL.
“The buzz of the Diamond Jubilee
chorus,” writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent, “is beginning to be heard in
the land. Soon lengthy, lathy, big-boned men
with bronzed, hungry faces will be here from
‘afar off’and ‘ way back,’ and add an unobtru-
sive humming refrain to the all-pervading and
prevailing babel of many accents greeting the
breaking of the reign record. Not that they will
care much for the tenacious Guelphic grip of a
velvet billet, but Victoria has broken the English
record on a ‘ Slumber ’ machine, so ‘ let us drink
and be merry for to-morrow we fly ’ —back to
jumbucks and Jericho, bovines and blasting,
sweating and swearing, and ‘ graft ’ everlasting.
Much 1 bitter ’ in London means much bitter-
ness in the street, and the wheel of a ’bus occa-
sionally steers the soul of the stumbler to the land
of the Never-come-back on the stream of internal
hemorrhage. Her Gracious, scared by senile
superstition, declines to date the f6te, but the
Government has hired several storeys of the
huge Hotel Cecil, and the directors thereof
breathe freely again.”
* * * •
A certain Australasian Governor struck a
snag the other day. His private sec. by letter
intimated to the leading country hotelkeeper that
H.E. intended honoring his establishment with
his presence during a forthcoming local shivoo.
But Boniface knew H.E’s strong points, and
wrote back that he “ wasn’t taking any Governor.”
* * * *
Led by Lady Gormanston and aided and
abetted by the great Dobson clan of both sexes,
the women and girls of Tasmania jubilate at the
Longest Reign by presenting a memorial to Queen
Victoria. The smallest contributions gratefully
accepted, and each subscriber is to have her name
attached to the congratulatory memorial in alpha-
betical order. Any small change left over after
the memorial is paid for is to go towards augment-
ing the ’B7 Jubilee Fund for the relief of con-
valescent women. The alphabetical order idea
ought to boom the thing, especially among the first
letters of the alphabet. But no province, however
loyal, can be expected to grovel persistently all
the way from A. to Z.
* * * *
About the latest idea for a great Record Reign celebra-
tion is that all the people of the empire should sing a
hymn of rejoicing at the same moment, the signal to
begin being sent by cable from London and repeated
along every telegraph wire in Her Majesty’s dominions.
The Bulletin doesn’t know enough about telegraphic
work to say whether the mechanical part of the scheme is
possible or not, but it knows for certain that a lot of Her
Majesty’s faithful subjects can’t sing, andalot more won’t
sing, because they will be away at the dog-fight, or bash-
ing each other with bottles down the harbor ; and quite a
large number will be too drunk to sing on this particular
occasion. Still, by way of doing its share, it has got the
hymn ready, and asks that the production be sung slowly
in a doleful key, as befits the solemnity of the occasion.
It is reasonably short and almost anv kind of tune will
fit

“I hate fashionable Victorians,” said a
character in one of Francis Adams’ Bulletin
stories, “ they are such snobs—they are worse
than English people. ” The Australasian, in its
notice of Rudyard Kipling’s “ Seven Seas ” vol.,
speaks of the capture of a battery “by a half-
drunken company of soldiers and a tipsy captain.”
* * * *
Sydney society is curious to know whether
a prospective benedict of Scotch descent, whose
wedding is to be a very tremendous affair, will
enter the marriage state in kilts. If so, he must
be very careful to mind the step, as the spectacle
of a kilted bridegroom coming to grief on a slack
carpet would shake any woman’s confidence in
him as a husband. Many a honeymoon has been
wrecked for less.
* * * *
Twenty-five per cent, of the employes of
Townsville firms are down with dengue fever. By
the way, what is the origin of the word dengue?
The writer much prefers the term “ breakbone ”
an eminently suitable descriptive appellation.
Dear Bulletin, —The
particularity of the Jews
about butchers’ meat may
have something to do with
their comparative immunity
from consumption, but there
is another reason. Tuber-
cular disease is the curse of
northern and western races
—the Hebrew is oriental and
(as regards the northern
hemisphere) southern. Pul-
monary consumption tak-
ing no account of exotic cases
—becomes steadily rarer as
you pass S.E. from, say,
England to Egypt ; and,
even amongst northern
peoples, the black-haired and
swarthy individuals (pre-
sumably with southern affini-
ties) are markedly more im-
mune than the fair, freckly,
and frigid.— Speculum.

week evening-service at St.
John’s, Latrobe-street. In-
cumbent Torrance officiated,
with about 20 surpliced choir-
boys, an unpleasant Phillips
memento just at this junc-
ture. The service was fully
choral, with a surpliced
organist. The congregation,
or rather audience, num-
bered eight! Two minutes’
walk took me to the Re-
demptorist Fathers, at St.
Francis’s, packed so that it
was impossible to squeeze in.
No particular music. No
sensation, beyond an Irish-
man giving a plain, un-
varnished narrative of the
Crucifixion in a conversa-
tional way. Why is this
thus ?
Miss BESSIE RIGNOLD, of the Frank Thornton Comedy Co. at

* » • *
One wealthy Australian Governor is achiev-
ing a reputation for meanness which is “more
than proverbial: it is damnable.” He is held bv
local cabbies especially, ■in withering contempt.
Two instances : one day he and his secretary took
a cab down town, drove to Government House
stayed there 15 minutes, and then—well, the job
in all extended over six miles, occupying quite an
hour - landed up half-a-
ero wn. “What’s this ?” said Jehu. » Your fare t”
replied the vice-rex. “But my regulation fare is
four-and-six ! persisted the cabby. Then the
rich man doled out another shilling with a grunt,
“There, that’s all you’ll get.” The same im-
ported satrap has conscientious scruples against
working his own carriage, etc., on Sundays, but
goes to church in a public vehicle. The other
Sabbath the “ party ” consisted of nine, and when
cabby delivered his load at the place of worship
he was rewarded with eighteen-pence ! He cursed
—even though it was Sunday.

N.S.W. Postmaster - General Cook, ex-
Labor leader, proposes to be the greatest groveller
of them all at Her Gracious’s “ diamond jubilee.”
He must needs issue a new set of postage-stamps
so that everybody can lickspittle royalty—behind
royalty’s back !
* ♦ * *
Despite all the fuss made about Butler in
’Frisco, and the trouble taken to bolt and bar him
on board the mail-boat, he will be treated at Dar-
linghurst as a very ordinary personage. The gaol
authorities will just, so they say, place him in the
“ committed yard ” amongst others awaiting trial
without giving themselves further trouble. Ac-
cording to the rule in the committed yard, thro"
men occupy each cell at night.

Some undermining Queenslanders have
sent their gracious sovereign a present of a large
consignment of ripe mangoes. The mango is that
deceiving yellow fruit with the brick in the centre,
that bursts and squirts juice in all directions when
you put your teeth in it, and hangs down in long,
moist strings, and makes you gummy all over,
even inside your clothes, and unfit for human
society. The natural result of sending mangoes
to Queen Victoria just now is that she will be too
sticky to celebrate anything next June, and won't
be able to see the ambassadors and premiers and
other potentates on account of the juice in her
hair and on her hands, and the stringy things in
her back tooth. Her Gracious is probably unac-
quainted with the mango, and the only way air

Sydney City Council, stony-broke and with
.£lOO,OOO overdraft at the bank, and an increasing
shortage, is going to spend J2lOO of the ratepayers’
money on an address to the Queen, on her 60th
anniversary. Said document will arrive along
with the other 19,900 similar grovels, and the
poor, tired old lady will simply be informed that
they have come to hand in bulk, and sundry
aggravated secretaries will acknowledge them and
make remarks about the pushing bumpkin aider-
men, who are the cause of all the trouble. And
then the mass of useless literature will be shovelled
into the imperial backyard, and called for by the
dustman.

A TATRIMONY. —Respectable Widow (46), anxious
-A*- for Home, correspond respectable eldy. widower.
Rebekah, Win.-st. P.O.
The modern idea of marriage : a Home ! The
pathos of it ! The dreary, lingering death of
girlish hopes, the crumbling away of life’s idols,
the long uphill struggle for a living, and the utter
breakdown of Hope ! My body and respectability
for a Home !
* * * *
Melb. : “ Miss Sutherland has broken
with the churches. She hires large halls in the
suburbs, on Sunday nights, makes speeches on her
work amid the child waifs, empties tabernacles,
and gets thumping collections. She wants to
know why the pastors talk about the Children of
Israel, thousands of years ago, while the Children
of Melb. are ruined in Chinese opium dens.”
Here are the facts, as stated by Miss Suther-
land at a public meeting, about a matter which
has been put endways in print. A few years
ago she rescued a street Arab who told her
of another. She followed up and caught this
boy, too, and then tracked out his mother in a
wretched Melb. opium den. She had stolen the
boy away from Ireland because he was entitled to
£13,000 and she to nothing. Miss Sutherland
established the boy’s claim to the money. He is
now being educated at a first-class R.C. college in
Ireland.
* » * »
Col. Smith, who now commands the
N.S.W. artillery - forces, “expresses himself as
opposed to the system of hiring horses.” It will
be remembered that the mokes which accompanied
the N.S.W. artillery to the Soudan were provided
by the Sydney ’Bus Co., and were utterly unused
to dragging guns. In fact, they were so inured to
pulling omnibuses that they obstinately refused to
move until the gun-driver had made a noise like
the banging of a ’bus-door. The doors of the co.’s
’busses never did bang, by the way, but that is a
mere detail.

The Melb. boat reaches Launceston in
time to catch the express, and enable Hobart
people to receive the Sydney and Melb. papers on
Saturday night, but the Tasmanian postal authori-
ties order the detention of the mails in Laun-
ceston until Monday morning—unless there is an
English mail, in which case they allow a Saturday
night delivery. Van Dobson Land is so English
that nothing Australian matters very much.

On the other hand, many women have benefited more
from cycling than they ever thought or hoped was
possible. But that the subject is a delicate one, and not

interesting domestic events that would not have happened
had the ladies concerned not taken to wheeling. That in
many households about Melbourne the bicycle has had to
be temporarily put aside for the go-cart is a matter for
congratulation to those in whom the maternal instinct is
strongly developed.

Young man knocks at door of house, which is
opened by young woman (unrelated to him in
any way) with infant in her arms. Young man
promptly knocks her and her infant down. Then
kicks her in the mouth. Subsequently seizes
billet of wood and smashes several windows. Is
afterwards arrested, and resists. Fined 10s. or
48 hours for the assault, ss. or 24 hours for the
wilful damage, and —20 s. or seven days for resist-
ing the police.

Queensland Oddfellows’ Lodges beat those
of N.S.W. in the varying scales of per head fees
they offer their medical doctors. For instance,
Isisford Lodge has offered at different periods up
to £‘S ss. per head of membership ; Toowoomba
and Rockhampton cut it down to £1 4s. Barcal-
dine carries it up to £1 195., and Longreach ad-
vances to .£2 2s. 6d. As you go inland, and dust,
flies, heat, and thirst increase, there is less medical
competition, and the sawbones can stick out for
higher rates.

Nowra (N.S.W.) professes profound con-
tempt for boroughs that do not pay their Mayors.
It lately fixed <£ls per annum as the salary of the
Mayor of Nowra.
Concerning mixed bathing, a Bulletin
correspondent sends word of a fleshly car-
nival which occurred in the South Melbourne
district during the small hours of a recent
fine night. He (the correspondent) seems
to have been taking a walk along the sad
sea-beach, with no companion to share his
horror, when he suddenly beheld a picnic-
party of twenty souls, and as many bodies,
gambolling in and out of the moonlit wave-
lets. The party was equally divided into
adult males and females,
an “ altogether ” cos-
tume being common
to them all. It was
just a case of mixed
bathing au naturel.
When the B.C. had
paused long enough
to draw comparisons
between the rival
sexes, and assure
himself that every
gentleman had
brought a lady, he
walked silently
away. Or, rather,
The Bulletin sup-
poses that he fled,
although he doesn’t
say so. The letter
leaves much more in-
formation to be de-
sired, on this and
various other points.

The Maoriland Wes-

One Melb. street is lit by kerosene, plain
gas, electric light, and Welsbach incandescent.
An epitome of everything since the “links.”
Mr. George Hill, who died last week after
a long illness, and whose portrait is printed in this
issue, was a generous backer in all classes of sport,
though he more than once earned the public
anger by his turf tactics, especially in relation
to that redoubtable horse Marvel. He was
a great friend to Larry Foley in the days
when Larry did not stand out for a thousand-
pound purse before tackling a man, and many a
time Foley has put down his tools of trade and
gone round to Captain Macdonald’s to “ try out ”
an aspirant for boxing honors, just for the sheer
love of the thing. George Hill found the money
for Foley’s historic fight with Abe Hicken, and
every shilling of it was handed over to Larry,
Jack Hall proved rather a bad pill for the
late George, as he backed Powell against
the clever cockney, and lost his money. Hall
was afterwards backed heavily against Peter
Newton by George Hill and the Thompsons, but
Jack deliberately lay down before the passe Peter,
and the clever Sydney division went down for
The late GEORGE HILL.
Photo. Brand.
once in their lives. Outside sport, George Hill
was as well-known in Sydney as Parkes or
Robertson, and the power exercised by him in the
political, municipal, and legal world of Sydney a
score or more years ago would read more like a
fairy story than fact if published in the present
day. George was second only to his father in his
humanity and liberality to the Botany Bay blacks,
over whose grounds he had tramped, fished, and
shot for the past half-century.

. -The Cretan Trouble. —The Eastern Question
is monopolising the interest of the whole world.
Round the island of Crete the Great Powers are
concentrated—so many lions all hungry for the
spoil, all fearing and mistrusting each other. A
touch, and they would all fall upon each other.
Savants say the lions must be blooded ; the im-
mense and ruinously costly armaments of Europe
are spoiling for a fight. The diplomacy exercised
in the trouble has brought affairs to a very
invidious issue. Fancy such a muddle as Crete
having autonomy under the supervision of the
Powers and at the same time remaining a portion
of the Turkish Empire! What a policy of Graft
all the diplomatists are pursuing ! It is only
Cameron’s Two Seas Tobacco that can preserve
peace in such a crisis.*
