A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ _ Sydney, April 13,1897.
Mt Dear Myee—
Red is so much the tint of fashion just now that
the Orotava, when around near Perim, endeavored
to give the modish hue to her cargo of finery by
having the Red Sea pumped into her forehold.
Among the deluged dresses was Hon. Margaret
Brand’s wedding-gown, which an unfeeling and dirty
deck-hose squirted along with many other bridal
garments now hung out to dry on the vice-regal
clothes-line. The dress was not irreparably
damaged, and will be worn at the ceremony next
Wednesday. Several high-toned leaders of fashion
had cases per same boat full of chiffon and feathers
now reduced to pulp. In fact, nearly every
woman in Sydney seems to have lost something
by the immersion. This excuse for last season’s
gown appearing at swagger functions this season
will probably be well-worn—“ You know, my dear,
all my imported things were in the Orotava.”
The “ Matsa ” mummies emerged from their
sarcophagi for the last time in Sydney on Satur-
day afternoon and evening. The afternoon resus-
citation was for the benefit of Florence Young,
who farewelled amid much enthusiasm and feeling.
As Clanside, in “In Town,” put on to give
Florence’s admirers a last sight of her in swallow-
tails and other things, she speechified a little on
her own, and choked audibly over saying good-
bye. “ You don’t know how much I feel saying
farewell to you all,” she faltered, and the audience,
standing up to get a last view, sniffed in sympathy
and regretted that Florence Young’s clear, fresh
voice will be heard no more on this side. Another
adieu was made by Knowles, and he also had to
say a few words. Likewise, at the evening per-
formance, he had some dry, up-and-down things
to say of The Bulletin —which, it would appear,
hasn’t gushed sufficiently over him. By the way,
in the press ballet’s final parade, The Bulletin
girl was the sole newspaper woman to get a hand ;
her smart black-and-pink has been applauded
right through the season.
Another final appearance on Saturday was that
of Juliet Wray, who settles down matrimonially
at Auckland, M.L., as Mrs. Sharman, after a
month with “ Matsa ” in Adelaide and a probable
trip to the West with the Co. She is to introduce
a turn into the third act in imitation of Knowles,
and will sing “ On the Benches ” in battered hat’
long coat, white trousers and lengthy boots. That
high-class ballad has caught on here far more than
in Melbourne, where there isn’t nearly so much
obvious flirtation as on the shores of our ’arbor.
Experts say that the oscillatory average here is
much higher per girl than it is down south.
At the first concert of the new season of Sydney
Amateur Orchestral Society, held on Wednesday
last week at the Centennial Hall, two original
works were presented to the musical followers of
Signor Hazon. A triumphal march, “ the Queen’s
Reign,” by E. de Beaupuis, was played to loud
applause. The composer was present and listened
to the frantic feelings evoked by his loyal outburst
without bowing his thanks from the platform. An
extract from a cantata “Hinemoa,” by a Maori-
land composer, introduced a real Maori song
played to a twittery string accompaniment. It
was characteristic, but was not so loudly applauded
as the brilliant march, because it didn’t end with
a riotous crash on the drums and cymbals like the
bang when the low comedian sits down on the
stage. That always fetches an audience. Alfred
Hill’s Maori music is interesting apart from in-
trinsic merit, as it is almost the first attempt to
preserve the beautiful native melodies of the
Pacific. J. C. Williamson is looking for a theme
for next Xmas. There’s an opera ready-made for
him in any South Sea village, with the pathetic
minor strains and wild original dances of the
islanders set to an accompaniment of the roar of
the surf on the coral reef and incessant rustling of
the palms.
The Cardinal, in blessing the Australian Fair
into existence last Wednesday, gave a very
modern turn to an old church miracle—the story
of how the votive gifts of some medieeval faithful
were turned into fragrant roses shedding sweet-
ness all around—by piously hoping that the
miracle would be repeated to-day and brought up
to date by the fair offerings being transmuted into
good hard cash. Roses are cheap ; but any prac-
tical-minded saint would make a large bid for
popularity to day by substituting for them the
yellow metal, and I suppose it would be just as
easy for him while he was about the transforma-
tion business. But heavenly intervention to-day
is superseded by the worldly, though effective,
raffle and race-game. The Fair is a vast surg-
ing crowd of buyers and sellers, principally
sellers ; before the show is over numerous
“Puritans” and Indians and “ Colleen Bawns ”
will be swapping raffles among themselves
to keep the thing going after the four days’
suspension of business during this week. The
stalls are crammed with the ordinary fairings—
multitudes of dolls, inscrutable and unuseable
articles made of crash towelling and twisted
string, cushions and chair-backs in loud plush
worked with aggressive patterns in positively
yelling colors ; also, many mirror-paintings. The
artistic merit of any exhibition is gauged by the
prevalence of looking-glass lilies and ducks among
rushes. There is much ecclesiastical lingerie
intricately needleworked, and some ecclesiastical
oil-paintings at Is. a member, and a signed photo,
of a royalty or two at a cheaper rate. And there
are some truly awe-inspiring landscapes ; it must
require heroic courage to put such pictures forth
as attractions. And one of the most thrilling
sights of the Fair is the wicked Mr. Tom Slattery
in a blue art-muslin bower raffling dolls—probably
by way of penance.
Convent needlework is famous, and that ex-
hibited at the Fair is quite exceptionally good.
Nuns are just the kind of people to have invented
plain sewing, a most elaborate form of torture for
highly-strung girls. After a whole afternoon’s
pricking hot little chubby fingers with a rebellious
and sticky needle, a chastened and meek spirit is
induced in the most fidgetty child. In these
blessed days of machines for'making everything—
even button-holes —it isn’t necessary to strain one’s
eyes and nerves over “ felling ” and “ seaming ”
and “gathering ” and “stroking.” German girls
spend half their time in sewing garments and
hoarding them in their “ wedding-chests,” and
the consequence is that the whole nation wears
spectacles. The modern child is revolting against
too much needlework, and even a nunnery has to
keep up with the times, so to attract pupils some
convents allow skirt-dancing and cricket.
The Arawatta Tennis Club entertained a town
hall full of people at Redfern last Thursday with
music and lime-lit tableaux. The first picture was
rather a gruesome subject, representing a pretty
girl lying on a bed as a corpse banked with white
blossoms, which the light refused to work on, and
the audience had its feelings harrowed in the
dark. The next, called “ A Mouse,” showed four
women on as many chairs, clutching skirts —no-
body played the title-rdle, by the way. Again the
lights failed after one brief glimpse of ankles.
“ Taken at a Disadvantage,” a well-arranged kiss-
ing scene, was the most successful of the series.
Monthly evening meetings at the Phillip-street
ladies’ club are blossoming into a regular institu-
tion. Last Monday the company politely and
silently simmered or suffocated while it listened
to a discourse on the original woman’s letter-
writer, Mme. de S4vign4, by M. Bulteau, entitled
“ Mons.” in the programme—which purely British
abbreviation of the French for “ Mister ” always
recalls Mons. Provo—he seems to be the only
legally-qualified “ Mons.” in Australia. The lec-
turer propounded the interesting, but not quite
correct, paradox that in France the best soldier,
the best statesman, the best scientist, and the
best journalist have all been women. Other items
were the charming piano solo of Mme. Kellermann,
and the ardent Italian warbling of the new bari-
tone, who was the lion of the evening for the
women present, while the neglected men scowled
about in doorways.
Owing to a desire to be in the movement, the
two women’s clubs of Melbourne and Sydney are
going to federate to the extent of conferring
honorary-membership on members of each club
visiting the other’s city for a limited time. The
local convention looks towards its elder sister for
much instruction and guidance in various little
matters of management—but it has not yet
plagiarised the brilliant notion of the Austral
Salon in having the afternoon meetings on a Mon-
day, by which worried members with houses to
keep may always be out on washing-day, and so
escape the steam and soapsuds, confusion and
cold-meat tea of the first day of the week.
Salonites are, of course, too intellectual to stay
home and help the overworked “ general ” through
with the pegging-out, and it would never occur to
them to hash the mutton and serve it hot for the
evening meal.
The alarm of fire raised at the Cleveland-st.
school-concert in Centennial Hall last week
showed what a serious thing a panic would be in a
building packed with about 5000 persons, mostly
children. Junior pupils as midshipmites were
marching by limelight when suddenly something
exploded, and everybody scrambled for the doors.
In a very short time the little boys resumed the
procession, by a wise direction the piano played,
and the reassured audience came back again—all
looking a trifle disappointed at the tame fizzle-out
to the scare. Some of the dances were done in
fancy costumes ; “ Ye Olde Englishe Gavotte,” by
remarkably well-grown “senior boys and girls,”
was encored, as the onlookers seemed anxious to
behold again the prance on the platform at the
beginning.
A deferred celebration of Bismarck’s birthday,
postponed on account of the death of the German
Consul, was held last week by a scanty band of
patriots. Judging by the attendance, the aged
statesman doesn’t appear to be overwhelmingly
popular with his countrymen out of the Father-
land ; they sat at little tables, drank Pilsener—
they drink Pilsener when it isn’t Bismarck’s birth-
day—and listened to a brass band. One travelled
guest, impressed by the continental look of the
place, raised his voice and called “Kellner”
haughtily, and seemed disillusionised when the
waiter answered “Orright, sir,” in good Aus-
tralian.
When a man pleads, like Craddock, that he has
committed some crime or made an egregious ass
of himself “all for the sake of a girl,” everybody
pityingly says “poor fellow,” and “let him off
lightly,” and other sympathising things, as if he
had made a valid excuse. But such a plea isn’t
an excuse, it is only a reason. Nobody thinks of
compassionating the woman in the case, who very
often would have to possess a cast-iron power of
resistance to ignore the temptation of making a
cheap and useful conquest. Some men seem to be
suffering all the time for some woman to become
their fair enslaver: they are just like the little
pigs who ran about with knives and forks stuck in
their backs, saying “ Come and eat me.”
The photographing of the Lamington baby on a
water-lily leaf has created a demand in Brisbane
for large tin-trays of the correct Victoria Regia
shape whereon the Nicest infants may sit and be
photoed like the northern Gov. ’s many-named son
and heir. Formerly it was the proper thing to
have baby “ taken” lolling on his back in a mam-
moth shell. The local Courier gives the episode
as stiff and stately a par. as if it were describing
a coronation at least, and names pompously those
who were present at the “interesting function.”
When De Kontski, the veteran pianist, was in
Maoriland, the Musin concert co. was touring
there also, and rows raged continually. Two
factions were enlisted, and the Musin backers
wrote to musical head-quarters to find out the
truth of the aged Chev.’s assertion that he had
been in his youth a piano-pupil of the great
Beethoven. The reply came from the Berlin cor-
respondent of London Musical Courier that
De Kontski’s claims were bogus, in that Beethoven
was stone-deaf ere the man now claiming to be his
only living pupil could have known him. The
marvel in the case is to find that any expert should
have taken De Kontski’s youthful reminiscences
as gospel.
A batch of ballads includes “Severed,’ by L.
Ronald; “’Tis June,” by Denza ; “Rose of the
Desert,” by F. Bevan—all nice in a drawing-room-
ballady way. A duet for violin and piano,
“ Characteristic Dance,” by E. Moor, is written
within amateur powers. W. F. Hutchinson’s
“ Grand Federation March ” gives the same sen-
sation as walking upstairs in the dark and count-
ing one step too many : you expect a foothold that
isn’t there.
The only point about the Lancers’ procession
through the city on Saturday last, differentiating
it from any other military procession, was the
dejected appearance of the horses, which a dis-
respectful public jibed freely.
Now that Florence Schmidt is discussed as a
coming prima-donna, some correspondents remind
me that red hair often goes with the musical
temperament. The reason is obvious —they have
plenty of iron in their composition to impart that
magnetism which enables an artist to get hold of
his or her public. Many women of genius have
brilliant hair, not necessarily red, but shining and
glossy ; it accompanies a type of face such as may
be seen in Gainsborough’s well-known portrait of
Mrs. Siddons ( not in Mrs. B. Potter’s pretty
photographed copy of the pose). Bernhardt’s face
shows the same characteristics; so does Duse’s,
the great Italian actress, though she is worn
by illness. There is in all the same clear-
ness of chiselling and delicacy about the nostrils
marking the artistic gift, the gift most fatal to
her own solid comfort ever bestowed on woman.
That most popular annual public dance, St.
Vincent’s Hospital ball, takes place on Wednes-
day (April 21) of next week and will, judging
from the ticket-sale, be very largely attended.
Among other innovations, a flashlight photograph
of the ball will be taken by Kerry, and all holders
of ball or gallery tickets will have a chance of
securing a 25-guinea bicycle, presented to the com-
mittee by the Waltham Co. Mrs, Douglas Mac-
kenzie and Miss Doyle are the hon. secretaries.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
( “ About the spiciest of Dr. Ryder’s addresses to
‘ hens only ’ is entitled ‘ Marriage—its Rights and
Abuses.’ Consequently, the crowd at Melbourne
Athenaeum Hall last week almost fought for the
privilege of paying nothing to go in. On this
occasion one felt no anxiety concerning the sex of
the person in the next seat. Certain stage pro-
perties boldly displayed over chair-backs (not to
mention a mysterious-looking indiarubber arrange-
ment like an elongated feeding-bottle which
dangled from a red screen) were calculated to
make any artful intruder of the wrong sex pull
down his veil and flee. Incidentally it may be
remarked that the garments recommended by the
learned doctress for feminine wear don’t lean
towards the aesthetic. A melancholy dame of
tough years acted as lay-figure, whilst various
combinations of wool and elastic were back
grounded by her velvet gown. These novelties
aroused very little enthusiasm. They are probably
excellent underwear from a medical point of view,
but they would seem to justify much violent abuse
of marriage by the husband of any woman who
gave them a show.
_ “ Austria, according to the lecturess, is an ‘all-
right ’ place for Women. Within the precincts of
that composite Empire no man can get a license
to marry until he has deposited a sum of money
(in proportion to his income) with the Govern-
ment. On the birth of every child a further sum
is demanded. No infants get baptised unless the
Government receipt for them is produced, so it
often happens that the neighbors club together
and loan the deposit on the first child, just by way
of encouragement. For non-compliance with the
law the hapless father is arrested, and kept at
work in the prison till he earns the necessary
amount. As time goes on the suffering parent
makes up his mind to commit suicide, and then
his widow and orphans scoop the pool.
“ Most of the women put their best ear forward
to catch the promised information ‘ How to be a
painless mother ! ’ The theory is that by much
exercise and a vegetable diet a woman can produce
such a, soft-boned baby that the whole proceeding is
apastime, and the curse bestowed upon Evebecomes
a dead letter. Osseous matter in the form of lime
water can be poured into the new arrival after
birth. This is briefly the American science of
Tokology, which, by the way, is strongly opposed
by many sections of the medical press in that land
of wonders. However, it sounds soothing.
“ Men, says the festive Ryder, are not naturally
of stronger sexuality than women. Education
and custom have developed it in the one sex and
suppressed it in the other, until the modern con-
dition of society is the unsatisfactory result. In
condemning the fashion of keeping girls in a state
of alleged innocence, Dr. Emily quoted the
opinions of a number of European celebrities, all
in favor of full revelations, save one man, who
said, ‘ Let the girls study the Old Testament, and
no more information can be given them!’ At
which the members of the W.C.T.IJ. simply
turned up their eyes and said ‘ Blasphemous 1 ’
“Finally, women were exhorted to revolt
against accidental additions,’ and the lecturess
told a somewhat pathetic story of a region where
the sex, being determined to avoid further acci-
dents, went on strike. Whether this happened in
a book or real life was not quite clear, but, any-
way, the bloated capitalists gave in promptly, and
rang the church-bells as a signal of submission.
One would like to know what happened in the case
of irreconcilable strikers who refused to resume
duty when the bell rang.
“An up-country military youth, named Purcell
seems to have turned all the Melb. officers green
with envy by his feats of horsemanship in the
barracks riding-school, during the recent tourna-
ment. The general verdict was that a first-class
circus-rider is not in it with Purcell, who hails
from Hay and is barely of age. His father was
very anxious to see him chosen for the team going
Ome, but, like many another anxious father, was
disappointed.
“ A Wesleyan church in a Northern suburb of
Melbourne has been closed for some weeks through
the inability of the pastor and the unwillingness
of his flock to pay the gas-bill. The poor man
frequently calls upon Heaven to lighten their dark-
ness, but Heaven seems to have no influence with
the company’s myrmidon who cut off the illumi-
nant at the meter.
“An event of the week in ’igh life was a five
days’ engagement; the principals in this brief love
story being a very wealthy orphan, living in St.
Hilda, and the son of a Melb. medico. The ring
was bought, worn for two days, and returned.
Congratulations still pour in, and the unfortunate
man sits by the telephone all day, making responses
to the friends who wish him joy. After three
minutes in Collins-street, at lunch-time, his right
hand is nearly as sore as his heart, so, to save
himself further pain and embarrassment, he has
‘ Explanations ’ published in pamphlet form, for
distribution around his circle of acquaintances.
“The sister to a well-known N.S.W. squatter
has changed her views of life within the past few
weeks. A while ago she returned to Sydney from
a doleful journey to London (and thence to Wies-
baden) with four first-class medical opinions that
she had lost the sight of one eye for ever, and was in
a fair way to become quite blind. A second course
of treatment in Sydney failed to improve the
lady’s outlook. Still hoping, but not expecting
much, she was presently induced to undergo
another operation, this time at the hands of a
Melb. oculist, who has a name for brilliant work
in unpromising cases. The result, last week, was
a joyful letter written by the patient to her
mother in Sydney, under the sole supervision of
the eye that had been blind when she left home.
“Visiting Westralians find life in Victoria fiat
and dull as ditchwater. One old lady, a highly-
educated woman, who went over to Fremantle
when the bad times came and started a hotel, is
back to Melb. for a health-trip. Now she longs to
get away to the fizz and fuss of the West. Hun-
dreds of strangers pour in by every boat, she says,
and ‘ you’re always wondering how you’re going' to
accommodate your share of the swarm. It’s more
like a big, jolly, tiring old picnic than anything
else, only you’re well paid for your fatigue ! ’
“Yet another Melb. girl has achieved a military
conquest in India. Miss Blanche Thompson, a
brilliant brunette, is shortly to marry Col. Holmes
in London. The colonel and major, also a captain
in the same regiment, have been captivated by
Melb. girls, the captain and major marrying two
sisters. ”
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

Dear Bulletin, —“ Gambler,” in your
last issue, says some “ things ” about the several
art-unions well-known to the public, and lays
himself out to especially “ worry ” the “ Druids’”
Art-Union and its management. Permit me to
point out a phase of the question unaccount-
ably (?) ignored by your correspondent. The point
of his comparison of these art-unions to Tatter-
sail’s lottery is lost in the fact that whereas the
latter is a matter of business entirely, conducted
by a gentleman for the benefit of himself and
those who are the lucky winners of the great
sweeps, the former primarily represent and
espouse a certain cause. The Druids, the A.N. A.,
the Hibernians, &c., are well-known Friendly
Societies throughout Australasia Bach vies with
the other in laudable offorts to advance a
particular society, and, in order to popularise and
advertise it, the method of annual demonstra-
tion is adopted. The art-union is tacked on
to it as the best means to afford sympathisers
in every part of Australia a chance to contri-
bute something towards this huge advertisement,
and the ever-increasing sale of tickets proves that
subscribers to these art-unions recognise that no
matter how remote their chance of winning a
prize their shilling is still not misspent. As to
the matter of expenses, if “Gambler” could in-
crease results with the same expenditure, or show
the same profit with less, or produce some patriots
who will do the managerial and secretarial work
for nothing, he will earn for himself the eternal
gratitude of his fellow gamblers, your humble ser-
vant included. JBy the way, the profit for chatty
of last year’s Druids’ Demonstration was .£1916
10s., not £640, as stated by “Gambler.” —Yours,
A. S. Gordon, Representative Druids’ Gala,
Sydney.
[The Bulletin fails to see that this is even an attempt
at an answer to “ Gambler s ” complaint. Said complaint
was that the public subscribed £7072, and received back
only £1333 in prizes, whereas “Tattersall” gives back
£6300 in prizes out of £7000. If the Druids, when they
advertise their next “gala,” will st’te openly in their
advertisements and on their tickets that thev give back
on an average less than 4s. in the £, and that about 12s.
in the £, or whatever it may be, goes in expenses and
salaries to official boss Druids, then the public will under-
stand the situation, and will know what kind of game it
is playing. Even according to the above Druid’s own
showing, the expenses swallowed a lot more than half the
subscriptions, so the corltenlion that the gala exists in
great part to supply comfortable billets for various people
looks justifiable. “ Expenses ” don’t help on any cause ;
they only help on the people who get the expenses.—
Ed. B. ]

One of the oldest and most bigotedly-bitter
adversaries of the drink-traffic in Australia draws
15 per cent, interest under a bill of sale over the
stock-in-trade of a city wine-cellar
Yic. Premier Turner gave up the bicycle
after a heroic attempt to conquer it. Unfor-
tunately, he tried to learn when suffering from a
severe cold, and every time he sneezed he fell off.
The scholarly C. H. Barton, who has, in
his day, edited both Maryborough (Q.) Chronicle
and Wide Bay News, has now seated his radical-
conservative self in the editorial chair of the local
Patriot !
Recent M.L. decease : Mrs. T. U. Cook,
wife of a well-known white settler. Mrs. Cook
(aged 81) was an aboriginal of high lineage—a
daughter of Te Horohau, of Otaki, the son of
Hapi, the great Arawa chief. She was also re-
lated to Te Rauparaha, of all-conquering fame, to
Thakara and to Kereopa. Cook, the relict, is 81.
Their family is a large one, and Bill Cook, a son,
is a printer.
Sylvanus Angus, the
patriarchal basso and oldest
member of Melbourne’s
Metropolitan Liedertafel,
died the other day without
getting the newspaper notice
that was fairly due to such
an ancient musical identity.
Angus’s reputation a a first-
class amateui singer dated
back to the Fifties, and had
prevented him, no doubt,
from developing into a capi-
talist. In his declining years
he still displayed the rem-
nants of a fine voice to the
best advantage, and the
shabbily - dressed old man
was a memorable feature of
many a smoke-concert. To
hear Angus—a thin, care-
worn, Moses-like figure, in
seedy coat and baggy trousers
—sing the solo, ‘ ‘ When Sor-
row Sleepeth,” to a hum-
ming accompaniment by the
Met. Liedertafel choir, was
to be impressed. The writer,
for one, will always think of
it- as a singularly effective
platform performance. The
sad song the venerable
warbler in the foreground—the melodious droning
of many voices from the rear. This was true
harmony.
Working in a Q. mine, in one gang, are a
once well-known police magistrate, a fully-degreed
solicitor, a bank manager, the son of a whilom
Supreme Court Judge, and a bank clerk. The
other men in the gang mostly spell cow with a k.
The remains of Miss Martin, sister of the
late N.S.W. Chief-Justice, were brought down
from Glen Innes last week and interred in the
family vault in the old R.C. cemetery at Parra-
matta. The father and mother of the dead C. J.
are in this vault, while his own dust lies in the
tomb of the Long family at Rand wick—the reason
of the separation in death being that “Jimmy
Martin did not die “friends” with the R.C.
church.

S. : “ What nonsense the
paragraphists get off about Pre-
mier Turner going back on his
Democratic habiliments since
receiving the Imperial brand!
I passed him in a Melb. street a
week or two ago. He wore a
creased grey sac-suit that had
evidently seen much service ; a
black boxer, certainly an old
friend ; and a tie that would
have sent O. Wilde into convul-
sions. Evidently George will
never deceive the world with
ornament, but, having lowered
himself to the ranks of the
K.C.M.G’s., he should adopt
the uniform.” All the same,
G.T. is reported from Ade-
laide to be sartorially as re-
splendent as all the other
K.C.M.G.’s, save in the matter
of “decorations.”
Whilst one of the Austra-
lias was in the middle of her
last election campaign the At-
torney-General was travelling
by train from the capital to
his constituency, accompanied
by his father and younger
brother. The compartment was
full, and the attentions of the
two relatives, who are inordi-
nately proud of their able kins-
man, were so marked that the
unfortunate Minister of the
Crown dared scarcely wink an
eyelid for fear of being made
ridiculous. At last, in an un-
guarded moment, he permitted
himself to raise his head and
rap out a terrific sneeze, but
scarcely had its echoes died
away before the aged progeni-
tor yelled hysterically, “ Shut
der vinder, Johnny, shut der
vinder; der Attorney-General
vill ketch cold.”

Queensland lecturer Buchanan, who goes
to England for a year, has signed on as parson at
Chalmers Presbyterian Church, Sydney, when the
lecture-tour is over. The ex-lawyer looks as far
ahead as a star actor.
Editor Finlayson, of the S.A. Register,
leaves Adelaide for England April 26. As he is
now on speaking-terms with Premier Kingston
again, it will not matter even if they travel by
the same boat, although, a few months ago, Great
Britain would not have been big enough to hold
both of them.

James Goldsburg, for many years driver
of the old mail-coach between Maitland and Wol-
lombi, and still a well-known identity of that dis-
trict, has inherited from a distant relative an ex-
tensive estate known as Tunrymo re, in the North

W. B. Dailey, whose statue in Hyde Park
will be unveiled next week, enjoyed his first Minis-
terial office—that of Solicitor - General—just 88
days. Which impressed him so badly that it was
sixteen years before he let himself be again drawn
into a Ministry.
Premier Kingston’s latest law-partner—
A. J. M‘Lachlan—-is the third in three years.
Although but a junior, he made a brilliant defence
in the Mann murder-case, tried at Adelaide last
year. He is Liberal in politics, and will probably
be in Parliament ere long.
Premier Turner has comparatively seldom
appeared in court as a speaker. He did not feel
his feet in that regard until plunged into the
waves of legislation. Wherefore prophets are
declaring that he will go to the Bar and ripen into
a good, common-sense Judge.
Chaffey Baker gave a garden-party to the
members of the Federal Election, but he omitted
to ask the President. The S.A. delegation is
indeed a happy family. Neither Symon nor
Baker will speak to Kingston, while Howe makes
no secret of his hatred for Cockburn.
W. T. Carter, ex-M.P., of Williamstown,
Vic., who was recently busy on a flying-machine,
has invented a torpedo which appears to be the
most expert and docile engine of destruction at
present in the market. At a recent trial Carter
put his highly-trained torpedo through some
remarkable pacings. At order it dived, floated,
went full-speed-ahead, back-pedalled, ran in
circles, turned somersaults, and rushed at a punt
moored in the distance, butting it amidships with
astonishing precision. After which it returned
for further orders. Carter, who, as a lay-preacher
and a ranting Methodist, is naturally a man of
blood, really appears to have contrived an engjpe
which should be of vast assistance to Providence
in all future efforts to uphold the “ Hempire.”
London Humanitarian al-
leges that the British merchant-
service contains i u nbers of
sailor-women disguised as sailor-
men. One Marie Herve, by the
way, who had worked as a she-
male farm-hand for 60 years, was
not detected until admission to
the hospital of Dinan, France,
the other day, at the age of 72,
to be treated for partial blindness.
It was the compulsory entrance-
bath that found her out. In
Carlton, Melb., for this last 20
years has worked a woman bis
cuit-machme maker, who swings
the sledge at the anvil and makes
parts as well as any male smith.
In 1888, the late “Johnny”
Bray, of South Australia, was re-
commended for a K.C.M.G.-ship
which, to the disgust of Gov.
Robinson, he declined to take.
Bray talked freely of his refusal,
contemptuously declaring that he
had no use for such rubbish.
Later on, the Pimperialists
hooked their fish, and “Johnny ”
was bebaubled. “ How was it,”
enquired a Sydney man of
“ Johnny’s ” colleague, Playford,
“ that Bray let him self be branded
after all ? ” “ Ah,” said the pon-
derous Honest Tom (so nick-
named after a famous S.A.
draught stallion), “it was the
women did it. Do you think Mrs.
Bray was always going to walk
into the Governor’s drawing-room
behind Lady Downer ? ”
“Queen Victoria visited
London for the first time in two
years and after ‘ bolding a draw-
ing room ’ fled to Windsor from
the city so distasteful to her,”
writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent. “She drove
through the thronged, carriage-
lined Hyde Park to the rattling
of nigger-slicers in the dish-cover
cuirasses, and the loud-sounding,
stony silence of the crowd. A
stunted, squat, old lady in dowdy
black, with clayish-grey hair, the
flush of indigestion on her face,
and nose particularly. She sat
in the open carriage looking ter-
ribly sulky, ignoring the hat-
liftings and bowings, and snap-
ping off bad-tempered comments
which her companions did not
appear to heed. Thus Victoria
Regina, 1897. God Save the
Queen ! The Empress Frederick
sat by the side of H.M., looking
matronly and queenly by com-
parison, and smiling complacently on all around.”

of their own before Melbourne had thought the
matter out—some of them ten years before.

A correspondent from the North
After seven years’ absence from Brisbane (Q.), I saw a
white-haired, slender, ancient and venerable gentleman
hailing a tramcar there from the “wrong side” of the
street. The driver didn’t see or hear him, and whipped
past with horses at best trot. Like a practised sprinter,
the ancient slithered after, with coat-tails flying as the
ground disappeared behind ’em, caught the tram in a
flash, after shaving the noses of some ‘bus-horses olatter-
ing by t’other way, leaped, while full pelt, upon the car,
and steadied himself like a practised tram-guard. Then J
caught sight of his face fully. Heavens 1 It was the in-
destructible old Methuselah, Dr. K. I. O'Doherty, quon-
dam M.P. in Q. and of Ireland! . . . And, by the way,
Dr. O’Doherty is patentee of a new anti-decomposition
substance, invented by himself. (Probably used on him-
self 7)
Coroner Youl, of Melb., having been asked
to narrate his most surprising experience, replied,
thoughtfully, with the air of a man who does not
expect to be believed : “ I onoe held an inquest on
a man who hung himself, and who had sixteen
sovereigns in his pocket, and had just been
divorced from his wife.”
Some Federal delegates, who are always
printed as “ Honorable ” in their own provinces,
hardly knew their illustrious names when they
appeared in the Adelaide papers behind plain Mr.
Some of the Van Dobson Land delegates—nearly
every Tas. politician is more or less “Honorable
within the province ” —are said to religiously avoid
any but their own specially-imported newspapers
while in Adelaide.
Melb. Met. Board of Works is a consider-
ably Irish institution. Among the colleagues of
White Knight Fitz-Gibbon (who married Mickey
Dawson’s daughter, begorra!) there are commis-
sioners Cody, Dillon, Fogarty, Moloney, O’Con-
nor, Gahan, Hennessey, and M‘Mahon. With
nine or more Irishmen on the Board, it is not sur-
prising that the Board’s office building is rented
from Paddy M'Caughan.
Apropos R.C. hierarchy, Archbishop
Vaughan was the only N.S. Wales prelate who
had a layman—Mr. Makinson, a ’vert—for private
secretary. Archbishop Pokling had an Italian
cleric, the handsome and courtly Dr. Colletti,
and Cardinal Moran has an Irish priest, Dr.
O’Haran. Archbishop Vaughan had a great
antipathy to Italian priests, and shifted them out
of his way whenever he could.

The law of supply and demand. Although
Sydney market is glutted with wheat and flour,
bread is still 3d. a loaf as in the days of the dearth.
Speaker Abbott (N.S.W.) has now been
a ‘ half y ears in that position. The record
£ll j , Speakership was that of Arnold, who
“lied the post for 10 years—’6s to 75.
One of the indications of the gradual
glacier-sliding going on in q. politics just now is
that the weekly paper which formerly boomed
Premier Nelson is barracking for Tozer with all
its might.
u Why not call the Upper Federal House the
tt Senate ” and the Lower one the “Corrobboree?”
House of Representatives” and “States As-
sembly ” are long and cumbersome and altogether
meaningless.
Australian: “ For my part I don’t like to
see the National Convention so starchy, trim and
smug. There has been a sad lack of fire and the
convincing. The wheels have been whizzing like
the knives of a cbaff-cutter, with no compressed
hay to out.”
Tas. Premier Braddon at the Convention
praised the Tas. franchise as sufficiently liberal
and remarked that one man might have as many
as twelve votes. “ Ah,” interjected Reid, “ that
is liberal enough.” Then Peacock, and all bar
Braddon, yelled.
The Vic. Old-Age Pensions Committee is
slumberously getting into harness. A State-con-
trolled system of all-round pensions is not in-
tended, but the “ more urgent ” cases of pauperism
may, the commissioners think, be fixed ud at a
cost of .£lOO,OOO.
Almost every day since the Convention
began its sittings it has been bombarded with
petitions that it shall “recognise God” in the
new j Constitution. If some arrangement could be
made for God recognising the Convention it would
be a great deal more to the point. The Almighty
will get on quite as well without the Convention
recognising Him, but if He doesn’t recognise the
Convention it is liable to be stepped on and flat-
tened out at any moment.
Wanliss, M.L.C., of
Ballarat, is about to pe-
tition Her Gracious about
the use of the word
“ England ” as a syno-
nym for Great Britain,
to the scandal of Welsh,
Irish, and Scotch “ sub-
jects. ”
Holder’s contention
that an Autralian Act-
ing - Governorship is not
a royal appointment—a
contention objected to
by his fellow-members—
would seem, if examined,
to be strictly sound, at
all events since Chief-
Justices by right of office
assume the position. The
Government which makes
the Chief Justice makes
thereby the Acting-Go-
vernor, and, as it can
theoretically dismiss a
Chief-Justice, it there-
fore possesses the same
power over an Acting-
Governor
It seems to be agreed
so far that the Lower
House of the Federal
Parliament is to be called
the House of Representa-
tives and the other the
States Assembly. Both
names are long and
clumsy, and the idea of
calling the Upper House
the “Assembly,” which
name has hitherto been
always used for the popu-
lar Chamber, makes con-
fusion. Why not simply
call them the Federal
Assembly and the Fede-
ral Senate ? Then a man
would be a senator instead
of an M.S.A., and the
prospective dreary joke
about these three letters
meaning that things were Muddled So Awfully
would be strangled in its infancy.
Lyne told the smaller states at Adelaide
that if N.S.W. and Vic. decided to federate, the
“ little ” provinces would have to come in. Which
was characterised by Gordon as “injudicious and
harmless bounce.” • -
Only three territories accepted the U.S.
Constitution Act when it came from its framers’
hands. The others held back or denounced it.
.Now, close on 50 states shelter under it. Why
should not N.S.W., Victoria, and S. Australia
make a beginning ?
Two prominent members of the Federal
Convention are Jews. Therefore the question
arises : Suppose the Convention decides to “ re-
cogm.se Cod,” as the soi-disant Christian parsoni-
cal crowd loves to phrase it, what arrangements
are to be made for recognising Abraham ?
Convention Delegate Dobson, ex-Premier
of Tasmania, would restrict the franchise for the
xederal Senate to persons over 25 years of age
who have sown their wild oats, and have left off
gomg to the theatres, and who have begun to read
the papers. ’ The Hobart papers, presumably.
When Dibbs went to England and left
Barton as his deputy, Premier Reid declared
that there could be no such functionary as an
Acting-Premier he was wholly unconstitutional
and impossible and non-existent. The proposed
appointment of Brunker as Acting-Premier dur-
mg Reid s absence in England on grovel now
looks a trifle awkward.
The way in which the Federation politicians
stick up, each for his own province, as if its
boundaries were eternally delimited, reminds
one of how a back-century pope divided the whole
unknown globe, by the aid of a holy ruler, it may
be supposed, and a sanctified pencil, between
bpain and Portugal. What are Spain and Portu-
gal now m the “general proportion? ”
The A.N.A. in Vic. is smiled upon by the
“•p 1 Church, because it has no secret ritual, etc.
hence a certain dominant tone therein (remarks
a Bulletin correspondent). The “ Tammany ”
metropolitan committee is run by a compact
band of smart young men. It is a beautiful thing
to attend a “ Tammany ” meeting, and watch the
majority voting like clockwork, while a few
oppositionists look on gasping.
“Toby” Barton’s denunciation of the
Referendum as a relic of ancient barbarism handed
down from the cave-dwellers staggered the Con-
wasn’t prepared for such aggrava
ted Toryism. It was a Referendum vote, by the
way, which appointed Barton to his present posi-
tion by the biggest suffrage in Australia, so, by his
own showing, “ Toby ” is now the boss barbarian
and principal elective troglodyte of this country.
The word “ Christian ” serves a lot of
purposes, as witness some recent cablegrams from
Crete :
A horrible massacre of Moslems has occurred at Deph-
nes, where 160 took refuge in a mosque where they were
afterwards burnt alive by the Christians.
Twenty Moslem women sought refuge in a cave at
uephnes, and the Christian mob endeavored to suffocate
them by setting Are to paraffin.
A “ Christian,” in the latter-day sense of the term,
is evidently a person who is known by the com
busted Mahommedan he leaves in his wake.
BIKEY HUMOB.
RUDE BOY (to old Mr. Johnson, who has bought a toy bicycle for his
grandson) : “ Hey, mister, have yer had a spill?”
Farmer Copley, the derelict leader of S.A.
Opposition, will be 52 on April 25. A 30-year old
scandal concerning him was resurrected" in the
Council by a political opponent last year, and he
has never been able to regain the prestige then
lost. It is hard on a middle-aged public man that
the escapades of his boyhood should be revived -to
damn him, but the other side had been so busy
with Premier Kingston’s social reputation that
poor Copley was pilloried in sheer revenge, al-
though for more than a quarter of a century he
has lived a blameless private life. Altogether, he
is better than most of the men on his side, although
he is a renegade in politics, for he began his par-
liamentary career in 1884 as a stalwart Liberal
and a true friend of the producer.
No recognition of Gaud wanted in the
Australian Federal Constitution.
The British House of Commons has passed
the second reading of a bill to establish a criminal
court of appeal. The miserable “mother country”
ahead again !

ANIMAL TEACHES ANIMAL.
“The association styled ‘ the National League ” held its annual
meeting at 274.Collins-st., Melbourne, last week. Mr. F. Madden,
M.L.A., said that the farmer was ‘a slow-going animal,” and made
references to breeches-pockets. Suggestions were made that lec-
tures should be given in the suburbs. Then someone interposed
with an interjection that the country should be ‘ educated ’ in the
belief that the capitalist was ‘the farmer’s best friend, not his
enemy.’”— Daily News Item.
Dear Bulletin, — You say that Turner and
Duffy will leave Vic. Ministry, and that Attorney-
General Isaacs will become Premier. More likely
that Isaacs will be on the Supreme Court Bench
ere the year is out. Hasn't Justice Williams al-
ready announced that he contemplates retiring on
his pension and taking up his abode in England?
The resignation would probably be tendered
straight away if his Honor could find a cash
buyer now for his fine suburban mansion and
grounds.—B.
“Native ” to The Bulletin :
Why disgrace the wattle by referring belligerent
Irishers to its blended green and gold ? Besides, the
wattle doesn’t bloom at the right time of the year. On
St. Patrick’s Day the most glorious combination of the
colors is found in the autumnal foliage of the willows,
poplars, and other imported trees and shrubs which im-
ported men have planted with their hideous old feuds in
Australasia. But, short of planting the chief actors,
nothing will cure the trouble. Meantime, despite the
jeers of Australians, the green and yellow factionists will
continue to act the variegated goat.
The Governor-General of Australia’s salary
is likely to stay fixed at £lO,OOO. Two State
Governors, Barkly and Hotham, have received
<£15,000 a year. Victorian Governors until lately
drew <£lo,ooo a year. Acting-Governor Darley,
in the interregnum between Gov. Duff’s death and
the sailing of Gov. Hampden from England was
paid at the rate (for the two offices) of <£10,500 per
annum, and from the time Gov. Hampden sailed
till he was sworn in, at the rate of £7OOO per
annum. These are the highest salaries Austral-
asia has paid under that head.
So far as things have gone the Federal
Constitution steers clear of one very bad rock on
which the United States struck. It provides
that the Constitution may be amended by a vote
of both Houses endorsed by the referendum. In
the States an amendment has to be agreed to by
a two-thirds vote of both Houses of Congress
and by three - fourths of the State Legisla-
tures, consequently no anomaly, however bad” can
be remedied if it is to the interest of a few tin-pot
States to prevent it. The U.S. Constitution-
makers started with the idea that their Constitu-
tion was practically perfect, and laid themselves
out to make alterations as nearly impossible as
might be, and their descendants have been more
or less sorry for it ever since.
. Q. Premier Nelson’s boast to an Adelaide
press-interviewer that the little brown men of
Japan cannot be imported into Leperland without
the shipper giving a 10,000 dollar guarantee that
they shall not become a burden on the Q. Govern-
ment, is little eo the point. A 1,000,000 dollar
guarantee wouldn’t matter. The question is a
wages one. The cheap Asiatic coolie, of whatever
territory, knocks over the white man’s chances
steals his necessaries of life, distils misery into his
existence, fattens only the already fat, and emaci-
ates the already lean, brings hell and hatred into
the ordinary relationship between employer and
employe, and makes men dream murder and
firestick by night and by day. The cheap and
nasty nigger and brownie, and yellowie, must be
walled out of Federated Australia.
Hardly a month passes without a case of
remission of sentence by the Vic. Governor-in-
Council. Sometimes, too, as in the Staples case
under circumstances unjustifiable and disgraceful’
Once more The Bulletin maintains that every
case of the kind should be publicly notified in the
Government Gazette.
Melh. : “I have just
had occasion to go to the
house of a democratic legis-
lator, and it is melancholy.
He receives visitors with a
kind of state—a state of litter
—in his dingy front room,
amid a dusty rubbish of Han-
sards, newspapers, pam-
phlets, and what not. Hig
wife and family look to ms
not far removed from squalor.
Altogether anunkemptnome.
It don’t pay. It is a reign
of terror—lest the member
should be sacked, as help-
lass as a dismissed police-
man. ”
“We have great advan-
tages over our opponents,”
says Westralian Premier For-
rest, in connection with the
alleged election campaign
(for three - fourths of the
people are disfranchised).
"We have a record behind
us.” Rather. Other things
too. The railways, tele-
graph-wires, and post-offices.
Forrest’s dreary waste of
jumbled sentences, by cour-
tesy called a speech, contain-
ing nearly 22,000 words, was
(writes a Bulletin corres-
pondent) first telegraphed
from Bunbury free of cost
and then distributed by post
and rail all over the pro-
vince, for there, as in N.S.
Wales, the Government pro-
tects the newspapers by
free-postage. Not satisfied
with this, the country lias
to pay for a supplement re-
print with Forrest’s correc-
tions. Issue something like
15,000. Three clerks in the
Premier’s department have
ever since been engaged fold-
ing and enveloping these sup-
plements in Govt, stationery
for transmission, at the coun-
try’s expense, through the
post to every butcher, baker,
and candlestick-maker whose
name appears on the Wes-
tralian directory.
Significant that Q. Home Sec. Tozer should
say that, “by July, whatever party is in power”
would bijing in a Federal Enabling Bill. “ What-
ever party is in power ! ” A sli-p of the tongue, a
sly hint, or a mere Tozerism ?
As one very natural result of leaving the
railways under provincial control, S.A. kicks
violently against Federal postal and telegraph
management. It wants to know why it should
lump its profitable postal system with the utterly
bankrupt ones of N.S.W. and Tasmania, if N.S.W.
won’t combine its small railway deficit with S.A.’g
large one.


Homely illustration of the superiority of
union over federation offered by a parson at” the
recent Melb. Wesleyan conference :
You talk of federation instead of union. Well, suppose
a young man had been engaged for 25 years, and said to
the young lady, “ I love you, but I’m too poor to marry
you—we’ll federate.”
The assembled parsons were mightily pleased with
this, of course—the cloth likes a joke if it is
sufficiently Biblical.
“ TASSY ” SCORES ONE !
We nave just received the following Marra
Creek, Brewarrina, N.S.W., 24th March, 1897.
To the Tasmanian Eucalyptus Oil Co., Hobart.
Dear Sirs, Enclosed please find a postal note for
•I s Will you kindly forward me a bottle
of your “Platypus” Eucalyptus Oil. It will,
doubtless, seem curious to you that I should send
over 1150 miles for this, but, as far as my observa-
tion goes, the art of making this commodity pro-
perly has not reached this colony. The stuff which
I have used and met with here, and which goes by
the name of eucalyptus, I can only describe by
the one word, ‘rubbish.’ Having frequently used
yours and knowing its value is my reason for send-
ing this order to you. Yours truly, .”
‘Platypus Eucalyptus Oil, which our customer
refers to, is to be had in Sydney of all chemists,
but purchasers should only buy original bottles
with our trade-mark (a platypus) on the labels.
Messrs. Elliott Bros., Ltd., of O’Connell-street,
Sydney, are the wholesale agents. The Tasmanian
Eucalyptus Oil Company, Limited, Hobart and
London. #
The record Victorian bullock to date was a
beast bred by Mr. Power near Maffra, Gippsland
—Bft. high at shoulder ; weight, 29|cwt.—some 12
years ago. Paul de Castella, of Yering, obtained,
when John Sayle was his manager, the highest
price recorded to date for a mob of fats—9o head
bringing an average of =£29 10s., after having been
bought six months before for =£4 ss. a head. But,
then, that was in the roaring days of ’54.
Mr. Charles Oertel, of Sydney, manu-
factures a “ raspberry-juice syrup”—obviously
prepared (as per analyst’s certificate) from pure
fruit without chemical aid. Being the only such
article pushed in the market it should have a
great and immediate success.*
March 22 was the 49th anniversary of
Scotch settlement in Otago, M.L. The first ship
to arrive was the John Wickliffe, which brought
el «’#J ants ’ ' nc hiding Capt. Cargill, nicknamed
the Moses ”of the settlement. Another ship
set out a day before the John Wickliffe, but
voyaged so slowly that pioneer meenister Burns
was prevented from being present to open the ter-
ritory-grab with prayer.
The case of two men who were charged at
a Melb. court, a while ago, with trespassing on
somebody’s back verandah in the middle of the
night, and who explained that they had come to
steal apples from the apple-treeless garden, has
borne fruit already. The other day, another two
men, surprised in a stable-loft at East Melbourne,
made the excuse that they were looking for grapes.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of GfiOft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
The bosses of a Presbyterian church at
Clifton Hill, Vic., wanting a clergyman, offered
a week, the devil-dodger to find his own manse
and pay all his own expenses. It was mentioned
as an inducement to ambitious “ meenisters ” that
there “is not a single pawnshop in the whole
neighbourhood.” Very foolish this —at such a
wage the poor parson would often need to spout
the family Bible.

’' FRON, formerly of New Kay, Clare County, Ire-

In the Zulu War.
A STORY OF BUSH AND BATTLE.
THE TALE OF AN OLD CAMPAIGNER.
BESIEGED IN A MISSION EKOWE—
FEEDING ON WEEVIL* AND OXEN
-ON THE VERGE OF A GRUESOME
GRAVE.
He was a bronzed and bearded soldier—an old
warrior of the Zulu campaign—and had much of
interest to tell.
His name is Mr. R. Groves, of Hyde Park,
Rook wood, and the representative of the Star
found him one morning in his well-kept orchard,
spending the evening of a stirring life amidst his
fruit trees, reading his paper in the coolness of a
shady porch, that, covered with blossoming bou-
gainvillea, rose as a splendid splash of purple and
green in contrast to the brilliant sepia of pounded
'clay around.
I’M FIFTY-FIVE YEARS OF AGE,”
he said, making room for us beside him, “ and
when I left my regiment, the 3rd Buffs, I held the
rank of quartermaster-sergeant.”
“ And the Zulu campaign ? ”
“Ah, yes! I was through that,” he replied,
shaking his head at the recollection. “ The cam-
paign of ’79. Yes, that’s what knocked me up.”
1 ‘ How was that ? ”
“Well, you see, our column was in six detach-
ments, and Cetewayo’s impis
MASSACRED FIVE OF THEM.
Ours was the sixth, and we made for a deserted
mission station ekowe, and kept the dusky troops
at bay. They couldn’t carry our building, and we
held out till help arrived.”
“ You must have had an awful time? ”
“Awful! My God, it was. We were herded
together there for 13 weeks without ever being
able to get a proper sleep. We had no bedding,
and the air was filled with heat and stench. The
only food saved was weevily biscuits, and then we
had to kill the half starved trek oxen to help keep
life together. Should ever we dare to show our-
selves for a minute to snatch a breath of air
AN ASSEGAI WOULD WHIRL
THROUGH SPACE,
and the man was dead that it struck.”
“ Did this conclude your perils ? ”
“ No, not by a long way. For fourteen months
we had nothing to sleep upon, and had to lie down
in the open air—in the dry heat and the cold,
pouring rain—it was all the same. Fever and
sickness carried off many of the men, and when
our tattered remnant reached the coast I left the
regiment, having served my time.”
“ What effect had the campaign upon you ? ”
“A terrible effect, that till last Christmas I was
never without. As a result of the exposure and
starvation I found my digestion ruined. For
seventeen years I’ve had twitching pains under
the shoulders and in the arms ; the nerves of my
arms were weak, and caused me agony whenever I
moved them. I suffered from cramps, and at
times
A DEATHLY NUMBNESS PERVADED
MY BODY.”
“ How about the doctors ? ”
“Ah ! well, I tried them,” he replied in a tone
tinged with contempt, “ and they never did me
any good. Some said it was one thing and some
another ; others just said it was general weakness,
and couldn’t or wouldn’t state what they meant.”
“ So you never progressed ? ”
“No, not a bit. I gradually got worse, and, of
course, I lost interest in everything. I could never
walk far, I never slept properly, and I hardly ever
ate anything except dry toast and tea. I was
miserable and restless, and always in pain.”
“ How long did this last ? ”
“ Till last December. Several people who saw
how bad I looked said they thought
I WAS GOING TO DIE.
And I felt just like it, and a doctor I consulted
told me the same thing. I was in a state of col-
lapse then. Even ordinary conversation would
take all my breath away, and I’d sink back ex-
hausted on the sofa. A lady doctor told me it
was all through my digestive organs, and that’s
what it was. ”
“ Well, what did you try next? ”
“ That’s the point. I picked up a pamphlet one
day in December thjit a man had thrown into the
house. It was an advertisement for Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People, and I there read a case
just like my own that had been cured.”
“ What did you do ? ”
“ Why, the only sensible thing. I got some of
the pills.”
“ Did you try ’em ? ”
“ I should think I did.”
“Well, you don’t seem very dead now,” we
commented, looking at the grey-bearded face and
sturdy military figure of our friend.
“ No, and
I DON’T FEEL VERY DEAD
either, young man. I feel very much alive. Why,
do you know,” he said impressively, “that I’ve
increased twelve pounds in weight since I took
those pills of Dr. Williams’.”
“ Have you ? ”
“Yes, I have. I improved gradually from the
first dose. First, I took a pill at a time, and later
on increased the dose, lip to now I’ve taken
about thirteen boxes, and I feel so well that [
intend to discontinue them altogether. As it is,
I haven’t had any for over a week, and I feel first-
class. ”
“ Have you a good appetite now ? ”
“Rather,” he laughed out. “ I’m always ready
for my meals now,
AND SLEEP LIKE A TOP.
And when I wake up I feel like a man instead of
the weary wreck that I was three months ago.”
“ How about the pains ? ”
“Oh ! they’ve all left me. I haven’t felt them
at all for a couple of months.”
“ I suppose you walk better now? ”
“ Yes. Before I took Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
I’d have to rest two or three times just walking
into Rookwood. Now Igo straight in at a good
pace, and only yesterday I carried parcels home—
a thing that I couldn’t have possibly done before,”
and the old soldier’s eyes twinkled at the humor
of the idea. “ I had to knock my cold baths off,
too,” he said, “ because I was getting so weak, but
now I take ’em regular every morning. My
digestive organs are in perfect order, and all the
functions of my body do their duty.”
“And I’m only doing mine,” he added, “in
telling you this.”
“ Do you feel as well as you did before the war?”
“Yes. I honestly feel better to-day
THAN I DID TWENTY YEARS AGO,
and that’s saying a good deal, as I’m getting an
old man now.”
“You firmly believe in Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, then?”
“ Believe in the pills ?” he queried. “ I should
think I do. They’ve done a hundred times more
for me than all the doctors and medicines I’ve seen
and taken for nigh on eighteen years. That’s
saying a good lot, but it’s true. Believe in the
pills ? I should think I did.”
Then we shook hands with this gay old veteran
and bade him “ Good-bye,” and wished him a long
and happy life.
As we went up the garden walk, “I say,” he
called after us, “ look at this.” We turned round,
and found him vigorously going through a number
of army extension movements with his arms.
Then he held his legs straight, and bending his
body touched the ground easily with either hand.
“ I couldn’t do that before,” he said.
We closed the gate and left him there amidst
his fruit trees and his blossoming vines.
It is not only in cases like those described above,
however, that Dr. Williams’ Pills are so valuable.
They have cured paralysis, locomotor ataxy, rheu-
matism, and sciatica; also all diseases arising from
impoverishment of the blood, scrofula, rickets,
chronic erysipelas, consumption of the bowels and
lungs, anaemia, pale and sallow complexion,
general muscular weakness, loss of appetite,
influenza’s evil after effects, palpitations, pains in
the back, nervous headache, early decay, all forms
of female weakness, and hysteria. These pills are
a tonic, not a purgative. They are genuine only
with the full name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, and are sold by chemists and store-
keepers generally or the Dr. Williams Medicine
Coy., Queen’s Place, Sydney, will forward on
receipt of stamps or post order one box for 2s 9d,
or half-dozen for 14s 9d.*

In our advertising columns will be found
the announcement of some of the largest trade
exhibitions and markets in the world, which
are annually held at the Royal Agricultural Hall,
London, England. It will be seen that there is
to be a Papermakers’, Printers’ and Fancy Goods
Exhibition from June 23rd to 30th ; and an Exhi-
bition and Market for Hardware, Machinery, In-
ventions, Electrical Appliances, China, Glass, &0.,
from August 2nd to 9th. The “Confectioners’
and Bakers’ Exhibition ” takes place from Sep-
tember 16th to 23rd ; the Grocery, Provision,
Oil, and Italian Warehouse Traders’ Exhibition,’’’
September3oth to October7th; and the “Brewers’
Maltsters’, Mineral Water Manufacturers’, &c.,
Exhibition and Market ’’ from October 30th to
November sth. The Agricultural Hall is the
largest building of its kind in England, and the
volume of commercial transactions effected during
the progress of such important organisations as
these is always phenomenal.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
If Newhaven be as brilliant, or nearly as bril-
liant, a horse as Carbine of old, he could (in the present
writer’s opinion) have won the Sydney Cup, given good
condition and fair luck. Three-vear-old Carbine, with
9st. up, carried off the race in 1889, from Melos and
Abercorn, and must have had a lot of weight in hand to
enable him to do the trick. Carbine was giving 121 b. to
Melos, who appeared to be winning easily until Donald
Wallace’s champion, getting a clear run, came along like
a steam-engine and snatched the prize. At present,
when there is no Melos among the second rank of three-
year-olds, or Abercorn among the older ones, the 9st. 51b.
allotted, to Newhaven is certainly not a “crushing
weight.” “ Crushing ”is a comparative term, considering
that the best “ form ” of one season may be 2st. better or
worse than another’s. Judged by the 151 b. plus a
ten lengths’ beating, which Newhaven gave to Resolute
in last Melb. Cup race, the mighty chestnut was positively
“thrown into” the handicap at Randwick. Now that
absentee Cooper has chosen to scratch Newhaven, the
most likely of the three-year-olds is Reka, and he or
Damien (thinks the present writer) should win Sydney
Cup. There isn’t much merit to be discovered in Reka's
past performances, but one has to allow for special proba-
bility of improvement in the half-brother to Portsea.
Anyway, on the Melb. Cup running, Reka—who finished
fifth—is more favorably handicapped than Coil, Damien,
or even Resolute.
• * » *
Punters will do well to be doubtful about New-
haven for his first race at Randwick until they have seen
him take his preliminary canter. There are two New-
havens—the ridiculously easy Derby winner, and the
animal that has been twice disgraced by The Officer. The
Newhaven that begins to sweat before reaching the post
is the wrong ’un.
* * * »
Victor : To-day (Sat.) the A. J.C. Autumn meet-
ing begins at Randwick. The St. Leger looks a gift for
Newhaven, whose only dangerous opponent seems to be
the much-improved Carbine colt, Charge. O.P. or Irish
Stew may win the Hurdles, whilst Delaware and Vivian,
in the probable absence of Newhaven, should battle for
victory in the Autumn Stakes. The valuable Doncaster
Handicap will bring out a good field of “ triers.” If
Vanitas be reserved for the Cup—a probable contingency
—Adoration, CrSme d’Or, Precaution, Pilatus, and Dal-
meny read like a quintet from which the winner may
spring. Adoration’s win at Hawkesbury meeting last
Saturday was full of merit, and “on paper” she looks a
good thing. Danger is to be feared from the lightly-
weighted Pilatus, who ran two good races at Flemington
(Vic.) a fortnight ago, in one of which he showed well
against the sprinting wonder, Key.
* * * *
The champion two-year-old, Aurum, should
comfortably win the Champagne Stakes on Monday ; and
even if opposed by Newhaven in the All-Aged Stakes
he should be returned winner of that race also.
• * * *
The most noticeable feature in the Hawkesbury
club’s day’s racing last Saturday was the defeat of the hot
favorite, Hopscotch, in the big handicap. The “ sensa-
tional ” gelding will have to show the now common
miraculous “improvement” if he is to be returned a
winner at Randwick next week. Mr. S. Hordern won
two races with Adoration (Nordenfeldt-Idolatry) and
Maldon (Nordenfeldt-Idolatry) both popular fancies.
• * * •
With the exception of Newhaven, all the best
three-year-olds will compete in Sydney Cup next Mon-
day. The Officer, Coil, Charge and Reka read best of
this class, whilst of the older horses Vivian, Battalion,
Hopscotch and The Meddler look most formidable. Good
three-year-olds invariably run well in this race, and if
Charge can beat Coil he should win. The best outsider is
that beautifully-bred mare The Meddler (Trenton-Busy-
body) nicely treated with 7st. 81b.
* * • •
Owners are complaining about the present
dearth of capable jockeys : most of the really efficient
ones are either “ standing down ” or have left the country.
For skilful horsemen who will go straight there is a
splendid opening now both in N.S.W. and Vic.
* * * •
Last year’s trotting-stallion prize at Sydney
Agricultural Show was annexed by a pacer. In America,
the home of the trotter, the gaits are held distinct, and if
the Sydney Agricultural Show people decide to allow the
two to compete, the competition should be advertised for
“ trotters and pacers.” To style the trotting exhibitions
for “speed, style, and action” is a farce, seeing that the
ring round which they are tried for “speed” is a bit
bigger than a good-sized soup-plate. Handsome little
pacer Tuxedo moved round in taking style last year, but
the owners of such majestic animals as Era and poor old
Tommy, since deceased, were not mad enough to allow
their valuable sires to climb all over themselves round the
good-sized soup-plate aforesaid. The competition should
be for “ conformation, action, and breeding.”
Considering that the V.R.C. has been liberally
charged with indifference to the welfare of deadheads, it
seems only fair to quote from a punter’s wail (in Melb.
’Erald) re the accommodation at small race-meetings :
“ It is scarcely credible, but nevertheless a fact, that any
person visiting the flat at Flemington is treated with
more courtesy than the persons who pay 2s. 6d. at
suburban registered courses. There (Flemington) they
can see the names of the riders, the sanitary arrangements
are not disgraceful, and in every other way their con-
dition is preferable.” That’s rather “up against” the
grumbling flatite, who wants a grand-stand gratis from
which to pass unfavorable remarks on the Melb. Cup.
• * * »
Tremolo, who ran third in the Steeplechase at
Flemington the other day, is a regular giant in appear-
ance, as he touches the standard at 18 hands —perhaps
the highest animal at the game in the world.
* * * *
At last El wick (Tas.) meeting, a wordy conflict
took place between the urbane clerk of the tote and an
excited person who had been celebrating a win, and now
came to draw “ the stuff.” By a pardonable mistake,
considering the circumstances, he went to the wrong
window—the receiving teller’s, as it were, instead of the
paying ditto. Here the courtly politeness of the clerk
only served to add fuel to his wrath, and after much
unseemly wrangling he expressed his intention of coming
inside and punching the face of the ever-urbane.
Answered the suave clerk : “ Pray don’t trouble yourself;
if it is any convenience to you I will put it outside for
you.”
Dear Bulletin,—A stickler for the well of
Maori pure and undefiled, the official spelling of the
name of M‘Sweeney’s Clarendon Handicap winner jars on
me. There is not such a word in the language as
“ Waihine”—“ wahine ” (meaning wife) being the ap-
parent intention. “ Hine ” is the Maori radix of
femininity, hence Ohine, a maiden ; Ohinetapu, the sacred
maid ; Ohineroa, the stately maid, and Ohinemure, “ the
girl I left behind me.”—Yours, Timi.
* * * *
In Tasmania recently a horse bolted with a
little girl barely in her teens, galloped some miles, cleared
a sft. railway-gate like a bird, and stood trembling
in front of the second facing it on the other side of the
rails. The child hung on like a postage-stamp, and her
name was—of all names—Corrigan.
Tha editor of London Referee, noticing Dai-
mio s failure (thus far) in England, suggests a reason for
Ebor’s present superiority to the other Australian ’chaser.
“ I do not maintain that Daimio necessarily must be a
nailer here, because he was educated for a business very
different from ours. I only say this is a horse who has
proved that he would have made a great mark put to any
jumping game you like. Whether he can be taught to
forget much that he learned ‘ down under,’ and adapt
himself to the altered conditions we offer, is another ques-
tion. The horse that can get over Flemington course can
be made to jump anything if apprenticed to that sort of
business. But now we have a journeyman, so to speak—
one who has served his time and become a master man.
For years he has been doing things in one way. To effect
them in different fashion a lot has to be unlearned, and
maybe he dees not make the new start quite on such
good terms as the likely novice.” Ebor was a likely
novice at “crosscountry” racing in England, and he has
taken kindly to the new business. Daimio never proved
himself a fast horse over a jumping course—not in the
Redleap sense of the term—and it now seems probable
that an Australian jumper must be trained to fly over the
small sticks, as Raleigh he is to cut a great figure
as an English steeplechaser in his middle-age.
• • « »
A lady bookie blossomed forth at a Danevirke
(M.L.) meeting recently, and did good biz. Further
north, a gaudy silk-and-satin Maori dame tried the game,
made £5 on the day, and got taken down for the lot later
by a spieler, while learning the intricacies of the classic
three-card trick.
* » * *
M.L. bookies are now fighting the race-clubs
tooth and nail, and mean to have it settle once for all
whether power exists to eject them from courses. Three
actions are pending against clubs for damages for eject-
ment.
• • • *
A return of 60 “ walers ” from Calcutta to Mel-
bourne is a unique experience in the Indian horse-trade.
The horses were “ wheelers” designed for artillery work,
not cavalry remounts.
* * * »
All the Australian daily papers bristle with ex-
pensive cables to the effect that one Bevan got second
place for high-jumping at certain university sports in
England. Ye gods ! But then this particular Bevan is
son of good old wax-dolly pastor Bevan, and it is probably
considered sufficiently astonishing to find a parson’s son
willing to take the trouble to jump.
* ♦ * *
Latest advices say that Stoddart’s team for
Australia next season will include Ranjitsinhji, F. S.
Jackson, Capt. Wynyard, M'Laren, Mason, Lilley, Hay-
ward, Hayman, Richardson, Peel, Bray (as second wicket-
keeper), and possibly Lohmann. All these batsmen are
noted for their dashing play, while on paper the team is
decidedly better than the last visiting XI. Ranji. has at
last definitely decided to make the voyage, a fact which
will delight all Australian cricket enthusiasts.
♦ * * *
Lacrosse promises to hit football still harder in
Adelaide this season. The former game is being taken up
at the colleges—the only football recruiting grounds
available last year. There are 17 lacrosse teams this year
as against about 12 in ’96, and already the Adelaide Oval
authorities are nibbling at lacrosse fixtures.
* * * *
Rockhampton turned out, the other day, to wit-
ness its periodical “ladies’ cricket match.” The men
played with the left hand and the orthodox pick-handle
bat; the ladies had the use of right hands, skirts, feet,
and umpires. After most grotesque cricket on both
sides, the horrid men won by four runs. As a dress-
makers’ parade, the show was very passable—and the
Lady Norman hospital will benefit to the extent of £l2O.
• • * *
An extenuating circumstance connected with
the latest big Sydney Sunday-morning dog-fight (400 per-
sons present) on Kogarah beach, is that one of the Sab-
bath-breaking pups represented St. Peter’s.
» » » *
Last Saturday’s bike meeting at St. Kilda
C.C. ground gave the club further encouragement to im-
prove its accommodation for sightseers and become the
Flemington of wheel-racing. Given a fine afternoon and
a lively programme, the multitude will always roll up to
the St. Kilda track. This time the attraction of Bilmartin
was guaranteed by the management, and an advertise-
ment to that effect probably accounted for 20 per cent, of
the biggest crowd ever seen in Melbourne at a show which
had no pretentious “championship” or big handicap
item on the bill.
Divine William, without a doubt, is the centre
of human interest in Australian bike sport. Exagge-
rated stories re Bill’s ailment and the consequent
agony he endures whilst occupying that small saddle
whereon it pleaseth Providence to place him, have
raised the Plugger in public estimation. Thb Bulletin is
even disposed to pay him the tribute of a silent tear. On
Saturday the indomitable artist rode four races, cover-
ing ten miles in all, with no richer result than a third
prize of 3 sovs. But if he didn’t actually get there he was
in each case thereabouts. Although beaten, Bill was not
disgraced, and small boys took chewing-gum from their
mouths in order to express appreciation of his exertions.
When will Australia have a native-born biker
of the plump and trustworthy kind—like Martin and
Porta ? The least uncertain of the natives seems to be
Walne (he rode with a lovely black eye on Saturday), who
beat Megson in one-mile and five-mile scratch races, and
was himself beaten by Porta over the shorter distance.
Should Walne ever quite get to the top of the tree he will
stay there a while, as Martin did. Megson is no sooner a
champion than somebody takes his number down, where-
fore Joe has no worshippers outside of Sydney. The
Melbourne public can’t take his flashes of supremacy
seriously, seeing that he ranks as a non-local man. They
have not yet recovered from the awful collapse of Parsons,
whom they took very seriously last year, despite the palp-
able fact that he never was supreme at any point of the
game.
Kellow’s persistent failure in scratch races
doesn’t harmonise with the handicapped form of that
fair-haired young man. At last Austral meeting Kellow
appeared to have a more effective kick than Walne, but
since then the Northerner has been much the faster man
in classic events. For which matter, Kellow’s brilliant
Austral victory, or rather the utter defeat of the front-
mark men on that occasion, begins to look like a fluke.
Bilmartin was blocked, clearly enough, in the
final of the Open Handicap, but his protest was rather
absurd, considering that Prowse and others got blocked
likewise. Anyway, the old, original Bill would not have
let himself be “pocketed” among a crowd of second-
raters after catching his field a mile from home. Martin
at his best would have rushed to the front somehow, and
spread-eagled them before the bell rang. As it was, he
couldn’t even push himself into a place.
J. M‘lnnes races under the immediate super-
vision of a stern parent, who gives the lad his shove-off.
MTnnes, junior, white-faced and anxious, would probably
do better as a rule if the “ old man” were not present.
The system of engaging a competitor to make
pace in the mile scratch race—the pacer of the fastest
heat to have a chance in the final—works very well. The
fastest go on Saturday was the fifth heat (‘2min. 15sec.),
the slowest taking only ssec. more.
Multicycle races give the handicappers a chance
to make some absurd blunders. At St. Kilda, on Satur-
day, a moderate triplet, starting from the 120yds. mark,
made a holy show of the tandems, that had to give them
ground, and eventually won easily from two pairs of light-
weights. When the limit-men go hard all the way, it will
need a precious smart tandem to win from scratch, seem-
ingly. Relph and Walne captured the League’s tandem
race on the previous Saturday, simply because the long-
start men took things too easily at the outset.
* * * »
Though a programme immeasurably inferior to
that of the League was presented, the one day St. Kilda
meeting netted £l3o—while the governing body with two
days and two nights on the Exhibition Oval was able bo
scrape together a profit of only £7O more. The public
patronises the best track.
A glaring breach of the rules was committed
when Beeson swung out young M'lnnes as the lad was
passing him 100 yards from home. M‘lnnes swerved and
almost touched the fence, taking others with him and
sickening the spectators with the prospect of a tragedy.
For this error of judgment Beeson, who won, was relieved
of the trouble of taking the £l2, L. Barker, who finished
second, being awarded the prize.
Farrell, the boy who ran into third place in the
Open Handicap, is classed as third-rate, but his sprints
even now can live with the best of them. He is a mere
hoy, but thick-set, and evidently could stand a lot of
training. If he gets into the way of following the early
pace of a race Farrell’s class should narrow very quickly.
Tebbutt has contracted influenza, and will race
no more this season.
Walne, who has the ardent support of nearly all
the female portion of Melb. bike audiences, owed the
black - eye above - mentioned to a little affair with
the possessor of “a dirty left,’’ in St. Kilda dressing-
room on Friday evening. The dispute originated
over the merits of the handicapping, and the Queens-
lander, though beaten from the first blow, showed splen-
did pluck, and came up six times from knock-down blows
before receiving his quietus. However, the collision
didn t seem to affect Walne’s riding much, considering
he "ext day put down Megson and Martin for the
£25 Five-Mile Scratch.
Porta, Walne and Megson, finishing in the final
of the Mile, were locked together for the last 50yds. It
was a grand race, and though neither the Queenslander
nor the Sydney man could gain an inch from the time
they sprinted, the result, was unknown to the majority of
the spectators until the numbers w'ent up.
A party of Melb. cyclists took Green and
Edwards rabbit-shooting last week, and by way of en-
couraging the Englishmen, jokingly asked the somewhat
celebrated firm of Melb. gunsmiths, who were fitting out
the show, to fill the visitors’ pouches with blank car-
tridges. I can tdo that,” said one of tho gentlemen
who compose the firm, raising his hands in virtuous
horror. “ Vy, look at our reppitations ! ”
Bilmartin announces his speedy departure to
Europe to reach Italy in time for the cycling season. It is
obvious that Bill badly needs a rest, and, as the Australian
season shuts up shortly, he sees that he might just as well
take that rest on board ship, and arrive recuperated to
battle for further dols. When Martin reached Vic. in ’95
he was alleged to have cleared £l5OO in the Italian season,
just closed, but his faithful blue serge suit belied the
allegation, and no definite information re the Italian
winnings has since been forthcoming.
When Porta won his first race in Australia, he
was quite surprised to receive a cheque the following
Monday. In Italy, he explained, the successful competi-
tors have to chase the sports-promoters with stilettoes in
order to get the stakes.
After his win in the 50-mile race, Megson’s leg
swelled so badly that he reckons he will take a couple of
months to resume his old form.
* • » •
Tasmania has lately begun road-racing largely.
Races on two consecutive Saturdays brought 33 and 41
before the starter. So far, the handicapping is weird.
In the first race (14 miles) one man was given 28min. i.e.
2min. in every mile, and he naturally romped home. ’ On
Queen s Birthday there will be a race from Launceston to
Hobart (130 miles), when, perhaps, 70 riders will be on the
road.
* * * *
Porta writes to contradict the statement that he
couldn t get his prize-money from the Westralian biking
clubs. On the contrary, the hardworking Italiano is so
well satisfied with everything Westralian, and so anxious
not to hurt anybody’s feelings, that he would seem to
have another foreign raid in view.
