* * ♦ *
If S.A. record-breakers have been having a bad
time lately. Snell, the cyclist, who went from Adelaide
to Melb. overland without sleep, is now laid up with
typhoid complicated by pneumonia, an indirect result of
the journey. Another S.A. cyclist lately started out for
Port Darwin overland, but collapsed at Oodnadatta, the
head of the railway line.
» * * *
A unique institution of Calcutta is an open
court-yard where the Hindoo sports meet to gamble on
the weather changes. The “books” lay odds against it
raining within a fixed period, and sometimes as much as
70 to 1 may be got against the occurrence of a shower
within a couple of hours. There are also professional
weather tipsters who, perched on high towers, eagerly
scan the horizon for the appearance of rain clouds, and
by secret signs convey the intelligence to their clients in
the court-yard below.

A lunatic has been found near Omeo, Vic.,
who moves about like a dog and snarls and snaps
at anything that comes near him. For long prior
to his attack he had charge of a fierce dingo at a
bush pub. He is now in the asylum at Kew.
Hydrophobia, or what ?
The W.A., Vic., and Tas. tens at the
Federal Convention get paid at the rate of <£3 3s.
per sitting for their services, and this is the
reason why it was arranged that the Convention
should meet each morningforaminuteortwoduring
the time the sub-committees were at work on the
details of the Constitution. If there was no meet-
ing of the Convention as a whole, there would be
no pay. The S.A. delegates get £2 2s. per day
from the date of their election to the dissolution
of the Convention.
Another leper has bobbed up serenely at
Toowoomba, Q. —where Premier Nelson lives
with his family when he isn’t record-reign junket-
ing. The victim is a Chinese gardener, and he is
described as “in an advanced stage.” An “ad-
vanced stage ” probably means that eight fingers
out of ten have been dropped into the vegetable-
baskets to eke out bundles of “ladish” and
“callot.” Ugh!

cession was sorrowful in this regard, there being
no ladies. Now had they been Chinkies, yellow

peppered the vehicles from the good missions,
where they teach AB Ab. Yet Ghollah and
Chunder, our esteemed fellow English, are in-
finitely finer men than oleaginous little orange-
mugged Hung Fat and Cow Bung.”

On Town Hill station, in the Mitchell dis-
trict, statistics of three years show that out of 37
oattle shot for cancer 22 were white, 13 light roan,
and two only were dark roan—which means that
35 figrfet-colored cattle are cancerous there for every
two dark oancerous beasts. But, when “ lumpy -
jaw ” cases of shooting come in, out of 24 shot
only four were white, and eight were red, whereas
12 dark roans were bulleted. Which points to
blonde cattle being specially cancerous, and
brunettes specially “ lumpy-jawed.” Statistics
from other stations would be interesting.
From a Perth (W.A.) builder : “Every-
thing dear except drapery. Fruit not obtainable
except from 6d. to Is. per lb. Potatoes, 2s. 3d. for
141 b. Bread, lOd. per cottage-loaf. Meat, Bd.
per lb. Vic. butter, Is. 6d. per lb. No food-in-
spectbrs. Sand, dirt, flies, fleas, mosquitoes, bugs
of all kinds, and ten times out of nine the worst
kind. Water bad, and only ‘on ’ about lOmin. in
24 hours. Rent of six-roomed house, £2 10s. a
week. Add to all this, business slack. General
movement hell-wards in search of something more
nearly akin to Paradise.” .
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in-
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-knownopticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection—
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
A number of rev. business men who run
shops with pews in them waited upon railway-
manager Mathieson in Melbourne, the other day,
to raise a chorus of howls over the running of
Sunday trains, but when their complaint was de-
finitely stated it was found to arise from the cir-
cumstance that the trains were timed to arrive
either after church was in, or too late to enable
people to rush round and be in time for the collec-
tion. It is a favorite superstition amongst parsons
that people are perfectly wild to go to church
most of the time, and they think the universe
ought to be run on special lines to provide men
with every facility for getting to the temple early
and often ; and yet it is a melancholy fact that the
parson spends his time during business hours de-
livering stirring exhortations to empty benches.
Commissioner Mathieson could not alter all the
trains, and it did not occur to him to advise the
clergy to run their churches in accordance with
the railway time-table, which strikes The Bulle-
tin as the reasonable course.

Wanted a name. The Argus doesn’t
desire the parties which must arise in the Federal
Parliament to adopt the American names, “De-
mocrat” and “Republican”—the latter is too awful
and too suggestive. It doesn’t want “Demo-
crat” and “Tory” or “Conservative,” for it
doesn’t admit that it is Tory. It objects to being
called the Fossil-party or the Stomach-party.
Yet when an all-Australian Parliament gets
together it will almost certainly shake down into
the usual two parties—the party of Progress and
the party of Stagnation, and a party needs a name
just as much as a dog does. Also, there will be
another division between the men who want to
continuously strengthen the union and those who
want to uphold the States against the union.
The party with the most brainy leader will set up
first as the advocate of Federalism and try to
shove the other by the natural cussedness of poli-
tics into supporting Provincialism. The best
thing the Progressive crowd can do when the
Commonwealth Parliament meets is to label itself
“National” right off, and announce itself as
the party of union, and let the Argus crowd
scramble for a name and take the principles that
are left after the Nationals have selected their lot.
Mr. Fisher, of Messrs. Fisher and Ling-
ham, has just returned from America, where he
was astonished at the ever-growing popularity of
the Smith-Premier typewriter, The factory at
New York is one of the largest, handsomest, and
best-appointed in the world, and the Smith-:
Premier typewriter itself is going everywhere—
among the snow-banks of the North and the cane
and cotton fields of the South ; where the ther-
mometer is 30° below zero, and where it averages
80° at noon.*
London Pelican (20 Feb.) joins the bri-
gade of London crows which live by picking the
eyes out of this paper. It reprints as its own,
under a somewhat altered title, Constance Clyde’s
forceful episode, “ Wansbury’s Second Wife,”
which originally appeared in The Bulletin’s last
Xmas number.

A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfahne Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
ill Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
riell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of I estimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders ar- : ve
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Public Notices.
-pUBLIO SERVICE BOARD.
60 Young-street, Sydney,
9th April, 1897.
The Public Service Board hereby notifv.that the next
COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION for JUNIOR CLERK-
SHIPS in the PUBLIC SERVICE of the colony will be
held on the 28th, 29th, and 30th days of JUNE* next, at
various centres, which will be fixed to suit the conveni-
ence of candidates. The estimated number of appoint-
ments to be made during the year is 50. Candidates must
be over sixteen and under twenty-one years of age on the
first day of examination, be of good moral character, in
sound health, and have resided in the colony for at least
three years at the date of application.
Every person who desires to submit himself to examin-
ation must forward to the Secretary to the Board, to
reach him not later than the 7th June, 1897, a statement,
in his own handwriting, giving his full name and address,
and his age, and notifying in which of the principal towns
of the colony he wishes to be examined. He must also
submit, with his application, an extract from the register
in which his birth is recorded, or other satisfactory evi-
dence of his age ; a certificate to the effect that he is of
good moral character, signed by his teacher, if he is at-
tending school, or has ceased to attend school for a shorter
period than six months ; by a clergyman or Justice of the
Peace or other responsible person if it is more than six
months since he left school; and a certificate from a repu-
table person to the effect that the applicant has resided
in the colony for at least three years.
A list of registered candidates will be advertised, with
the times and places of examination; and, in addition,
every candidate registered will be personally notified of the
time and place when and where he must present himself.
Every candidate whose name is not registered by the
Board will be so informed, with the reason why registra-
tion is refused.
The subjects in which candidates will be examined are
—Handwriting, English (dictation, grammar, and com-
position), Arithmetic (full course), Geometry (Euclid,
First Book), Outlines of Physical and Political Geography,
and the Geography of the Australasian Colonies in
moderate detail, English History from the middle of the
Eighteenth Century to the present time, especially with
reference to the colonies and dependencies. Candidates
will also be allowed to take any two, but not more than
two, of the following subjects Mathematics, English—a
prescribed author—(the same as that prescribed for
the Junior Examination of the current year), Latin,
French, German, Elementary Chemistry, and Elementary
Geology. Every candidate who wishes to be examined in
the extra subjects must give notice of his desire in his
application.
The fee for examination will be 10s., which must be paid
to a Clerk of Petty Sessions or into the Treasury, and the
receipt lodged with the Secretary of the Board 10 days
before the examination. Every successful candidate must
produce a medical certificate to the effect that he is in
sound health, and free from physical defects, before he will
be allowed to enter upon his duties.
The regulations governing the examinations appeared
in a supplement to the Governmbnt Gazbtt* dated Ist
April. 1807.
By order of the Board,

Tbe Royal Baok of Australia Limited.
HEAD OEEICE : MELBOURNE, VICTORIA.
EIGHTEENTH REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting 1 on 21st April, 1897.
The directors have pleasure in submitting herewith balance-sheet and statement of accounts to Slst March, which are
made out in the form prescribed by the new “ Companies Act, ’ and are duly audited and certified ; the net profits,
after defraying all costs in connection with the reduction of capital, amount to £3398 9s. 6d., out of which the direc-
tors recommend the payment of a dividend at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum, which will absorb £2250, and leave
a balance of £ll4B 9s. sd. to be carried forward. The directors are pleased to be able to announce that the resolution
to reduce the Bank’s capital, which was carried unanimously at the special meetings held in February, has been con-
firmed by the Court, and duly registered. The Bank being now free from the incubus that has hampered it for so
long, the directors can, with perfect confidence, appeal to the shareholders to assist the institution in every possible
way by opening current or deposit accounts, or by introducing good mercantile business. The liquid business is
sound and profitable, and, with the help of shareholders, could be largely extended. Shareholders are requested to
forward their scrip to the Bank, in order that it may be exchanged for fresh scrip, in accordance with the altered
capital. By order of the Board, H. T. WILSON, Manager.
AUDITORS’ REPORT. To the Shareholders of the Royal Bank of Australia Limited. Ladies and Gentlemen, —
We report that we have audited the accounts of your Bank, for the half-year ending 31st March, 1897, and that, so far
as we are in a position to form an opinion, the balance-sheet and accounts have been drawn up in accordance with the
provisions of the “ Companies Acts,” and present a correct view of the state of the company’s affairs. —THOMAS
KIDD, F.S.A.A., ARTHUR W. CLEVELAND, F.1.A.V., Auditors. Melbourne, 7th April, 1897.
STATEMENT OF THE BANKING COMPANY STYLED THE ROYAL BANK OF AUSTRALIA LIMITED.
For the 31st day of March, 1897.
I> Hugh Thurburn Wilson, manager of the Royal Bank of Australia Limited, do solemnly and sincerely declare •
That the liability of the members is limited ; that the capital of the company is £2,400,000, divided into 600,000 shares
of four pounds each, of which one pound per share, amounting in all to £150,000, is actually paid up in money • that
the number of shares issued is 150,000, all of which are contributing ; that calls to the amount of one pound per ’share
have been made, amounting to £150,000, under which the sum of £150,000 has been actually and bona-fide received in
money.

Melb. : “I think a sensible outcome of
*97 will be the Victoria Hospital. Book Arcadian
Cole and others have imagined the scheme, as the
best chimney-shaft for the exuberance that is to
be realised in some form of subscribing money.
The site will be Latrobe-street, Melb., and the
institution is proposed for women and children
under female-doctor management. There is a
good deal of animus against “ the Anglican toffy
tabbies ” who boss the existing Women’s Hospital.
Charles Hall, committed for trial at Ben-
digo (Vic.), is charged with a somewhat novel
development in wife-murder. Mrs. Hall was
found in her night-dress, kneeling beside a tub
with her head under water, and quite dead. The
case for the defence is that she was washing her-
self and fell into the water in a fit, and was
drowned, or else that she suicided, or else that it
is siihply a mystery. The prosecution alleges that
Mrs. Hall never had fits ; that Hall had the usual
reason for wanting to get rid of his wife (the usual
reason, in such cases, mostly has golden hair and
very small, shiny boots), and that he held her
head under water until she was drowned. It was
Hall who first gave the alarm about his wife’s
death ; he didn’t take her out of the tub, bub
simply ran for assistance. The defence alleges that
he had just come home and found her dead ; the
prosecution claims that he had just finished hold-
ing her head under water when he started call-
ing for help. Bendigo, or rather the adjacent
little town of Eaglehawk, mostly regards Hall as
guilty—any town would, under the circumstances
—for the sufficient reason that, if he isn’t guilty,
a really picturesque horror will be spoilt. What
it is especially proud of is the mark of the rim of
the tub, which some theorists claim was caused by
Hall sitting on his wife to keep her steady while
he shoved her head under. This is the sort of
artistic touch which has hanged many a man ; it
isn’t in human nature to readily let go such a
really first-class bit of melodrama as the ac-
cused sitting on his wife to keep) her from
jumping while he drowned her head in a bucket.
Judging by the police-court proceedings it is
evident that the case will turn, in part'at least, on
the question whether there were soap and towel
in the room. The Crown lawyers persistently
enquired about these accessories, the idea being
evidently that, if there were no soap and towel,
Mrs. Hall was not in the act of washing herself
when the alleged fit came on her. This will pro-
bably open up a new question. Suppose it was a
very small bit of soap and it had dropped into the
water and dissolved unnoticed. And suppose the
lady had forgotten about the towel. One witness
was even asked if there were soap, towel, and
flannel in the room. Is everybody supposed to
wash with a flannel ?
Quaint item of evidence in re the Hall
mystery at Eaglehawk (Vic.): “I saw an experi-
ment tried with a Mrs. Morton, who was about
the same size as Mrs. Hall, with the object of
seeing whether the position of deceased was a
natural one.” The question at issue was whether
Mrs. Hall had been kneeling before a tub of water
when she fainted and her head dropped in, or
whether some one sat on her and shoved her head
in. Apparently the ladies of the locality have
since then been experimenting with each other,
and possibly sitting on each other in front of tubs
of water, to see how the attitude works.
A tale of suffering almost on a parallel
with the old-time Pearce horror comes from Tas-
mania. Two prospectors, Joseph Davies and
William Beech, left Hobart on Jan 7th last for
Port Davey with six months’ provisions. Arrived
at their destination, the fishing smack which
brought them left, and they stored their provisions
in a hut erected for the purpose. On 20th, return-
ing from a prospecting trip inland, they found the
hut and all provisions burnt; only a few rotten
potatoes remained. There was nothing left but to
make for Strahan. They lived on snakes, crabs,
dead fish and anything else they could find, and
finally they had to eat their dog. Their track lay
mostly along the beach. Heavy rains set in and,
on March 4th, while crossing the Mainwaring
river, Beech was drowned. Davies continued
alone and reached Macquarie Heads pilot station
terribly exhausted and with his feet in a pitiable
condition. It had taken him 54 days to travel 80
miles —as the crowflies. Extract from diary: “Had
scalded dog’s skin and feet for dinner to-day.”


Torquay (Eng.) Gazette, Dec. 9 :
“Every child in Australia who is not supported by
parents becomes a ward of the State, and is paid a pen-
sion for support, and placed in a private family, where
board and clothes are provided until the fourteenth birth-
day. After that he may be able to go to work, in which
case the pension is placed to his credit until the age of
18, when he becomes a citizen, with a balance due to him
from the State to begin life with. This inculcates a
humane, charitable, and responsible spirit in all residents,
decreases the chance of pauperism, and places every
young man on a fair and square footing with the world.”
This record bears the imprint of truth, but it
omits to say that these wards of State have a daily
allowance of gardenias, and shirt-collars of alarm-
ing altitude, chucked in. They have also the
entree to all theatres, and the very nicest society
on the nod, with the pick of the finest girls at all
balls, picnics, &e. —to say nothing of free passes
over all railways, and the privilege of getting all
drinks at half-price.
“ John Bull is a mixture of lion, mule, and
octopus.”-—Max O’Rell.
Whoever wants a good, reliable liquor
that will never go back on him should drink Alex.
Ferguson’s famous P. and O. whisky. The places
where it can always be procured are Beach’s Cafe
Francais, and Watson’s Paragon Hotel. *
A Dutchman, the other day, got work as a
miner in W. A. and was shown into one of the
drives. Soon after his advent, one man said there
must be bad air in the place, another said it was
bad dynamite, and a third thought there must be
something dead about. Presently, however, they
began to eye the new-comer’s bag with suspicion.
“What have you got there, Dutchy?” one en-
quired. “ Dot is my lonch,” said Dutchy. On
being opened, it was found to contain a lump of
rotten cheese. “ But,” said its owner, apolo-
getically, “dotvas not to eat; only to schmell
und give an abbetide.”

When the Shearers Go A way .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Tis lonely on the station when the plains are wet
and wide,
’Tis lonely when the plains are dry and brown,
You can’t be very cheerful when the saddle burns
your bide,
And ditto when the floods are coming down.
We don’t feel over-pleasant when the final load of
wool
Is swallowed up in dust and distance gray ;
It wouldn’t do to show it, but our pannikins are
full
Of sorrow when the shearers go away.
The shed is like a graveyard, and but yesterday
the din
Was deafening—we heard it from afar;
We heard the woolpress rattle as we rushed the
stragglers in,
And shearers shouting “wool away” and
“ tar ” ;
The yarder hustling sheep about and yelping
like a hound—
How earnestly that yarder used to pray
And curse, and make his pack-roll on the woolly
backs resound !
We miss him since the shearers went away.
The silence seems to settle ’mongst the rafters
once again,
Forgotten in the days of work and song ;
And strange, fantastic shadows that we cannot
quite explain,
Depress us like the memory of wrong.
No slush-lamps meet the twilight, the galley fire
is dead ;
No shouts from lusty throats, nor laughter gay ;
No groups around the grindstones, no bleating
from the shed—
No nothing since the shearers went away.
When darkness hides the brown and dusty sun-
cracked plain from sight,
And silent buildings sleep ’neath silent stars,
We seldom care to wander near the gloomy huts,
for night
Brings memories whose presence somehow jars ;
The shades of last week’s revels grin and leer
from every crack,
And gambol ’mongst the bunks in ghostly play ;
Our hearts beat fast, and faster beat our foot-falls
up the track—
We’re nervous since the shearers went away.
They left us prematurely when but half the clip
was through,
In troublous times a year or two ago ;
They struck work in a body, like a mutineering
crew,
One evening as the sun was getting low.
They said they had their orders “ fiom above ” to
down tbeir tools,
And orders they intended to obey ;
We tried how to persuade them they were some-
thing worse than fools —
But cheered them as they slowly rode away
At times we want to sling the place and travel
with the boys
From shed to shed, and follow up the Spring,
Escape the station silences, and share the griefs
and joys
Of those who take what luck the road may
, brin & ’
Wed leave the dusty drafting for the mortgagee
to do —
’Twould do him good to swelter for a day—
Perhaps his death-grip would relax if he experi-
enced, too,
Our feelings when the shearers go away.
JOHN DRAYMAN.
In the Settlement.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.
It was a dreary winter night. Henry Vignold
stood in the doorway of his store surveying the
scene with sombre eyes. He hated it, he hated it all.
The rain pattered heavily in on him, and gathered
in little heads on his rough, serge coat —the coat
whose cut and finish made the most unobservant
dweller in the settlement mark him out as a man
from town. The canal, a brown and muddy strip
of water, poured noisily over the drop, and the
canal hank was one long stretch of mud. The
houses near, little two-roomed weatherboard huts,
looked drearier than ever, with the light shining
through their tiny windows and through the little
chinks in their walls. He had not lit up his own
rooms, and the blackness behind him was not
cheering. “What money,” thought Vignold
“could repay a man for living in a place like this?”
The grey eyes looked at the canal longingly. It
would be so much less wearisome to be lying at
the bottom, forgetful for ever of tbe unspeakable
dreariness of life and tbe hopelessness of labor.
His thirty years of life had been pleasant enough,
yet he would not wish to have them over again.
And the years that were to come had no interest
for him. He knew what life was. There was
nothing new under the sun.
The woman who lived over the road lit her lamp
at last. He watched till she did, as he had often
watched before. The light flashed over her yel-
low hair and lit up with freshness her worn face.
The people of the settlement told strange stories
of Mrs. Bardew. Her husband was away—he
generally was away, Vignold remembered —and
Mrs. Bardew often came to the store about his
business.
Sbe was an amusing woman, and a pretty one.
Vignold was always glad to see her. Any incident
or any person that led him to forget his surround-
ings for a little while was not to be despised. He
would smoke a pipe and go in to light his own
lamp. The little flicker of the lighted match
made Mrs. Bardew aware of his presence in the
darkness.
“Aren’t you dreadfully dull?” she called out
in her sweet, high-pitched voice. “Come over
here and keep me company. I’m dull, too.”
Vignold looked over his shoulder irresolutely at
the blackness behind him. It was very dreary.
Mrs. Bardew was a most amusing woman, and if
she did not mind the proprieties, why should he ?
He was dull, he was lonely, and the woman
tempted him.
Vignold turned the key in the lock, and crossed
over the road, whistling softly, “If this be vanity
>>
So, lightly, blithely, he entered into bondage. . . .
Mrs. Bardew amused him, and spent his money,
and ordered his affairs. Fortunately for Mr.
Vignold there came a time when money was less
plentiful, and Mrs. Bardew less affectionate, and
much less amusing.
When the disreputable and drink-sodden indi-
vidual calling himself Mr. Bardew at last appeared
on the scene in the character of a virtuous and in-
dignant husband, Vignold was ordered out of ihe
bouBe .
Shortly after, the Bardews disappeared. They
did not, a, a rule, star long in one place, and
Vignold turned his whole mind to the task of re-
storing a neglected business and re-gilding a
tarnished name.
jj
-w-r • ~ . ,
Vignold stood on the canal bank, watching the
Shining water with absent eyes, and thinking of
Lizzie—Lizzie who loved him and whom he loved.
When one is happily in love there should be no
weariness and no sadness; yet Vignold looked both
tired and sad as he stood there in the sunlight,
It was just two months since he had met Eizzie.
It was two years since he had seen the last of Mrs.
Bardew. Why had he ever seen the womans
tempting, evil face. If Lizzie should come to
hear about it, what would she say? His pretty
Lizzie, who at this moment was most likely sur-
veymg her image m the glass with serious eyes,
to see if all was as Harry would like it. Lizzie
had such dainty, pretty ways, such a trim, sober
fashion of dressing.
Lizzie s father could soon be coaxed over. Ever
since the evil days of Mrs. Bardew Vignold had led
a straight, steady ife. He had given no woman a
second thought till that delicious, rainy morning
when Lizzie came his way, with skirts held deftly
from the mud, and mind much troubled about the
feathers m her hat. His waggonette proved then
a haven of refuge. How pink-cheeked and fresh
she looked, withthe ram-drops glistening on her
curling hair! How child-like and innocent her
sweet-voiced talk ! Ihe friendship begun under
the coyer of the old waggonette had lived through
ramy days and dry, had grown strong and mighty,
had blossomed mto love. What man could tell
such a shameful tale as his to the girl who loved
him ? A little girl with brown eyes and pink
cheeks and a smile like the sunshine. A little
lass as innocent as the day. What would she say
when she found her hero was hut a man as other
men—nay, even worse ? It was no use to hide it.
Such stories always come to light some day. If
he sold out and, with reckless hardihood, began
life anew in some distant place, still that story
would find him out. No, he would tell Lizzie,
and have it over. She would be kind, he knew.
But Vignold shrank from his determination as
he thought of the wonder in those soft eyes, the
doubt, the dread, the utter bewilderment. “What-
soever a man soweth, that shall he reap.” Now
he knew what the words meant. With a half-
uttered curse at his folly he turned away. It was
nearly time for Lizzie, and with the thought of
Lizzie’s smile of welcome he cast unpleasant
thoughts behind him and hurried on to meet her.
“ Harry,” said Lizzie, half-an-hour later, as she
sat on the bank arranging some wattle-blossom,
“ Harry, our mailman has got married.”
“ Lucky man,” said Vignold, lazily.
“ Oh!” said Lizzie in a shocked voice, “Harry!
But he’s only got 13s. 6d. a week for carrying the
mail! And he hasn’t much time to earn any
more, and Harry, you know he isn’t clever. I
don’t think it’s right to get married like that I Do
you ? ”
“ Like that,” repeated Vignold, vaguely, as his
thoughts flew back to that old story—that stoi y of
®®till untold. The mailman might get only
bos. bd., but he had no secrets to hide. That is
best of not being clever. “What a little
woman you are for thinking about what’s right,
Lizzie! You re always saying it.”
asked Lizzie, wistfully,

Lizzie, contentedly. “Harry, you frightened
me t I thought you meant-dreadful things.
Like one reads about, you know. ”
And Lizzie, whose experience of the evils of
this world was very, very limited, slipped her
hand in his, in mute apology for the passing
traitorous thought. There were bad men, she
knew, who gambled, and drank, and swore, and
did—dreadful things. But that was only in hooks,
or perhaps in Melbourne, or further away than
that. Not here, where she knew everybody and
everything. Not here, where she loved everyone,
an d everyone loved her. And if Harry had been
careless (her mind flew back to a possibly irregular
attendance at church) he was a dear, good fellow,
and she was very fond of him.
A nd as Vignold, following out his own train of
thoughts in the silence, looked up and caught the
swe etness in those brown eyes, and felt the touch
0 f that tender hand, he forgot—as who would not?
_ a n e ] se but that he loved her<
So it ever was. All through that beauteous
spring-time, while the wattles blossomed and
f ade d, they met and parted, and still the story
remained untold. Vignold almost forgot it. It
seemed so long ago, as if it were not he, hut
another, who had sinned. Some poor unfortunate
who had not known how sweet were truth and
love and constancy, nor seen them mirrored in his
dear one’s eyes. Some lonely wretch whom God
had forgotten. But, as for himself, had he not
a i wa ys dwelt in Paradise securely ?
HI.
In the early summer they were married. There
was nothing to hinder. Lizzie’s father was im-
patient to be gone, and glad to put his daughter
into such good hands. Lizzie herself made no
objection. She hated the thought of Harry living
all alone in that draughty store, careless of him-
self and his health as such men are. Cups half-
washed, floors unswept, badly-cooked meals,
haunted her housewifely soul. She knew Harry
didn’t take enough care of himself. The little
rooms at the back of the store took on anew aspect
under her skilful hands ; the little garden plot was
planted afresh when Lizzie’s peaceful reign began,
The days went by swiftly and smoothly. It was
only long afterwards that Vignold looked back on
them and realised how truly blessed they had been.
Lizzie proved herself shrewd and business-like,
and he grew to ask her advice and listen to her
more and more. Yet she was just his little girl,
as soberly decided, as innocen ly narrow-minded
as when he went along the canal bank on Sunday
afternoons to meet her. Vignold did not meet her

now—he wer* to church regularly. Whatever
Lizzie’s wistiul eyes asked he could not refuse.
And Lizzie, seeing his old, careless ways leaving
him day by day, smiled contentedly and said noth-
ing. She had always known that at heart Harry
was the best of men.
They had been married nearly a year when the
storm burst. Lizzie went out ono morning to the
gate —some strange woman had beckoned her. A
rough, untidy woman, with yellow hair unbrushed
and uncombed, and dress that hung in crumpled
folds around her. So much Lizzie noticed, and
then she saw that the stranger had a handsome
face with bright, blue eyes; eyes that surveyed
her with a curiosity that she felt unwarranted.
“Are you Henry Vignold’s wife?” demanded
the stranger peremptorily.
“Yes, ’ said Lizzie, checking her footsteps at
the strangeness of the questioner’s manner.
“ Well, tell him I want to see him, will you ? ”
the woman said, not heeding Lizzie’s gentle dis-
approval.
Instead, she leaned upon the gate, surveying,
with the same air of curiosity, the trim, little
garden, with its bordered paths and the blossom-
ing fruit-trees beyond.
“ Harry ! ” called Lizzie sharply. Whatever
could the woman want ?
Vignold came to the door, strolled down the path
towards them, stopped abruptly as she herself had
done, and the three stood in silence.
“ Why, Harry,” said the woman smiling, “you
needn’t forget who I am altogether. It’s not so
very long that you’ve been a virtuous husband,
and I’ll be bound you haven’t forgotten the old
days yet,”
Lizzie stayed to hear no more. She turned
away and walked back to the house with an un-
faltering step, and shut the door noiselessly. She
sat down, weary, and put her hand to her head,
wondering in a helpless fashion what had confused
her so. The bright spring sunlight had dazzled
her eyes ; there was something in the freshness of
the morning that had taken her strength away.
And Harry, why was he talking to that woman
so long ?
It was just a minute or two after that Vignold
came in and stood in silence beside her.
“ What did she want ? ” asked Lizzie wearily.
“ She came about some business for the store,”
Vignold made answer, putting a gentle hand on
her shoulder. “Come and have your breakfast,
Lizzie. ”
“ When did you know her, Harry ? ”
“ Long ago, before you came here.”
“ What did she want I” asked Lizzie, twisting
herself away from him. “ Harry, tell me.”
“ She wanted money,” said Vignold doggedly.
“ Did you give her any ? ”
“ Yes.”
Was this his Lizzie with that white, frightened
face ? “ Why did you, then ? ” she asked. “ Oh,
Ml me, and have done.”
Vignold told her, in stumbling, broken sentences
and by slow degrees. Bit by bit that old tale
came out. And when it was finished he had no
other word to add in explanation or excuse. His
wife lay sobbing in his arms, and he could not com-
fort her. The happy light was gone from those
tender eyes, the soft pink from that child-like
face. And he could not say one word.
A customer came. Lizzie drew herself away
and tried to stay her tears, and her husband went
in to his day’s work. To-day, as on all other days,
he must dispense tea and sugar, and explain the
merits of gaudy calicoes to talkative and close-
fisted customers. He must reckon up endless six-
ponces and shillings correctly, and all the while be
thinking of that still, white face and those tired
eyes. He longed for the day to be over, that he
might—stupendous task !—charm the haggard
look from his dear one’s face and bring content-
ment back again. Yet, when he went in to tea,
the same helpless silence came upon him. He
talked mechanically about the day, and the people,
and the business he had done, but said no word of
the morning’s revelations. Lizzie shrank from
any approach to the subject. She made no com-
plaint and asked no more questions. Vignold felt
as though he could almost see the barrier that
reserve and silence were building up between
them. And still he could say no word.
The days passed and Lizzie grew paler and
thinner, but fretfully resented any notice being
taken of the change. “ It was nothing,” she said.
Oh ! the torture of those dreadful days! His
penitence, his tenderness, were all of no avail.
Lizzie endured his solicitude with apathy or else
was annoyed by it.
One day he came upon her. crying as if her
heart would break, but she hushed her tears at
his approach, and turned away. Yet she was
never unkind, but looked after his comfort and
waited on him as tirelessly as ever.
“Perhaps,” he thought with an aching heart,
“ one day she will turn to me again.” He could
do nothing but wait. All the world was changed,
and he remained the same —to endure the days as
best he might, and pray that he might for a little
while forget that other happy spring-time so long
ago. How could he find the way to her heart
again ?
IV.
A month later. Lizzie was setting the table for
tea when a knock came to the door, and she turned
to her husband with frightened eyes. She, who
had never known what nerves were, was now thus
easily startled and afraid.
Vignold rose to open it, but the door was flung
open, and the strange woman appeared on the
threshold, this time with a child in her arms—a
little boy about three years old. She was dressed
for travelling. “ Here, take the child,” she said,
placing it in Vignold"s arms. “I have enough to
look to without bothering with yours. I’ve seen
the last of this place.” And she shut the door
again, and went out as suddenly as she had come
in. They heard the sound of wheels as she was
driven away. Lizzie went on with her work with
trembling hands. “Don’t let the child cry so,
Harry,” she said.
"V ignold put the little fellow down, and walked
out bareheaded into the night. Along the road
he went, not knowing and not caring where, until
he passed some reeling tramp upon the road, when
he turned back, remembering that Lizzie was alone
and would be frightened.
The little room looked still and peaceful when
he returned. Lizzie was sitting sewing by the
lamp-light, and the child, pink-cheeked and curly-
headed, slept in an improvised bed in the corner.
“Don’t fret about it, Harry,” his wife said as
he came and stood over the child, “ It can’t be
helped now. You’ll have to get a cot fixed up in
that corner.”
He timidly touched her hand in mute gratitude,
but she hastily drew it away and rose to prepare
his supper.
How she managed so well with the child Vignold
wondered often. She was not fond of children,
and this little waif was unused to order and
obedience. He was a pretty little fellow, with
yellow curls—and grey eyes like V:ignold’s own. In
a day or two his former home seemed forgotten, and
he attached himself unreservedly to Lizzie, who
tended him with faithful, albeit somewhat puzzled,
patience. His little feet never pattered i ery far
away from her, his little tongue never tired of
telling her wondrous stories of his wonderful child-
world, his little hands clung to her with childish
love and happy child-like certainty of love re-
turned. Was the child to bring them nearer to each
other, or more hopelessly to keep them apart ? lor
a long time the struggle went on, Lizzie’s pleasure
in the child’s pretty ways and her remembrance
of his mother struggling each to be uppermost in
her mind. Vignold waited, but not now so hope-
lessly. He had a stronger partisan than he knew.
Vignold’s grev eyes looked at Lizzie from the child’s
face, filled with all the love that he himself
was afraid to show ; childish sorrow and remorse
for his little sins and misdemeanors made her
think more tenderly of her husband’s humble be-
seeching. Little by little the light came back to
her eyes again. Would they ever shine on him
with their old tenderness ?
He stood wistfully watching her, as she bent
over the cot in the corner, and pulled the bed-
clothes up closer round her little charge.
“ Harry,” said she, turning to him with that
look in her eyes he had so prayed to see, “how
like he grows to you ! ”
And she slipped her hand in his, and drew him
nearer to watch the little sleeper.
MARY ALSTON.
Memories.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The longing of the deaf for the voice of the world,
of the blind for the light, of the lover for his lost
mistress, in which soul and flesh cry out together
passionately, only to sink back in trembling bitter
silence how the wonder grows that death is
feared at all, that it is not rather life that terrifies.
It terrified us twenty years ago, do you remember f
Yet the years have gone somehow. I have not
seen your face, nor touched your hand, nor heard
your voice in all that time. On this earth we
will never meet again, and is there a hereafter ?
A vague, wide eternity, a misty, crazy haze,
where even a thought or a kiss would fall through
were it carelessly dropped. It must he more than
that (this eternity) ; it must give you to me body
as well as spirit. You, with the dimpled chin,
the wide, grave eyes. You, with your ribbons
and laces, and tiny French shoes, flashes of
temper, and impulsive repentance. Twenty years
ago, and memory is steadfast to that one sweet
year worth an eternity of commonplace happiness.
Worth all the tears and agony that came after,
worth any penance that earth or hell can devise.
Our secret has been ours alone, has out-balanced
all other joys and sorrows. We loved each other
and were parted, but the gain of our love out-
weighed even our loss.
We are growing old now, and are very lonely,
but the love is as strong as twenty years ago.
Your glove lies with some old dead roses ; your
faded picture is on my wall; for the rest I have
the memory of one year, and perhaps a little hope
for that hereafter of which you spoke so bravely.
However it be, I have loved you, have gained
your love; though the grave be final, and I never
see you more, yet I can be glad and utterly grate-
ful for that one perfect year.
There is an old log on a far-away hill, half
hidden among bushy saplings, bracken, and
kangaroo-grass. There are great white gums
with flapping strips of bark hanging from their
shining limbs ; and around the wide valley the
abrupt, encroaching mountains. Here and there
brown patches of ploughed land, flights of cocka-
toos and parrots, and the warm spring sun finding
the reds and golds in the young transparent pepper-
mint-leaves ; the brilliant blue of the distant hills
the hazy mist by the river where the wattles
hang over the clea-, rapid water. Do you re-
member it ?
There is a winding mountain road, where the
rough track is bordered by high trees and flower-
ing hazel, and twigs and dead leaves crackle under
the horses’ hoofs. Where, here and there, shows
a clearing, a bark hut, a raw new fence, and a
wilderness of gaunt, ringed trees. Jays clatter
overhead, blue-wren 3 flit about among the under-
growths. There are sudden dips and turns in the
track, steep places where the horses slip and
scramble and frightened rabbits scurry away into
the friendly cover of ferns and sword-grass. All
around are the mountains, far away in the East
there lies snow on the taller ones, and a rosy glow
is reflected as the sun sets in the West. Do you
remember ? The night is passing, the stars are
growung dim in the grey sky ; the years are pass-
sing also, and have been lived for you and through
you. There have been dreadful moments of realisa-
tion when the hopelessness became physical pain
when my empty firms stretched out into the dark-
ness for you, and through each smallest vein
tingled the longing which burnt in my heart and
eyes and lips. But I have loved you, and to have
loved you is pure gain. And maybe there is a
hereafter ; come what may, we have had one per-
fect year. And so, good-bye !

Maoriland was much shaken up of late by
a sensational vitriol-throwing case. One man had
cut another out in the affections of his best girl,
whereupon the loser of the girl went a long way to
call on the other man —or, at least, he went a long
way and they’ met—and, w hile they were together,
a quantity of sulphuric acid flew over the success-
ful rival, being, it was commonly alleged, thrown
at him by his infuriated adversary. Two juries
and a Judge wrestled with the case at great length.
The Judge directed the second jury to consider,
among other things, whether the vitriol was
thrown “ with intent to do any bodily harm to
the complainant or to disfigure him,” which
opened up the great possibility that it might have
been thrown out of benevolence, or by way of
congratulation upon his marriage, or something of
the kind. Then the jury went one better by ex-
pressing a doubt as,to whether it was thrown in-
tentionally or not, sulphuric acid being a thing
which any man might have about him by acci-
dent, and which might uncork itself and rush out
of his pocket entirely “bn its own.” Finally, the
verdict was ‘‘not guilty.” The verdict may have
been right enough, but as much can't be said of a
Judge who wants to consider whether vitriol
might not have been thrown with the best of
motives, and a jury which thinks it may haveb en
thrown accidentally or may have thrown itself.
